rear window's rear view - REVIEW

Newspaper December 27, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Joe Aston Section: Weekend Fin 2282 Words
Page: 47
OpenURL Link
Poking through the embers of 2014 reveals a slowburning year heavy on chatter and light on action, writes Joe Aston.

In 1994, blockbuster action flick, Speed, hit cinemas. Twenty years on, writing Rear Window is just like driving that bomb-rigged Los Angeles city bus. If your pace falters even ­momentarily, the whole thing blows sky high. Full tilt is the only hope of success and/or survival, and ­assistance is rendered by superiors whose sirens shepherd us through dicey ­intersections and hazardous obstacles.

Unfortunately I'm no Keanu Reeves , my sidekick Will Glasgow looks nothing like Sandra Bullock and we haven't grossed $350 million at the box office, so that's where this analogy ends.

Summing up a calendar year becomes more difficult the longer you do it. With a few exceptions, they all start to resemble each other. But 2014 was a kind of holding year, remarkable more for the amount of talk than the amount of action.

All of this calendar's great totems were slow-burn affairs – Network Ten's journey to market, the ABC's fallout with the Abbott government, Vocation's fallout with ­investors, and Daily Telegraph editor Paul Whittaker's fallout with reality. Gone are the days when flaming out took just a few days! Except perhaps in the instance of ­billionaire James Packer and Nine boss David Gyngell duking it out in Bondi, but even that fuse had been burning a while. And Ian Thorpe coming out. Knock me down with a feather. My first editor remarked to me at a cocktail party 10 years ago: "Ian is the Rock Hudson of this generation." Glad I remembered that one . . .

As always, faces changed, but not with the morbid enthusiasm of the previous year. Cameron Clyne vacated the helm of National Australia Bank and Gail Kelly is preparing to do the same at Westpac. Lazard investment banker John Wylie retired too. Leighton's Hamish Tyrwhitt and Peter Gregg were executed by those rampaging Spaniards in March, but made off with the loot. Virgin Australia chair Neil Chatfield stepped down and is still to be replaced, while both AMP and David Jones have been relieved of Peter Mason 's services as chairman. Ron Radford departed the National Gallery and in March Andrew Demetriou announced he was leaving the AFL, but not before a parting gift for his foes at News Corp Australia: in February, as we'd predicted, their former boss Kim Williams was appointed an AFL Commissioner.

In Canberra, there was not a single leadership challenge! Despite feral commentary and eye-watering backstabbing at the end of the year, federal politics was remarkable mostly by virtue of its stability. Well, everything's relative, and Rudd-Gillard-Rudd left a hell of a low bar.

Yes, the budget sell job was messy. And Tony Abbott wasted valuable political ­capital on extraneous policy – such as a war on the ABC and reform of the Racial Discrimination Act – to placate a few conservative commentators who already supported him. That didn't win the PM a single vote and ended with Alan Jones , Andrew Bolt and The Australian newspaper birching him anyway. Julia Gillard made the same ­mistake playing to feminazis and mummy bloggers. There are few swinging voters on the ideological periphery of politics and the centre has no interest in culture wars.

The path to redemption for the Coalition is in crafting its every message through the prism of economic security, then lather, rinse and repeat. Which is precisely what the PM's reshuffle seems designed to enable, with talk of welfare policy as "economic ­participation" and the efficacious Josh Frydenberg installed as Assistant ­Treasurer. Arthur Sinodinos may be in exile for a while yet but his counsel on strategy should be kept regularly at the highest level. And the nagging diplomacy in the Senate, whose most skilful practitioner is Mathias Cormann , must continue.

Nevertheless, disunity is death: it's a ­cliché because it's true. The visceral feeding frenzies on Abbott's chief of staff Peta ­Credlin and Treasurer Joe Hockey are hopefully done. The PM's Office merely serves the PM and he is now listening. So his frontbench has no excuse not to stem its panic (or, in some cases, treachery) and get on with that mantra: economic security.

In this feature last year, we predicted Ten Network's disastrous foray into breakfast television would be over by Easter. It was axed in May. We floated Alexander Downer going to Washington as our ambassador. Instead, like his father, he was posted to London. For that, he can blame his relationship with Chinese telco Huawei – not a favourite of US intelligence agencies. We suggested Google Australia's foray into ­creating its own content. It didn't happen in 2014 but it will in 2015.

Despite the stubborn rumour, UBS ­rockstar banker Matthew Grounds went nowhere, nor did Crown Resorts CEO Rowen Craigie (although he does report to a new co-chairman: Grounds's former UBS colleague Rob Rankin). And Qantas boss Alan Joyce had four-fifths of a horror year, force-feeding the airline some ugly ­medicine, yet by November he was announcing an upgrade to profit guidance and blue skies ahead. Clearly, Canberra was right to make him sink or swim, refusing him a debt ­guarantee. But his success has given his ­tenure plenty of breathing space. Following this month's management shake-up, Gareth Evans looks like the internal successor, moving from CFO to run Qantas's ­international and freight divisions and beefing up his operational CV.

While the Australian economy hardly roared along, investment bankers had their best year in a long while: $41.4 billion of equity was raised on the ASX, up 62 per cent, including $17.8 million in initial ­public offerings.

The biggest bonanzas for our Masters of the Universe were the Medibank Private and Healthscope floats, the trade sale of ­Queensland Motorways, Shell's $3.2 billion block sale in Woodside and the restructure of the Westfield empire. The Lowys alone paid $70 million in fees to the investment banks. Bonus time!

But, as always, there were a few ­headaches. UBS was burned badly ­underwriting Arrium's undersubscribed $754 million capital raising. And Citi wore fried egg after an aborted attempt at a block sale in Veda for major shareholder PEP.

JPMorgan is lagging Down Under and is under considerable pressure from New York. Watch this space.

Caliburn founders Ron Malek and Simon Mordant (or what's left of him!) are free to compete with their former masters Greenhill early in the new year. Nobody will be surprised to see them hang out a new shingle. It will also be interesting to see what star broker Charlie Aitken does in 2015, having joined Bell Financial's board this year only to leave it soon after.

Gordon Cairns joined the Macquarie board on October 31. Presumably, he's Kevin McCann 's preferred successor as chairman – the two men share a healthy respect and the same happened at Origin Energy. Nothing's imminent, but it would have implications for who is to succeed Nicholas Moore as chief Millionaire.

Mercifully, things settled down in our industry – at least compared with 2013, a year of seismic profit collapse and CEO changes at Ten, Seven and News Limited.

In the publishing industry there are still polar views on print decline and digital ­sustainability. Print whisperers point ­stoically to the stubborn premium on print ad rates but fail to account for the higher costs of printing and distribution. One side will be proven triumphantly right and the other catastrophically wrong. Prayer time?

Rupert Murdoch spent a week in Sydney in December, meeting with senior management and editors. There is every chance that Peter Tonagh will soon be named to ­succeed Julian Clarke as Australian CEO, although APN News & Media's Michael Miller is a wildcard. Ten boss Hamish McLennan , tied up with the network's sale process, has long coveted the top Holt Street job (as has The Australian's editor in chief Chris Mitchell ). After the hospital pass that was Ten, McLennan may return to ad ­buying. Hey, it worked out well for Harold Mitchell and McLennan is still well regarded by industry leaders such as Seven's Kerry Stokes .

What does the future hold at News for the Tele's Paul Whittaker? His brazen ­front-page advertorials would sit more comfortably on daytime television (NutriBullet, anyone?). There's a Grand Canyon between being commercially savvy and selling out.

The problem for Boris? He doesn't have Mitchell's grasp of the subtleties of politics, his tabloid instincts are profoundly inferior to Col Allan's (or indeed The Courier-Mail's Chris Dore), he lacks the charisma of John Hartigan , the larrikin editorial humour of David Penberthy has utterly bypassed him and, to boot, he's a total punish to be around. Apart from that, he's a wonderful embellishment to Murdoch's empire!

How much fire is left in the belly of Nine's David Gyngell ? Clearly, he'd rather be chairman than CEO, and pull the strings from Byron Bay, but who'd be mad enough to take the job under him?!  Anyhow, Gyng will have to stay another two years if he's to make off with his full post-float incentives and a big deal on regional television is still in his, and the board's, sights.

As much as Fairfax's Roger Corbett and Greg Hywood would love to pull off a deal with Nine, it won't be countenanced by the network while we're still printing ­newspapers, and that's a while yet.

What Hywood did pull off, on the stroke of Christmas, was a merger with John ­Singleton's Macquarie Radio Network. ­Analyst consensus had valued the entire Fairfax radio division at $110 million. But Fairfax made off with $78 million for one Perth FM station, another $18 million in cash and 54.5 per cent of a merged national talkback powerhouse worth an estimated $200 million. Worth a Maserati or three?

By the way, we never meant any of that mean stuff we said about 2GB stars Alan Jones and Ray Hadley – they're both stand-up fellows and it's about time we focused on the positives!

2GB and Fairfax is a textbook odd couple and everyone will be treading very carefully while the dust settles on this new bloc. But how incredible that Singo, Jones and Hadley – for so long in political and commercial lockstep with the Telegraph – are now in business with News's archenemy Fairfax. Team Singo insists none of this changes their love and affection for Holt Street. But how long will News buy that line?

Our real media bugbear for the year was book publishing. The market was swamped in 2014 by turgid memoirs. Pity the fortunes of those trying to market quality fiction. We especially tip our cap to Melbourne ­University Publishing's Louise Adler , who treated us to yet another publicity tour by look-at-me TV producer Adam Boland and a list of Kim Williams ' favourite things.

Pity the trees who died for that! MUP made a whole $93,163 in calendar 2013, down 85 per cent on the previous year, but we'll have to wait until April to see their accounts for 2014.

The precipitous Q4 fall in the oil and iron ore prices spelled disaster for ­mining and energy stocks. Since ­September, Santos has more than halved in value. Origin, Karoon Gas and Beach Energy have all been hammered, as have Fortescue, BHP, Atlas, BC Iron and Arrium. Yep, the resources boom is toast. Compounding their pain, BHP totally botched the messaging of its demerger plans for non-core assets, playfully dubbed CrapCo but now known as South32.

It was a very bad year for some. Barry O'Farrell ? Say no more . . .

Vocation CEO Mark Hutchinson arguably misled the market in August, denying a funding suspension which had been in effect since July. It subsequently tapped investors for $74 million in new equity in September. Chairman John Dawkins is gone. The CFO is gone. So is 90 per cent of the company's value and any faith from investors. Two shareholder class actions are in train. And Vocation has issued three profit downgrades in the last two months (the last snuck out on Christmas Eve). Yet, inexplicably, Hutchinson remains.

David Jones chairman Peter Mason tried to throw fellow directors Leigh Clapham and Steve Vamos under the bus for share trading that he approved. The nerve! All three fell on their swords and Mason also copped grief for the manner in which he disclosed a merger approach from rival Myer.

Myer's Bernie Brookes was leaving, then staying. With DJs snatched from him by the South Africans, and his ­margins being pummelled by the ­international retail invasion, Dural's second favourite son (after The People's Champion Ray Hadley) finds himself in a strategic cul de sac.

Woolworths boss Grant O'Brien goes into 2015 under huge pressure, with his Masters hardware division bleeding cash and in similar shape to the federal budget. Can he bounce back?

Divisive Murray Goulburn chief Gary Helou copped an adverse finding from the ad watchdog for running a ­racist TV commercial depicting ­bungling Japanese dairy executives. Gary The Great also has quite a few peeved farmers after his deal with Coles locked in $1 milk until some time after the Tehran Olympic Games.

Treasury Wine Estates succeeded in boganising its most prestigious brand, Penfolds, with heavy discounting and free wine fridges.

And the big banking lobby, chaired by Gail Kelly , copped a s--t sandwich from David Murray 's financial ­systems inquiry while asleep on duty.

In a perfect world, 2015 would begin with a stabilisation in commodities and a February interest rate cut, then a rash of big infrastructure deals led by Mike Baird 's poles and wires, and a functioning federal Senate. Wesfarmers makes a big acquisition (AMP?) as Richard Goyder 's swansong before succeeding Mike Fitzpatrick as AFL chairman. BHP spins off South32 and Glencore comes back for a second tilt at Rio Tinto. Bankers get rich and I go home happy. And pigs are seen flying over the Paul Whittaker International Airport at Badgerys Creek.

Happy holidays. Our editor in chief Michael Stutchbury will be researching his history of the Productivity ­Commission and writing folk songs about Glenn Stevens (regardless of the rate cut). The rest of us will watch some cricket and gorge ourselves on Neil Perry 's Christmas pudding. Enjoy.

Treasury Wine mulls US acquisition

Newspaper December 17, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Yolanda Redrup Section: Companies and Markets 650 Words
Page: 18
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates is considering acquiring a United States business to give it access to a sustainable supply of quality grapes to try and bolster a revival, as part of the company's long term growth strategy.

Chief executive Michael Clarke said it had a list of companies it was considering acquiring which would give it access to the best grapes.

"If we could get access to more luxury and masstige, i.e. top end grapes, that would allow us to even further step-change our growth rate in the luxury masstige part of the portfolio in North America and outside of North America," he said.

"The spot market works very well with commercial wine . . . but to get a regular supply of luxury masstige grapes on the spot market is very difficult . . . everyone is looking to get access to those grapes,'' he said after the Treasury annual meeting in Melbourne on Tuesday. Masstige is an industry term for a brand which sits between prestige and mass market.

Mr Clarke said he was not interested in taking on any new brands, he only wanted access to the grapes.

"I'm not looking to buy a business that gives us brands, I'm not looking to buy a business that gives us infrastructure – we have that," he said.

He also signalled that Treasury was close to finalising some changes in the lower end of the troublesome United States business, where it would slim down its commercial brand portfolio.

"We are exploring some imminent opportunities to address the non-priority commercial brand portfolio," he said. This segment in the US represents between one to two million cases of lower-priced wines for Treasury, which he described as a "significant drag on our core portfolio''.

He would not elaborate on which brands were on the chopping block, but said they would be wines under the $US3 ($3.70) price point.

Mr Clarke also outlined that of Treasury's 80-plus brands, about 15 have the potential to be what he calls "global umbrella brands" capable of being solid performers on the world stage.

Mr Clarke, a former chief executive of British food group Premier Foods, was appointed to the top job on March 31, and has largely been applauded by shareholders for the changes he has made. The wine company's financial performance for the first half of 2014-15 is "on plan and ahead of prior year'', Mr Clarke told shareholders, as his fix-it plans and reinvestment in the business continue to bite.

Mr Clarke said the solid progress in the first five-and-a-half months of the first half had been driven by the switch of the release of high-end Penfolds wines to October from the previous May 1 date, and reinvestment behind the major wine brands.

Mr Clarke said he was "very pleased" by the release date switch.

"By moving it to October it allows us to capture those gifting occasions . . . and it's definitely happening around the world," he said. "In prior years we would release Penfolds in March and May which would result in them trying to sell as much Penfolds as they could in the last quarter of the financial year. That I felt was damaging the brand."

Treasury chairman Paul Rayner told shareholders he believed the board had done the right thing in terminating talks with private equity bidders earlier this year, with "clear feedback" from almost every major shareholder that a price of $5.20 per share undervalued the company.

"I am confident that [what] we did was right at every step of the process,'' Mr Rayner said.

Treasury Wine's annual meeting is usually held in late October but was rescheduled months ago on the expectation it would coincide with a shareholder vote on one of the two private equity takeover proposals the company had received.

But the Treasury board on September 29 announced that it had ended talks with private equity suitors about a proposed $3.4 billion buy out after months of due diligence.

Treasury shares closed down 4.56 per cent at $4.40 on Tuesday.

Dreaming of a red Christmas

Australia's most iconic premium wine, Penfolds Grange, has been off limits as an ideal Christmas gift. The only time wine buffs could be showered with the famed Barossa shiraz is if the gift giver got ridiculously organised and snapped up a bottle when the winemaker traditionally released its iconic wines – in May.

This year marks a first for Penfolds. It ditched next May's release for its 2010 Grange and instead released the top drop on October 16. The decision was aimed at capturing Christmas sales and gift giving at Chinese New Year in February while smoothing out earnings for parent company Treasury Wine Estates.

It means this is the first Christmas you are able to waltz in to a fine-wine retailer and grab a bottle from the shelf.

The wine recently ranked second in James Suckling's top 100 wines list, beaten at the post by Argentina's 2011 Vina Cobos Malbec.

The 2010 Grange has so far collected three 100-point scores, from Tyson Stelzer, Andrew Caillard and Nick Stock, and is viewed by most critics to be superior to the 2009 Grange released earlier this year for the same recommended retails price ($785 a bottle).

James Halliday gave the 2010 Grange 99 points and said: "There is not the slightest question this will be one of the greatest Granges in the pantheon of '52, '55, '71, '96 and '06." With some astute buying, it can be found for about $650 a bottle.

Julie-anne Sprague

HEAD OVER HEELS

You will definitely be putting your best foot forward with these intricate sandals in calf skin from luxury maison Hermès. $1495.

Tel: (02) 9287 3200

Burge bemoans Aussie as sale decision looms

Newspaper November 18, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Michael Bleby Section: Companies and Markets 532 Words
Page: 20
OpenURL Link
Grant Burge, the owner of Grant Burge Wines, which is in serious talks with Accolade Wines about a potential $50 million-plus sale of the fifth-generation Barossa Valley wine business, says the high Australian dollar has crimped profit margins.

Mr Burge, who owns the company with wife Helen, said on Monday negotiations were delicately poised and the sale "could go either way".

The company generates annual sales of $45 million.

Accolade is 80 per cent owned by private equity firm CHAMP, with United States-based alcoholic beverages giant Constellation Brands holding the remaining 20 per cent.

Accolade, which is the renamed former BRL Hardy business, with brands including Hardys, Leasingham and Houghton, is involved in exclusive negotiations with Grant Burge Wines about acquiring the business.

Mr Burge said the high Australian dollar had been hurting profit margins, although the company hadn't been hit as hard as some rivals because Grant Burge Wines has a large domestic focus. The company sells 450,000 cases of wine in the domestic market, and exports 150,000 cases.

But the strong currency had been an overall negative.

"It hasn't helped, for us it has certainly affected our margins in Europe," he said. He declined to comment on a potential price tag.

"It's not finalised so I'd rather not comment on that."

If a deal is struck, it would mark the second large transaction among big Barossa Valley winery companies in the past week.

Peter Lehmann Wines last week agreed to a $57 million buyout by Griffith-based Casella Wines, maker of the big-selling Yellow Tail brand.

Accolade said in a statement on Monday it was "in exclusive discussions to acquire the award-winning Barossa-based Grant Burge Wines".

"There is no guarantee that a final agreement will be reached as a result of the discussions," Accolade said.

Grant Burge Wines is a fifth-generation Barossa winemaking entity, with a history dating back to 1855.

The Burges took over running the business in 1988. They also own 400 hectares of vineyards.

Grant Burge Wines sells its flagship Meshach shiraz for about $180 a bottle.

The latest vintage is from the 2009 grape harvest and the company entered the top end of the market with that label almost two decades ago in an attempt to match it with other high-end premium wines, such as Penfolds Grange, owned by Treasury Wine Estates, and Hill of Grace made by the family-owned ­Henschke Wines.

The well-known Barossa region marks a gap in Accolade's offering.

The company has been without a Barossa brand since 2011, when it pulled out of the Barossa Valley Estate joint venture in which it had a minority stake. Barossa Valley Estate was ­last year bought by New Zealand-based Delgats.

The Australian Financial Review understands the companies hope to reach a decision either way before Christmas.

The latest set of accounts lodged by Grant Burge Wines with the corporate regulator covers the 2012-13 year and shows net profit was $264,247 from sales revenue of $43.2 million.

This compared with 2011-12, when net profit was $679,099, from sales revenue of $43.6 million.

Constellation acquired ASX-listed BRL Hardy for $1.9 billion in 2003 and privatised it, before selling 80 per cent to CHAMP in 2011 following a slump in profits.

Key points Winemaker says high dollar has hurt profit margins, especially in Europe. Sale deal to Accolade Wines 'could go either way', likely decision before Christmas.

Treasury axes Ryecroft winery

Newspaper November 11, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 445 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates is looking closely at other potential closures of smaller wineries to cut costs after announcing on Monday it will shut the 120-year-old Ryecroft winery in South Australia.

The Ryecroft winery, in the McLaren Vale region, is running at about half of its capacity and produces a range of Rosemount labels.

The decision to shut the production facility marks the first major step in an overhaul of the company's processing infrastructure as Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke steps up his plans to strip more costs out of the supply chain. He wants to divert funds into investment in the company's major brands.

The shutdown of Ryecroft, which has 33 employees, means Treasury will be left with 10 wineries around ­Australia.

Most of the production of the Rosemount labels currently made at ­Ryecroft will be transferred to the Wolf Blass winery in the Barossa Valley, which is about 140 kilometres away on the opposite side of Adelaide.

Treasury will transfer production from the Ryecroft facility in time for the 2015 grape harvest.

Company executives on Monday informed the 33 staff at the winery that it will be shutting, and meetings were held with unions.

Redundancies will be offered to those who can't find a job at other Treasury facilities.

Three very large processing sites operated by Treasury are the Wolf Blass winery, Penfolds, which is also in the Barossa Valley, and Karadoc in regional Victoria.

Treasury's director of wine production in Australasia, Peter Taylor, said the decision was ''tough but necessary''.

"The decision to stop operating Ryecroft winery was an extremely difficult one,'' he said. The full closure of the ­Ryecroft winery was likely to take several months to be finalised.

Mr Taylor said the production economics were not sustainable. "Rosemount wines previously made at Ryecroft will continue to source their fruit from McLaren Vale and surrounding regions, the only change is the location where the wine will be made.''

Mr Clarke joined Treasury as chief executive on March 31 and has had a torrid seven months. It included takeover proposals by two private equity firms, Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and TPG. But the Treasury board terminated takeover talks on September 29 after it became clear the bidders weren't willing to pay a high enough price for the company, which also makes brands including Penfolds, Seppelt, Wolf Blass, Lindemans and Saltram.

Under Mr Clarke's overhaul strategy, there has already been a super-charging of marketing investment in Penfolds, the most profitable brand in the portfolio, and cost cutting in back office positions with 175 roles scrapped several months ago.

Ryecroft, which was bought by the Oatley family's Rosemount business in 1991, became part of Treasury through a series of takeovers.

Coles can raise its game, says Diageo head

Newspaper November 10, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 629 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
The managing director of Diageo Australia, which sells brands including Johnnie Walker, Smirnoff and Bundaberg Rum, believes Coles will eventually be able to reinvigorate its liquor retail business to be a much stronger competitor against rival Woolworths.

Tim Salt, who runs the largest spirits maker in Australia, said that while Woolworths was currently the stand-out performer when it comes to liquor retailing, he expects Coles will be able to catch up in a similar way to its dramatic improvement in supermarkets.

"I wouldn't back against them, given what they've done in supermarkets,'' Mr Salt said.

Coles has outperformed Woolworths in supermarkets for a record 21 consecutive quarters, but in liquor retailing is a long way behind, as Woolworths powers ahead led by its flagship Dan Murphy's superstore chain.

The underperformance of the Coles liquor business has been a source of frustration for Coles's owner, Wesfarmers, for several years, considering the supermarkets business has made such strong advances since Coles Group was acquired in a $19 billion takeover in 2007.

Diageo Australia makes about 75 per cent of its total sales to liquor retailers, mostly to both Woolworths and Coles. The rest goes to bars, pubs and other licensed venues.

He said if Coles and other rivals to Woolworths such as the smaller independent chains also improved their performance, it would be a plus for suppliers across the board.

"It raises the game,'' he said.

Mr Salt said Diageo had found the going tough in Australia over the past year, with consumer confidence still soft and volumes lower, even though there is a trend of "trading up'', where drinkers buy more premium brands. But that isn't enough to offset the lower volumes.

"People are drinking less but they are drinking better.''

He added Diageo Australia's sales revenue was down by 2 per cent in 2013-14 from the previous year's figure of $542 million. Net profit after tax was also marginally lower than the 2012-13 figure of $43 million.

However, consumers are still cautious. "We're not seeing a pick-up.''

The widely held view that spirits is a category which is immune from economic softness is just not right.

"The answer is, it's not true,'' Mr Salt said.

The company's ready-to-drink alcohol brands in pre-mixed spirits are suffering from an onerous taxation regime which is hurting growth.

"You're seeing continued erosion of the RTD segment.''

The first few months of trading in 2014-15 across the Diageo Australia business had been patchy and Mr Salt said it was too difficult to make a profit forecast for the full year.

"It's holding. We are where we want to be,'' he said.

Diageo Australia is preparing to launch a new product called Smirnoff frozen cocktail pouches this month, which is a 250 millilitre soft-pack selling for $5 each, in a pack of three.

The company is also gearing up for a traditional spike in sales over the next few weeks through Christmas gift packs of prestige spirits containing extra promotional specials such as two glasses.

Mr Salt said Diageo Australia, which is part of the global Diageo Plc conglomerate based in Britain, won't be altering its tactics in the pre-Christmas lead-up because of a change in strategy by Australia's largest wine group, Treasury Wine Estates.

Treasury, led by chief executive Mike Clarke, has aggressively overhauled its own approach by switching the release of its flagship Penfolds Grange to mid-October each year, ditching three decades of the previous timing of May 1.

Mr Clarke's rationale is in part built upon the premise that Grange and other high-end Penfolds red wines will capture a large share of the pre-Christmas luxury goods gift-giving market.

Mr Salt said Grange was a "wonderful product'' but is a small, niche player and Diageo would be focusing on its own business.

Key points

Trading has been tough this year as consumers go for quality over quantity.

Ready-to-drink products hit hard by tax regime but company pins hopes on Christmas gift market.

TWE offers 'fair terms for all' - Retail Mike Clarke hits out at disgruntled independents

Newspaper November 3, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague Section: Companies and Markets 510 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke has lashed disgruntled independent retailers upset about recent deals the nation's biggest ­winemaker has cut with Woolworths.

In a stern defence of the winemaker's strategy, Mr Clarke said the company was offering fair terms to all of its retail customers and, in a sign that his ­turnaround plan was beginning to gain traction, trading so far this financial year had been strong.

Some independent retailers are ­furious with Treasury for allowing Woolworths – the nation's biggest ­liquor retailer – to participate in a wine fridge promotion in July, which they blame for stealing customers. They are also angry at a recent decision by ­Treasury, which makes Penfolds, Wolf Blass and Rosemount, to craft an ­exclusive label for Woolworths under its popular Pepperjack brand.

Woolworths' big box chain Dan Murphy's has sold the new Pepperjack shiraz cabernet for as little as $17.90 compared with the mid to high $20 mark independent retailers sell ­Treasury's Pepperjack shiraz label. "They are looking for easy volume in the chains," said one independent retailer.

Mr Clarke hit back at the criticism, saying independent retailers received more exclusive labels from Treasury than the major chains. "I think sometimes they [independents] get upset when they see someone activate an exclusive better," Mr Clarke said. "Good on Woolworths if they have taken an exclusive and made it a good deal."'Principled' stance

Mr Clarke, who joined Treasury in March and has spent much of the year defending takeover bids, said the ­winemaker was being fair to all its customers. "I can assure you we have been principled in all our dealings with our retail partners, which I think is a new thing for this company," Mr Clarke said.

Mr Clarke has embarked on an advertising blitz as he ramps up the winemaker's marketing effort to drive sales. It's his strategy that has gained the backing of shareholders, he said.

Mr Clarke said a number of shareholders initially supported the board engaging with private equity bidders for a $3.4 billion bid but changed their minds after backing his turnaround plan. Treasury Australia and New ­Zealand general manager Angus McPherson said Treasury's sales this fiscal year were "on budget" but declined to provide specific details.Sector's healthy growth

Aztec figures show the value of the Australian wine sector grow 2.9 per cent in the year to August 3, but the value of Treasury's sales fell 3.2 per cent. Wine volumes nationally slipped 1.5 per cent but Treasury's plunged a staggering 9.2 per cent.

These figures take in to account a disastrous fiscal 2013 for Treasury, which lost sales after pulling back on promotions leading in to Christmas last year.

In the past, Treasury's wine fridge promotion has excluded Woolworths.

Mr McPherson said Woolworths ­initially declined to participate in the promotion because it wanted the deal exclusively, as did other retailers.

Treasury refused to give exclusive terms to anyone. Mr McPherson said Woolworths later joined the promotion. A number of independent retailers said they did not sell as much wine as they had anticipated after Woolworths joined the fridge promotion.

Treasury is releasing new exclusive Pepperjack labels with on-premise chains this week.

Treasury's premium clear out of Penfolds Grange

Newspaper October 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague Section: Companies and Markets 655 Words
Page: 23
OpenURL Link
Veteran liquor retailers are being offered steep discounts on Penfolds Grange for the first time in 35 years in what they say is a move by Treasury Wine Estates to clear out excess stock of Australia's iconic premium wine.

The Australian Financial Review spoke to numerous retailers across the country who confirmed they had been offered discounts of between 10 per cent and 30 per cent in the past few weeks to help move excess stock of the winemaker's 2009 Penfolds Grange.

"It's a cash discount," said one of the country's largest independent retailers, who asked to remain anonymous.

Asked if the retailer, who has been in the industry for 35 years, has ever been given a discount on Penfolds' flagship Grange product he said: "Never".

Treasury Wine Estates managing director Mike Clarke strongly rejected that the winemaker was not offering cash discounts for its 2009 Grange. But he said the winemaker had increased marketing and advertising support to retailers.

"I can assure you there has been no discounting of Grange at all," Mr Clarke told The Australian Financial Review.

"We have provided additional marketing support to help sell through the May release of our Penfolds icon and luxury wines. This is not pricing support and allegations of Treasury offering 10 per cent and 20 per cent discount on the 2009 Grange are utterly false."

The increased marketing spending is to help retailers adjust to a decision by Treasury to bring forward by seven months the release of its 2010 Grange, which is viewed by critics as better than its 2009 stablemate. Rather than release Grange and its Penfolds Bin range in May next year, Treasury released the wines on October 16, allowing the company to better smooth out earnings and attract consumers during the festive season and Chinese New Year in February.

But it meant retailers had to adjust to two releases of Grange in the one year. Penfolds is crucial to Treasury's performance, accounting for 70 per cent of profits. Advertising and marketing incentives typically cover adverts and in-store displays and merchandise.Documents obtained by this newspaper show a senior Treasury Wine Estates executive has offered one retailer a 30 per cent "rebate" in the past fortnight for bottles of 2009 Grange.

The retailer was charged $580 a bottle. But after paying for the bottle, the retailer receives a 30 per cent "rebate", which he said was a cash deposit in to his bank account that meant the average wholesale price he was paying for 2009 Grange was now $406 a bottle.

Including GST, the cost price for Grange is typically $638 a bottle. Penfolds recommends the wine to retail for $785 a bottle. Woolworths' Dan Murphy's is selling Grange for $679 a bottle. But some retailers said the wine had been sold for as low as $500 a bottle in recent weeks.

One retailer, offered a similar deal, said the company structured the discount as a "rebate" in order to keep invoiced prices for Grange consistent around the country."The discount will never appear on the invoice," the retailer said. "They protect the invoice price because if I get taken over by Woolworths, Woolworths looks at my accounts and goes back to Treasury and wants a 30 per cent discount for everything they have bought. So I send them a bill. It's a convoluted way of hiding a discount." The Financial Review spoke to retailers in Sydney, Melbourne, Perth and Adelaide. "In the last 25 years that I have been doing this is the first time they have offered money on Grange," one retailer said. The retailer said he was being offered "bonus stock" and money for marketing and promotion."They can call it whatever they like. I call it a discount."Mr Clarke said most of the 2009 Grange and Bin wines released in May had been sold. But he said the winemaker was no longer trying to sell its entire production in the one year, instead holding on to stock that it could sell more profitably in future years. "We are not trying to sell everything in one year; even the current release," Mr Clarke said. "

Grange fatigue? Twice in six months

Newspaper October 11, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: News 468 Words
Page: 3
OpenURL Link
Luxury If you liked the 2009 in May, you'll be happy to buy the 2010 now. Or will you?

Grange fatigue is a rare affliction among affluent wine buyers. But there are some in the fine wine retailing trade who are warning it may become more prevalent.

The latest version of Penfolds Grange, Australia's most famous wine, is set to go on sale next Thursday after a controversial shift in strategy by the brand's owner, Treasury Wine Estates, and its relatively new chief executive, Mike Clarke, who has just fended off a $3.4 billion takeover proposal.

It is the second version of Penfolds Grange to go on sale in the past 5½ months. The one coming out of the blocks on October 16 will be the 2010 version, which is highly regarded and from a grape harvest lauded for its quality. The recommended retail price is $785 a bottle, although the real price tends to be whatever is set by Woolworths' superstore chain Dan Murphy's thanks to its market power.

The sale will be close on the heels of the 2009 vintage, which was released on May 1 at the same price. Most experts regard it as slightly inferior when it comes to quality because of the poorer 2009 grape harvest.

Jules Gutierrez, the fine wine manager at North Sydney Cellars, says the prospect of Grange buyers feeling they've had enough is high. "My feeling is there will be fatigue,'' he says, adding that sales should still be brisk.

Gutierrez says other stablemates at the luxury end of the Penfolds portfolio priced well below Grange are also being aggressively marketed but for lower prices, and many drinkers believe they offer better value.

He also points to instances across the industry over the past few weeks where the 2009 Penfolds Grange was selling for $500 a bottle as stocks were cleared out in readiness for the 2010 version.

Two Granges in less than six months is a one-off as Treasury Wine, which also owns Wolf Blass, Rosemount and Seppelt, overturns its long-held strategy of releasing Grange on May 1 each year.

Clarke outlined the change months ago as part of a drive to ensure Grange is under consideration for Christmas stockings. Treasury hopes it might replace expensive bottles of whisky and other tipples as cashed-up buyers hunt for something special.

Chris O'Brien, the general manager of Liquor Barons, which runs 51 liquor stores in Western Australia, says the cachet of the Grange label will still draw in buyers even though they are being asked to open their wallets for a second time. He believes the amount of Grange allocated to Australian retailers is steadily declining, as Treasury puts energy behind turning Penfolds into a truly global brand in the luxury market.

"Grange is Grange. They will sell out year after year because of its reputation, investment credibility and its collect­ability,'' he says.

PE proposals leave shareholders hanging and wincing

Newspaper October 11, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 970 Words
Page: 35
OpenURL Link
Takeovers Investors should know to wait for deals to hatch before counting chickens.

More than $800 million in market value has been shredded from two ASX-listed companies, Treasury Wine Estates and compliance and quality assurance firm SAI Global, since proposed takeover bids by private equity firms amounted to nothing.

While shareholders in both com­panies are wincing, should they have been surprised? Private equity suitors in Australia have been breathlessly charging into boardrooms with non-binding and indicative proposals for potentially big takeover deals for more than a ­decade. But the latest misses add to an increasing sense that investors should be wary about the prospect of a change of ownership actually happening. The private equity firms vigorously defend their actions. The over-arching body representing their interests says there needs to be an overhaul of regulations and policies about when an initial proposal should be disclosed to the market. The Australian Private Equity and Venture Capital Association chief executive Yasser El-Ansary says on many occasions it is so early in the process and highly conditional, and price expectations are only a rough guide but taken as gospel by the market.

He points to the strong returns ­delivered by private equity firms to their institutional investors, which at 23 per cent for the 12 months ended March 31, outstripped the 13 per cent returns from the ASX 300, as the best yardstick for the disciplined nature of their work once they get under the ­bonnet of a company.

But big fund managers such as Perpetual's head of equities Matt Williams say it's natural for more cynicism to be building about the low number of deals that actually happen. Treasury and SAI are just the latest examples in a long list over the past few years, which has also included approaches to underwear and clothing maker Pacific Brands and his own firm, Perpetual. "I think private equity need to consummate a few more deals than we've seen lately for that sort of feeling not to perpetuate," he says.

"The more non-binding indicative bids that occur and don't amount to anything, the more cynical the market becomes about the whole process," he says.

The heavy falls in share prices of Treasury – which owns brands including Penfolds, Rosemount and Wolf Blass – and SAI – which administers the five-ticks quality standards mark as well as selling 7000 different sets of standards to industry – has, of course, been accelerated by the overall drop in the sharemarket in the past few weeks.

But the share price spikes that accompany the public announcement that a private equity approach has been made and then which stay built in for months as due diligence is completed, disappear quickly when no deal results.

US firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and rival TPG both did almost two months of due diligence on Treasury but in the end couldn't ­justify paying a high enough price to win over the board. Although KKR originally made an approach in mid-April, which wasn't disclosed to the ASX until a few weeks later in May, TPG was much later to the process.

In early August, they both signalled indicative proposals built around $5.20 a share. But after weeks of looking inside the company's inner workings and much more serious number-crunching they didn't end up getting that high, although KKR went close.

In the SAI case, Pacific Equity ­Partners made the initial approach on May 15 with a proposal in a range of $5.10 to $5.25. It also wasn't publicly ­disclosed initially, but SAI then disclosed it on May 26 to the ASX in a twin announcement which also revealed the shock exit of its chief executive Stephen Porges after just four months.

KKR then joined forces with PEP but in mid-September SAI announced there had been no whole-of-company bids, and the share price has been ­sinking since. It is currently wallowing at around $3.70. There have also been rumblings that the SAI board didn't over-extend itself in the due diligence process and didn't really want to be taken over anyway, but SAI defends the sale process as being a model one.

Perpetual's Matt Williams says fund managers do like to see market val­uations tested and have the opportunity to cash out in a successful takeover bid.

"As investors you always want the opportunity for the market to be tested," he says.

El-Ansary says Australia is a net ­capital importing country and global private equity firms take into account a vast number of factors including the regulatory framework when assessing potential buyout targets.

He says AVCAL thinks an overhaul of the disclosure regime is required, and it is about to undertake detailed work on the issue before making represen­tations to authorities and regulators in early 2015.

"Here in Australia we do have a somewhat unique approach to when things are made public. That does, in some ways, pose a challenge in that expectations are set very early on," he says.

El-Ansary says it is difficult having to put forward a highly conditional ­proposal with pricing indications so early in a process, and then have that publicly aired prematurely.

This leads to an assumption by shareholders that a binding offer may take place at that level, even though boards of target companies do warn that there is no guarantee a formal bid will occur.

"There's a question here that needs to be asked about what is the right model."

Escaping the bearhug

■ 2014 Treasury Wine Estates rejects a $3b takeover from Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and a separate bid from TPG

■ 2014 Pacific Equity Partner's offer for SAI Global comes to nothing

■ 2013 Altamont Capital Partners takeover offer to Billabong is trumped by a restructuring deal that keeps the group public

■ 2012 Pacific Brands ends takeover discussions with KKR

■ 2010 KKR's $1.75b approach to Perpetual proves unsuccessful

■ 2007 Orica rejects $10b takeover approach from consortium of private equity firms including Bain Capital Partners, Blackstone Capital Partners, and Pacific Equity Partners

■ 2007 TPG and Macquarie lead a failed $11.1b bid for Qantas

■ 2006 Coles rejects bids from KKR, ultimately selling to Wesfarmers

Profits up 75pc in time of tragedy

Newspaper October 7, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 553 Words
Page: 16
OpenURL Link
It's been a tragic time for Peter ­Lehmann Wines.

In late June former managing ­director Doug Lehmann died just a year after his famous father and company founder Peter Lehmann lost a long ­battle with kidney disease.

But the Barossa Valley company has stayed on course to deliver improved sales and profits, with sales of its ­bottled wines particularly strong in Australia.

Peter Lehmann Wines produced a 75 per cent increase in net profit to $754,000 in the 12 months ended June 30, 2014, while revenue improved by 13 per cent to $47.9 million.

The company is 86 per cent owned by Swiss-based wine and hotels ­business Hess Group and still has a total of about 450 shareholders. It remains an unlisted public company since a $149 million takeover by Hess in 2003 meant its listing disappeared from the Australian stock exchange.

The annual financial statements for 2013-14 have just been lodged with the corporate regulator in Australia and they show that on September 26, 2014, the company began a new ­financing facility with GE Capital. The five-year agreement contains facilities totalling $22 million.

Peter Lehmann Wines chairman Christoph Ehrbar said in the ­company's financial statements that domestic sales had climbed by 39 per cent to $22.8 million.Exchange rate difficulties

"The domestic market now ­represents 61 per cent of our overall ­bottled sales," he said.

But the high Australian dollar meant export sales had had a difficult time, particularly to the United States.

Mr Ehrbar said overall revenue from exports was down by 17 per cent and North American markets represented more than half of that decline. The ­company had made substantial changes to its distribution network in Canada to sales and was closely ­scrutinising its arrangements in the US.

"We are currently reviewing our strategies in the US,'' he said.

Hess Group is the company's ­controlling shareholder with 86 per cent. The estate of founder Peter ­Lehmann holds 10.4 per cent and among the shareholders are grapegrowers from the Barossa Valley.

Doug Lehmann, who was managing director for 17 years until mid-2010, was deputy chairman until his death from a heart attack on June 29 this year.

The company opted not to pay a ­dividend for 2013-14 and instead focused on paying down debt, which has been reduced by 25 per cent. It has $16.6 million in franking credits available for use for future dividends. During 2012-13 the company breached banking covenants and was forced to negotiate a new banking facility with National Australia Bank. At June 30, 2014, the loan facility with NAB was $15.1 million.

The increase in profit and revenues is a welcome event for shareholders after such a difficult period for the company with the deaths of Peter and Doug Lehmann in a short time, although profits are well below those the company made five years ago.

Peter Lehmann Wines made a net profit, after tax of $3.8 million in 2009-10, then showed a decline to $1.01 million in 2010-11 before plunging to a loss of $520,000 in 2011-12.

It returned to the black in 2012-13, making a net profit after tax of $430,000.

The decline in the Australian dollar over the past few weeks is set to be a plus for Australia's wine exporters, both small and large. Treasury Wine Estates, owner of Penfolds and Wolf Blass, ­outlined in mid-August that it benefits by about $2.3 million for each 1¢ drop in the Australian dollar versus the US ­currency.

The Australian dollar has fallen nearly US8¢ since the start of ­September, and is now sitting at a ­four-year low.

Ghosts from Foster's past - Inside the deal

Newspaper October 4, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Sarah Thompson Section: Companies and Markets 1239 Words
Page: 38
OpenURL Link
TPG's Project Fitzpatrick had some expert advice from ex-Foster's wine men, but KKR's Project Southern Cross offered higher value.

A towering figure loped into the United States head office of Treasury Wine Estates in the picturesque Napa Valley wine region in California in mid-August. As a potential $3.4 billion takeover loomed, the presence of Pete Scott, a former chief financial officer of Foster's Group, was an intriguing twist to an already complicated private equity play for the locally listed wine producer.

Scott had been the chief number-cruncher for the Australian beer and wine giant from 2002 until early 2008 when he was based in Melbourne.

Strolling into the Napa Valley office with Scott was Walt Klenz. Klenz is also a person very familiar with the inner workings of Foster's and the Beringer wine business in the US. The US is at the centre of Treasury's troubled times over the past 18 months and its poor performance in that region is part of the reason why Treasury found itself fending off takeover bids. Klenz had in the 1990s been the chief executive of the same Beringer business in California which Foster's paid an eye-popping $2.9 billion for in 2000 to dramatically expand its global wine operations.

Scott and Klenz both live in California and run the Vincraft Group. They were two secret weapons enlisted by private equity firm TPG as part of its "Project Fitzpatrick'' tilt to try and over-take rival firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts in a battle to produce a winning bid of up to $3.4 billion for Treasury.

Treasury split off from Foster's in mid-2011 to become a separate entity, with wine brands including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Rosemount and Lindemans, plus the large US wine business led by the Beringer brand.

Ultimately both KKR and TPG have come up empty-handed for now after an exhaustive due diligence process, Treasury will continue on as an ASX-listed company. But it has a much deflated share price.

More than $460 million has been wiped from the Treasury market capitalisation since the board led by chairman Paul Rayner officially announced on September 29 it had ended talks with both suitors because they wouldn't pay the price tag the board wants.

Treasury's board and advisers from Goldman Sachs led by investment bankers Christian Johnston and Zac Fletcher, who code-named their defence Project Kingdom, had a large amount of input from offshore fund managers who own stakes in Treasury.

They included Wellington Management, Capital Group and Harris Associates. Those fund managers were convinced that even at the indicative price outlined in early August of $5.20 per share the private equity firms would be racing off with Treasury at the bottom of the cycle.

Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke spoke to many of them on post-results investor ­roadshows overseas after the company's unconvincing full-year results on August 21.

They were buoyed by the latent value of large amounts of Penfolds wines in storage due to come out onto the market over the next two years, plus a weakening Australian dollar which lifts the bottom line by $2.3 million for every one cent drop against the US dollar.

A series of phone hook-ups were then held with fund managers in the days leading up to Friday, September 26, most of whom thought $5.20 per share undervalued the company. Treasury set a 5pm deadline on September 26 for the suitors to show their final wares, to try and bring matters to a head. KKR was closest to the $5.20 mark with its indications of value, and had its financing completely locked in under the deal, code-named Project Southern Cross. It was well ahead of TPG. TPG wanted an extra week to see if it could get to the upper end of a range it had been working in of $4.50 to $4.70 per share. It was refused.

A pre-arranged Saturday morning Treasury board phone hook-up was reminded of the resolute view of big shareholders that $5.20 wasn't enough. But had KKR really played its last hand? Was this part of a broader tactical game? There were further phone calls between advisers over the weekend seeking absolute finality.

After one last re-read and check by company boffins early on Monday September 29, Treasury fired off an announcement to the ASX, revealing the plug had been pulled. It had been an exhausting and distracting six months since KKR first approached Treasury.

Nomura's joint head of investment banking John Hanson, and its executive chairman Peter Meurer worked closely with KKR and its junior partner Rhone Capital. KKR's Hong Kong-based ­consumer expert Steve Ko, and KKR's Australian boss Justin Reizes were closely involved.

A profit downgrade by Treasury on January 30 originally sparked KKR into action. It made an initial approach in mid-April, which wasn't publicly disclosed until a few weeks later by Treasury. The prize, if secured, would sit in KKR's Pan Asian Fund II, a monster $6 billion fund hunting across Asia for good deals.

There was extraordinary cross-over in specialist advisers used by both firms. KKR enlisted the help of former Coca-Cola Amatil managing director Terry Davis early in the process. Davis was running Foster's wine business in 2000 when it bought Beringer for $2.9 billion. Scott, an American who was finance director for Beringer at the time of that acquisition, then shifted to Australia to work for Foster's in 2002, while Klenz in late 2001 took over running Foster's entire global wine operations while still based in California. Klenz was abruptly put into that role when Davis left Foster's to begin his 13-year stint running the CCA soft drinks business.

TPG had been eyeing the Treasury business for more than three years. Project Fitzpatrick, named after former Carlton premiership captain Mike Fitzpatrick in the AFL, who is now a Rio Tinto director, has been alive since 2011. TPG is understood to have made an approach to Foster's about buying a stake in the Treasury wine business just before the de-merger of Foster's in May, 2011, but was spurned.

The glitz of showbiz also cast its shadow in the sales process.

Treasury's chief executive Clarke walked into Goldman Sach's Los Angeles headquarters at Fox Plaza on the Avenue of the Stars in LA in early September for a lengthy five-hour meeting with up to 60 bankers and financiers from TPG's bid team. At that point things were getting very serious. Treasury itself on August 29 announced it was delaying its annual meeting for shareholders because it may need to coincide it with a scheme of arrangement meeting where shareholders would vote on a buyout deal.

High-flying Los Angeles is in stark contrast to the Builders Arms Hotel in the trendy inner Melbourne suburb of Fitzroy. In all of the high-stakes negotiations, there was room for a laugh. Soon after TPG came roaring into the bidding process with a $5.20 indicative offer on August 11, Clarke, his chief financial officer Tony Reeves and TPG's key Australia executives Ben Gray and Simon Harle were invited to a dinner by Goldman Sachs bankers to foster good relations. The location was locked in at the Builders Arms. It was discovered only at a late hour they didn't have Treasury wines on the wine list. Some hasty BYO of Treasury's finest was organised.

Treasury shareholders though won't be laughing much now. The shares are wallowing around the low $4.20 mark. That is despite the company revealing on September 29 that trading was ahead of budgets on revenues and gross profit after the first three months of 2014-15. A week ago the shares were $4.92. The private equity suitors have vanished for now, but there is a strong sense among many in the market they could make a return.

Treasury benefits from wine glut

Newspaper October 3, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 401 Words
Page: 16
OpenURL Link
One of Treasury Wine Estate's top executives says it will continue to benefit from an oversupply of grapes in the Australian wine industry over the past 10 years.

Stuart McNab, Treasury's global supply and production manager, says the oversupply of grapes has been a plus for Treasury because it lowers input costs. He says it is likely to continue, although it is hard to predict for how long. "It's not normal to stay in oversupply for 10 years," he said.

Mr McNab says the company's strategy is to tilt towards higher-priced luxury and premium wines.

The company's vineyard self-sufficiency ratio is at about 25 per cent, which means it buys many of its grapes from outside growers. Treasury has 83 wine brands in its portfolio, including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Rosemount, Wynns and Lindemans, and is the world's largest stand-alone stock exchange listed wine company.

He says it's difficult to forecast when supply and demand might move back into balance because of factors such as the size of grape harvests in other countries and the determination of local independent grape growers who might be running at a loss.

Mr McNab said many grape growers were reluctant to exit the industry even if they were uneconomic because there were few alternatives.

Treasury's board on Monday announced it was ending talks about a proposed $3.4 billion buyout. This came after an exhaustive due diligence process where Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and junior partner Rhone Capital, and rival bidder TPG Capital, investigated the inner workings of the company but couldn't pay what the company's major institutional shareholders wanted. Treasury shares have fallen 14.4 per cent from $4.92 since the rejection on Monday morning.

Chairman Paul Rayner said about half of its share register by aggregate didn't think a price of $5.20 per share was enough. This was despite indicative proposals made in early August by both private equity firms at the $5.20 per share mark, which allowed them to conduct due diligence.

Treasury announced on August 29 a delay of its annual meeting, expecting it would be held to coincide with a scheme of arrangement meeting in which Treasury shareholders would vote on a takeover bid.

Mr McNab said the Australian grape crush over the past few years had been averaging about 1.8 million tonnes, although there were seasonal fluctuations, and it was unusual that supply remained so high with so many grape growers making a loss. "They've stayed in production but at a much lower capital base," he said.

Treasury Wines 'unzips' top-end labels

Newspaper October 1, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 614 Words
Page: 17
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke and one of his senior ­executives are assessing up to five ­different options before making a final decision on an internal structure likely to include a separate sales and marketing arm for the company's top-end wine brands.

They are aggressively moving to "unzip" them from the more commercial wine brands, which will also have a dedicated sales and marketing force which is likely to be in place by the end of the year.

Treasury has also singled out several commercial brands in its troubled United States operations as "non-priority'' but won't divulge which ones they are, as it attempts to move higher up the value chain and divert more investment into the luxury end of the market. The non-priority lower-priced brands may be sold, or funnelled into a joint venture structure.

Mr Clarke is working closely with the general manager of Treasury's Australian and New Zealand operations, Angus McPherson, on what Mr Clarke terms the unzipping project to put more grunt behind the higher-priced wine brands and obtain better returns from the commercial portfolio.

The initial separation will be confined to the Australian market and some of the commercial brands in the United States, but then broaden out across the entire US operations.

The duo are assessing a number of different models for the structure internally, with a separate sales and marketing unit devoted to brands like Penfolds, Wynns and the higher-priced Wolf Blass labels, and one for the commercial brands.

Mr Clarke has been holding a series of "town hall''-style meetings in Melbourne with Treasury staff over the past 48 hours along with phone briefings for the major Treasury overseas offices following the announcement by the board that it had ended talks with private equity suitors about a proposed buyout after months of due diligence.

Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, with junior partner Rhone Capital, got closest to an indicative $5.20 per share value in final proposals last Friday, but TPG Capital was some way lower. Treasury shares slipped further on Tuesday to close 5.8 per cent lower at $4.24. It has lost 14 per cent in value since Monday morning in the wake of decision announced by chairman Paul Rayner that many of Treasury's large institutional shareholders believed $5.20 per share undervalued the company.

The structural changes inside Treasury are part of Mr Clarke's fix-it plans after a series of heavy write-downs and profit downgrades over the past few years prior to his arrival on March 31. The Penfolds brand in particular will receive more investment spending to try and turn it into a global luxury brand, while the release date of new vintages for the flagship Grange and other high-priced Penfolds labels is being switched to mid-October to cash in on the pre-Christmas market.

Credit Suisse analyst Larry Gandler said the reason the buyout transaction failed "was because there was no silver bullet to unlock significant value and rapidly retire debt or partially fund the transaction''.

UBS analyst Craig Stafford has a 12-month price target of $4.33 on Treasury and recommends investors sell.

No silver bullet

Reasons the private equity deal failed

Divesting brands probably could not realise significant value because of their low margin or declining revenue profile. (Rosemount or Lindemans, for example.)

Divesting geographies such as US probably would have resulted in complex licensing and distribution arrangements, or alternatively revealed diseconomies of scale.

Rapid inventory depletion of luxury wine without replenishment, while cash generative, would not have been beneficial to the long-term health of the Penfolds brand.

Cash generating capability of TWE is scant given that growth objectives require working capital and acquisitions.

Availability of tax losses turned out to be an unimportant value factor.

A sale and leaseback of key vineyards would have been unlikely to unlock significant value.

Groans around bank boardrooms

Newspaper September 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Michael Smith Section: Chanticleer 1233 Words
Page: 60
OpenURL Link
The threat of intervention by regulators on high-risk mortgage lending is not keeping bank chief executives awake at night but it adds to the uncertainty hanging over the majors at the moment.

Three of Australia's big four banks have a September 30 financial year end and will sign off on another 12 months of record profits on Tuesday.

But the prospect of a repeat performance in the current year is less assured.

A decline in bank stocks has been a major contributor to the Australian ­market's 6.4 per cent fall in September, although a lot of this is to do with offshore investors putting their money elsewhere now that United States interest rates are set to rise rather than a problem with ­fundamentals.

But the regulatory threats coming from all fronts are not helping sentiment either and the banks admit life is about to get a lot tougher than it has been in the past.

David Murray's report into the financial system and G20 agenda dealing with the "too big to fail" issue raise the prospect of tougher capital requirements for Australia's banks.

The surge in property prices in parts of Sydney, Melbourne and Brisbane is ­creating another issue for the banks to ­contend with if the Reserve Bank decides the economy can no longer afford a hands-off approach when it comes to risky lending.

The banks are opposed to the ­introduction of macro-prudential controls which could impose restrictions on loan-to-value ratios, something New Zealand has been doing for a while with mixed results.

While Australia is unlikely to go down the New Zealand path, the Reserve Bank has put the issue back on the agenda after flagging the prospect of measures to tackle "unbalanced" lending.

This could include forcing banks to increase capital against certain types of loans and assessing lending requirements based on potential future interest rate rises. This could mean providing a buffer by assessing a home loan application on the ability to repay a loan at a higher future ­interest rate instead of the 5 per cent actual rate currently.

While there was a collective groan around bank boardrooms last week when the central bank flagged the possibility in its financial stability review, bank chiefs have remained tight-lipped on the issue.

This is partly because it is unclear what the RBA and APRA will do and it is too early to make a formal assessment of the potential risks to their lending profiles.

The challenge for banks is to avoid a knee-jerk response to a series of what-ifs. They are also confused about how a crackdown on lending would fit into anything that emerges from the Murray inquiry.

The banks are also hyper-sensitive about talking up an issue and scaring away investors at a time when their stock is on the nose.

Macro-prudential control is not a new idea. It regularly crops up in conversations with bank chairman and chief executives and the RBA and the Australian Prudential Regulation Authority.

Some bank executives say privately they are not worried about the issue, taking comfort from previous interaction with the RBA where it indicated an unenthusiastic approach towards intervention.

They also argue better quality control is good for them as well and that their own loan-to-value ratios have come down a fraction anyway.

The banks will also argue macro-prudential controls are a waste of time if you are applying restrictions to loans covering all geographies when the rampant growth in prices is confined to particular types of properties in Sydney, Melbourne and parts of Sydney.

There is a case though for targeted intervention in loans for investment properties, particularly those with high debt levels which pay interest only. The signs are that first home buyers would not be considered high risk.

The banks will also point out comments by the RBA last week that the risk from the current strength in housing is more likely to be future household spending than to their own balance sheets. Interest rate rises may impact a consumer-led economic recovery because many Australians will funnel any discretionary funds back into servicing their home loan .

Banks will also argue they already have an interest buffer on residential home loans. A 225-basis point interest rate buffer already applies to many home loans but could also be applied to investment properties.

Bank chiefs are a conservative lot and they won't want to rock the boat on this sensitive issue until there is more clarity.

Treasury Wine Estates boss Michael Clarke has his work cut out for him as he finally sets the winemaker on a solo course after months of ownership uncertainty as the private equity sharks circled.

TWE and suitor Kohlberg Kravis Roberts have decided to part ways after being unable to a agree to a deal which valued the company at the $5.20 a share the board wanted.

TWE chairman Paul Raynor and Clarke pulled the plug on talks over the weekend after realising KKR was not willing to come to the party with a $5.20 per share offer.

The other bidder in the mix, TPG Capital, was offering a figure well south of that and both bidders had their own regulatory or funding issues which were going to make a deal even harder to get away.

The onus is now on Clarke to prove the sceptics wrong and show shareholders his turnaround strategy can deliver greater value than the potential $3 billion-plus offers that were proposed.

Clarke and Raynor have been opposed to a takeover from the beginning but did the right thing by agreeing to engage after rejecting an initial low-ball offer.

Differences around strategy, forecasts, valuation and regulatory obstacles for the bidders were listed among Treasury's reasons for wrapping up the talks with KKR and TPG.

This was a surprise to the market which had been betting on a deal getting through after years of on-again, off-again takeover talks. The the other surprise was news from the TWE camp that just under half its shareholders believed a $5.20 a share offer undervalued the company.

Certainly, the market saw value in an offer at that level and Treasury shares are now down 8.5 per cent now that the takeover premium has been stripped out.

If half of TWE's shareholders were in fact willing to reject a $5.20 bid it would say a lot about how the market views a company's fundamentals these days. Shareholders will always agitate for a higher offer but traditionally have been happy to accept a private equity cash offer f there is a half decent premium involved.

Clarke is desperate for a chance to implement his turnaround strategy and did not want talks dragging on until Christmas if there was no certainty of getting a deal away.

He has been complaining for months the process was hugely distracting for the company and that he wants a chance to get on with it without the uncertainty of an ownership change cramping his style.

The pressure is on. Investors have high expectations now that an offer of $5.20 was within their grasp.

There are positives in Clarke's plans to shuffle the release dates of Penfolds premium wines and cut back the number of brands in the stable but the jury is still on out on whether it can repair the value ­erosion from some bad decisions by former management.

TWE says the private equity bidders endorsed Clarke's strategy and believes there was not a lot of additional taking the company private would bring.

It is back to the drawing board for KKR, which has been unable to land that big Aussie deal that has also made bids for fund manager Perpetual and Coles Myer. Buyout firms are hungry for deals but only at the right price now that the heady pre-2007 days are well and truly over.

Bidders not likely to return: Treasury boss

Newspaper September 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Sarah Thompson Section: Companies and Markets 620 Words
Page: 21
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates had $275 million wiped from its market capitalisation on Monday after terminating talks with two private equity suitors, with chief executive Mike Clarke saying he doesn't believe they will return with a fresh proposal.

"I think it's over, is my point of view," Mr Clarke said.

The Treasury boss also hinted at "opportunities for inorganic changes within our organisation" including bolt-on acquisitions at the luxury end of the market in the United States, small divestments and potential alliances to help accelerate a turnaround. This is on top of his fix-it plans already outlined several months ago.

The Treasury board led by chairman Paul Rayner is backing those fix-it plans by Mr Clarke, who joined the company as chief executive on March 31. They include aggressively lifting investment behind the higher-end brands like Penfolds and stripping out $35 million in costs from back-office and admini­stration.

But the exit of Kohlberg Kravis ­Roberts, and rival TPG Capital, triggered a collapse in the share price.

Treasury shares plummeted 15 per cent in early trade on Monday after the company said it had decided to scrap any more talks with bidders on potential buyout transactions, because the proposals weren't high enough.

The shares recovered however, and by the close were down 42¢ to $4.50, a slide of 8.5 per cent.

Wellington Management, the large Boston-based fund manager which holds 7.9 per cent of Treasury, is understood to be one of the large institutions backing the Treasury rejection, and part of the "clear feedback" received by Mr Rayner from investors who hold around 50 per cent of Treasury's share register. Most of the top 15 institutions have told Treasury that a price of $5.20 per share undervalues the company.

Mr Clarke also said he was indifferent about whether Treasury was listed on the stock exchange or in private hands, because the most important thing was to have the right strategy and then deliver results for whoever the owner was.

"I'm indifferent as to whether we're publicly or privately owned," he said. Mr Clarke said in the first three months of 2014-15, Treasury was ahead of its financial budgets on a range of measures including sales volumes, ­revenue, gross profit and earnings. That was an early sign that the company can win back the trust of investors and ­consistently deliver what it promises after failing to do so previously.

"I'd like to crawl, walk and run and prove to shareholders that's what we can do," he said. Mr Clarke said the private equity firms had been unable to reach an acceptable price tag in their proposals, and so there was no point in continuing with discussions.

KKR and junior partner Rhone Capital are understood to have signalled late on Friday they couldn't reach the full $5.20 per share indicative level previously flagged on August 4, but outlined a range with conditions attached where the upper end was above $5 per share.

This put them well ahead of TPG. But the board wanted more.

It is understood that TPG's final proposal late on Friday was pitched broadly between a range of between $4.50 and $4.70 per share but with a condition that more work and time was required. This was well below its indicative proposal of $5.20 on August 11.

Mr Clarke also said the level of Treasury's share price was for others to judge.

"My team and I clearly cannot manage the share price," he said.

But the due diligence process had proved Treasury was on the right path because both of the firms had validated the Treasury strategy, which gave the company more confidence.

TPG's bidding power was curtailed by regulatory issues connected with its ownership of casino assets in the US and tied house regulations around the ownership structures for licensed ­venues, hotels and bars.
