Treasury hit by hangover after talks fail

Newspaper September 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 516 Words
Page: 40
OpenURL Link
A 15 per cent plunge in the share price is a sobering lesson.

The pressure on Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke – and his board, led by chairman Paul Rayner – to deliver a consistent and sustained improvement in profits over the next few years is immense.

With the help of some influential offshore shareholders, the board has decided that if private equity suitors Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts and TPG Capital are both fully behind what Treasury is trying to do with its own fix-it plans and the overall strategy, then there is little point in handing the keys over to them right now, rather than making the changes themselves.

But then there's the small matter of the takeover premium built into the stock for the past five months.

After the board announced on Monday that talks had been terminated, Treasury shares plummeted 15 per cent, but recovered about half the losses to be down 8.5 per cent at the close.

Building back that value will mean selling a lot of Penfolds, Rosemount, Wolf Blass, Wynns and Beringer, consistently, right across the globe over an extended time frame.

Both KKR and TPG indicated during due diligence that they thought Clarke was doing a good job, his plans were sensible and he was the right man to keep in place should they become the new owners.

Ultimately, they could get the numbers to work in the final number-crunching but not high enough to win over the board. Clarke himself says they were crimped by complex liquor, gaming and hospitality regulations, and issues around financing and the private equity model of putting more debt inside Treasury than the company currently faces. Rayner and his advisers sounded out institutional shareholders and the view was consistent: $5.20 was not enough.

The big question now is: Can the Treasury board deliver the goods? The business has been a difficult beast for more than a decade – in the past three-and-a-half years as a stand-alone wine group and before that as the wine division of Foster's Group. There have been a litany of stumbles and mis-adventures in an industry which is extremely capital hungry.

It is very early days, but as Clarke revealed on Monday, trading for the first three months is tracking ahead of plans. That's a small positive. And it's across all of the main trading regions around the world, including the troublesome US.

Both KKR and TPG in the end, submitted final proposals below their previous indicative proposals of $5.20 a share made in August. KKR is believed to have been close to the $5.20 mark in its final play, but that did not cut it. TPG was some way below that. It's worth remembering KKR's very first indicative proposal in May was pitched at $4.70. That price was at a 28 per cent premium to the one-month volume weighted average share price of $3.68 over the preceding weeks.

It's been a roller coaster ride but on paper, those patient, former Foster's shareholders who stayed put are still ahead, with the stock having traded at $3.45 on its first day as a stand-alone company on May 9, 2011. But in May, 2013 the shares were flying at $6.47.

KKR tipped to win Treasury Wine - Acquisitions Bid beats TPG, values company at $3.4b- Exclusive

Newspaper September 29, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Sarah Thompson Section: Companies and Markets 874 Words
Page: 11
OpenURL Link
Private equity giant Kohlberg Kravis Roberts is in the prime position to win a recommendation from the board of Treasury Wine Estates after lodging a bid significantly higher than rival firm TPG.

The Treasury board is understood to have held KKR to a price of about $5.20, which values Australia's largest wine company at $3.4 billion. KKR and junior partner Rhone Capital offered that amount on August 4, up from the original solo KKR proposal of $4.70 which became public on May 20.

The board of Treasury and its advisers were still locked in high-level meetings on Sunday night as they approached a final decision, which may be announced to the Australian Securities Exchange as early as Monday.

Treasury chairman Paul Rayner has been under intense pressure after a series of missteps at the world's largest stand-alone wine company over the past 15 months, with a change of ownership now highly likely for the owner of Penfolds, Rosemount, Wolf Blass, Wynns and Lindemans.

KKR and New York-based Rhone lodged a formal takeover bid for Treasury at around 5pm on Friday, while TPG also did likewise in time to meet a secret deadline put in place by the Treasury board and its advisers from Goldman Sachs.

The merits of each offer were closely examined by the Treasury camp over the weekend, with both bids ­understood to have a lower-than-usual debt component because of the unpredictable nature of the agricultural industry in which Treasury operates.

A spokesman for Treasury declined to comment on Sunday.

Both private equity firms lodged indicative proposals in August valuing Treasury at $3.4 billion and made separate official bids after an exhaustive and intense due diligence period over the past few weeks which included visits to offshore operations and wineries around Australia.

Both bids were pitched under a scheme of arrangement proposal.

The decline in the Australian dollar has played into the hands of the US-based private equity bidders, with each 1¢ drop delivering a $2.3 million injection to the company's profits.

The weaker Australian dollar delivered more financial firepower for the final bids because it makes Australian assets cheaper, with the maker of Penfolds and Wolf Blass increasingly likely to end up with an offshore owner.

While both KKR and its rival TPG are building in long-term projections for where the Australian currency is likely to be against the United States dollar for the next few years, most experts believe it will continue to fall, meaning the timing of the US predators is good as they look to rebuild profits should a bid be accepted by the Treasury board.

Stephen Harvey, chairman of Deloitte's Australian Wine Industry Group, said the local wine industry was in for a much better era when it comes to currency, with local exports to become much more competitive on the shelves of retailers in the US and the United Kingdom. "We're in a much stronger position,'' he said.

The Australian dollar slipped to an eight-month low on Friday hitting US$0.8772 briefly because of a slump in commodity prices and fears about Australia's growth prospects. This was its lowest point since February, 2014. It compares with a peak of US$1.10 in mid-2011, with the currency remaining ­stubbornly high for much of the period since then, before its bout of recent weakness.

Treasury itself outlined some foreign exchange sensitivities on August 21 when it delivered its full-year results. Using a spot rate of US$0.93, it stated that a 1 cent change in the currency results in a $2.3 million impact on earnings before interest, tax and the SGARA accounting standard for 2014-15.

It also gave an outline of its foreign exchange risk management approach around hedging for the next two years for both the US dollar and British pound, but in practical terms analysts believe the $2.3 million impact is still around the mark when it comes to the US currency shifts. Stockbroking house Citi says the upside for the entire Australian wine sector is substantial from a sustained decline in the Australian currency, and for Treasury it will translate into better profit margins.

Citi analyst Gino Rossi said on Friday that demand from consumers in the United States for Australian wine is extremely sensitive to the level of the Australian dollar.

"US demand for Australian wine is highly sensitive to the Australian dollar and the US market alone is large enough to bring the Australian wine industry back into equilibrium. This will translate into higher wine prices and margins for Treasury,'' he said.

In 2006, Australian wine producers' total share of the wine import market in the US was 29 per cent at a time when the Australian dollar was at US$0.75. For the 12 months to the end of July it had shrunk to just 18 per cent.

Deloitte's Mr Harvey said during the past few years rival wine companies such as Argentina and Chile had made strong gains in the key US and UK markets at the expense of Australian producers because their own currencies were so weak and their bottled wine prices were extremely competitive on the shelves of liquor retailers in the US and Europe.

KKR and junior partner Rhone Capital lifted an indicative offer for Treasury on August 4 to $5.20 per share, while on August 11 TPG Capital made a proposal also pitched at $5.20 per share.

They both valued Treasury at $3.4 billion, with the rival proposals made via a scheme of arrangement where Treasury shareholders will vote at a meeting on a preferred bid.

Waiting for an Aussie miracle

Newspaper September 23, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Matthew Smith Section: Companies and Markets 699 Words
Page: 18
OpenURL Link
Wine Since 2010, Australian Vintage has been wearing the pain of the high dollar.

It has been a tough four years for Neil McGuigan, chief executive of Austra­lian Vintage. The high Australian dollar is eating into the company's export margins, making the already challenging job of running an unpredictable agriculture business even harder.

Profits from wine sales overseas are barely enough to keep the lights on back at its southern NSW Buronga Hill winery and processing plant.

But McGuigan has persisted, and by keeping Australian Vintage wines on the shelves and in front of wine drinkers in Britain, Canada and in the burgeoning Asian wine market, he has positioned the business to benefit from even the slightest movements of the currency in its favour.

"All we need now is a little bit of luck," McGuigan says.

Australian Vintage, maker of brands including McGuigan Wines, Nepenthe, Passion Pop and Tempus Two, has the sixth-largest brand in Britain by volume of wine sold and has the largest brand in Ireland. In August the company quietly announced it had signed a China-wide distribution agreement with COFCO Wine & Spirits, part of state-owned COFCO, China's largest food processor and manufacturer.

Since 2010, when the Australian dollar reached parity with the US dollar, McGuigan and the Australian Vintage board have been gritting their teeth and wearing the pain to avoid forfeiting the foothold the business has established in overseas markets over the years.

"Wherever the exchange rate has moved against us it has hurt, but you don't want to walk away from the hard work you've put into building those brands . . . When the [British] pound moved below 69 pence [relative to the Aussie in March last year] that was very painful. It's been tough," he says.

Australian Vintage is leveraged to the movements of the currency as is any other Australian listed company. When the dollar moves in a favourable way it has a material impact on profitability.

The relatively lower Australian dollar in the last financial year, when compared with previous years, contributed to the 97 per cent increase in contribution that British branded and bottled sales made to the group.

The company's overall net profit increased by 49 per cent to $10.5 million in 2013-14 thanks to more favourable foreign exchange and growth in sales of branded wines over the less-profitable bulk wine business.

But the Australian wine industry has felt a lot of pain over the past few years. There is still a way to go before Austra­lian Vintage's shareholders can expect to reap the rewards of more favourable trading conditions, should the dollar continue to follow its recent trajectory.

In addition, the damage done by the oversupply of Australian grapes a decade ago still hangs over the heads of Australian winemakers.

Australian Vintage, formerly named McGuigan Simeon, has transformed itself over the years from a bulk supplier of wines to now making most of its wine for its higher-margin branded products. But McGuigan has tried unsuccessfully in recent years to secure a distribution agreement for branded wines in the United States, which he says will be essential to selling into the North American market.

Australian Vintage had an agreement with US-based E&J Gallo to supply bulk wines until 2011, but the company has never been able to make a branded wine distribution there.

McGuigan believes the noise around the proposed private equity buyout of rival listed wine company Treasury Wine Estates (TWE) is not helping his efforts to position Australian Vintage's branded wines in the US. "Every conversation you have is about what's happening with TWE – what's going to happen with the ownership and what will happen to some of those brands," he says. "They're less interested in talking to us about our wine . . . [The buyout] needs to be resolved quickly and we need to get back to the knitting and talk about how good Australian wine is."

Under a KKR & Co-led buyout it has been speculated in the media that TWE's Penfolds brand could be spun out and floated separately in the US.

After years of Australian winemakers either going bust or being bought out by larger companies, Australian Vintage is one of three remaining ASX-listed winemakers, next to Treasury Wine Estates and the much smaller Brand New Vintage.

Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke has held an exhaustive five-hour meeting in Los Angeles with about 60 high-level bankers and financiers who are central to TPG Capital's $3.4 billion buyout proposal for the owner of Penfolds and Wolf Blass.

Rival Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and its junior partner Rhone Capital are also in the last stages of their due diligence, with control of Treasury now almost certain to change hands in one of the biggest takeovers in Australia this year.

KKR and Rhone made an increased buyout proposal of $5.20 per share on August 4 and TPG followed a week later with a rival proposal pitched at the same price and with similar conditions.

Both TPG and KKR are proposing a deal done under a scheme of arrangement where Treasury shareholders will get to vote on the deal, and 75 per cent approval will be required.

Takeover target Treasury Wine Estates has struck an exclusivity deal with Woolworths's Dan Murphy's liquor chain for a new wine under the Pepperjack brand, in the first major sign of chief executive Mike Clarke's push for some exclusive arrangements with Australia's two major liquor retailers.

Striking more exclusive deals with Woolworths and Coles is one of the planks of a revival strategy for Treasury to generate better profit growth for some of the middle-range brands in its portfolio.

Treasury is also in talks with Coles about potential deals for other labels which it intends selling only through Coles's Liquorland Australia chains: Liquorland, First Choice and Vintage Cellars.

Pepperjack is part of the Saltram stable, one of Treasury's middle-range brands. Other derivations under the Pepperjack brand are available in other non-Woolworths liquor chains. But the new exclusive deal is an example of part of the strategy being used by Mr Clarke to drive more profitable growth for the Treasury brand portfolio, with analysts suggesting it may go some way to blunting the combined market power of Woolworths and Coles in liquor.

The bigger selling brands owned by Treasury including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Wynns, Rosemount and Lindemans are quarantined from any exclusivity arrangements.

A spokeswoman for Woolworths liquor group said the company was pursuing further opportunities to expand the number of exclusive offers among the Treasury brands.

"The Pepperjack 2013 shiraz cabernet is exclusive to Dan Murphy's and is part of a continued review of other opportunities within Treasury Wine Estate's portfolio,'' she said.

A spokesman for Treasury said "we continue to work with all retailers across the portfolio to find opportunities that drive category growth, and deliver wines and brands to meet their shoppers' needs."

Treasury's $100m siphon for Project

Newspaper September 8, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 537 Words
Page: 20
OpenURL Link
Takeover target Treasury Wine Estates has flagged to private equity bidders that a substantial chunk of the $100 million earmarked for capital spending on the business this financial year is for "Project Uplift'' – a company-wide push to divert more spending into the production of higher-end wines.

New chief executive Mike Clarke inherited Project Uplift when he was hired in March this year but has ­reaffirmed internally he is right behind the drive which began in earnest three years ago to lift the levels of investment in oak barrels, and spend more on improving vineyard quality and other infrastructure used to produce ­higher-priced wines.

Private equity firms Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and its junior partner Rhone Capital, and rival TPG Capital, are heading into the final fortnight of due diligence after both making indicative buyout proposals which value ­Treasury at $3.4 billion.

Treasury, the owner of brands including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Wynns, Rosemount and Lindeman's, spent $17 million on oak barrel purchases in 2013-14 and ploughed $9 million into luxury fermenters at Penfolds as part of broader capital spending of $98.4 million during the year.Capital spending

Capital spending is foreshadowed to be around $100 million in 2014-15. This compares with total capital spending of $124 million across the Treasury business in 2012-13 and $81 million in 2011-12 when Treasury split off from former parent Foster's Group in May, 2011.

Mr Clarke is accelerating a push by Treasury to move higher up the value chain, led by Penfolds. Project Uplift has been undertaken across Treasury since the demerger to try to lift the ­company's ability to increase supply of high-end wines.

Peter Gago, chief winemaker for Penfolds, said the brand had proven resilient through a string of ownership changes. He is preparing for the release of the 2010 Penfolds Grange on October 16. This is a major shift from the ­traditional release date of the flagship wine in the Penfolds stable of May 1 each year and part of a deliberate ­strategy by Mr Clarke to better harness the long-term profitability of the brand by releasing it in the lead-up to the ­Christmas gift-giving season.

Mr Gago said the quality of the 2010 Grange is impressive and it is one of the best he has been a part of in his 12 years as Penfolds chief winemaker.

"Internally, the team are talking about this being one of the best,'' he said. "We aren't crowing too much yet though, because it is up to others to judge these things,'' he said. But he added that the 2010 grape harvest had been highly impressive in its quality.

Mr Gago also revealed that he hadn't yet had any discussions with either KKR/Rhone or TPG.

"I deliberately steer clear of that sort of thing."Indicative proposals

Both KKR/Rhone and TPG Capital have made indicative proposals pitched at $5.20 per share.

Paul Xiradis, head of equities at Ausbil Investment management said the fact that the Treasury board had on August 29 signalled a likely delay in the date of the company's annual meeting, indicated a transaction was looking more likely.

"It seems like all the ducks are lining up for that to occur,'' Mr Xiradis said.

Key points

Treasury Wine Estates is using Project Uplift to spend more on higher-end wines. Capital spending is foreshadowed to be around $100m in 2015-15.

US private equity giant Kohlberg Kravis Roberts has severed ties with former Coca-Cola Amatil chief executive Terry Davis as it heads into the final three weeks of an extensive due diligence on the inner workings of Treasury Wine Estates.

KKR and its junior partner Rhone Capital are understood to have now dispensed with the services of Mr Davis, who ran soft drinks and beer group CCA for 13 years until departing earlier this year.

Mr Davis provided broad advice to KKR and Rhone on a range of behind-the-scenes aspects on the proposal to buy out Australia's largest ­winemaker.

It comes as KKR and Rhone move into the final stages of due diligence on Treasury. This process may be completed by mid-late September as KKR attempts to outmuscle rival bidder TPG Capital.

Both private equity firms have made indicative proposals pitched at $5.20 per share for the maker of Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Rosemount, Seppelt and Wynns. This values Treasury at $3.4 billion.

Treasury shares closed 2¢ higher on Monday at $5.13.Support for Mike Clarke

Both private equity firms have been privately signalling they are right behind the fix-it plans of Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke for the company, which means there is a strong likelihood he will remain at the helm of the company should a change of ownership happen. Mr Clarke himself has said publicly he would like to remain in the job even under new owners, to finish the job of reinvigorating a company that had lost its way. His strategy involves dramatically increasing the amount of investment behind high-end wine brands such as Penfolds and Wynns, stripping out back office costs and shifting the release date of luxury Penfolds products to October each year, away from the original March to May release.

This switch will manifest itself in six weeks' time when Treasury will release its 2010 Penfolds Grange on October 16, bringing it forward almost eight months compared with the traditional release date, which would have been May 1, 2015.

The involvement of Mr Davis in the KKR/Rhone consortium had some in the market convinced he might be a potential future chief executive of Treasury, but KKR is believed to be heavily backing Mr Clarke's ­continuation.

The Treasury board has sent a clear signal to the market that a change of control is a strong likelihood, by foreshadowing on Friday that it wanted to coincide its upcoming annual general meeting with any scheme of arrangement meeting where shareholders will be required to vote to wave through any takeover.

This means the annual meeting is likely to be delayed beyond the originally scheduled date of October 30 this year.'No certainty' But it still left open the possibility that a firm, binding proposal may not eventuate.

"The board reiterates that there is no certainty that any proposal will result in an offer for the company,'' the Treasury board said in its note to the Australian Securities Exchange on Friday.

Both KKR/Rhone and TPG Capital have signalled they will use a scheme of arrangement in any final proposal.

Mr Clarke and Treasury chief financial officer Tony Reeves are at the forefront of the internal deal team inside Treasury as the two private equity firms move into the final stages of due diligence.

Schemes of arrangement are increasingly the model for bidders in big takeovers.

TPG makes up for lost time on Treasury Wine Estates

Newspaper August 29, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Edited by Sarah Thompson, Anthony Macdonald and Gretchen Friemann Section: Market Wrap 445 Words
Page: 14
OpenURL Link
It started a week behind, but private equity firm TPG Capital is setting a blistering pace on due diligence at Treasury Wine Estates.

It's been a whirlwind five days of management sessions as TPG puts the hammer down on what is shaping as a very serious proposal.

It is pushing hard to pull the rug out from under the original bidder, Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, and its junior equity investor, Rhone Capital.

TPG – which, in a slightly cute way, is still referred to in official circles as an "unnamed" party in the whole chase for the owner of Penfolds, Beringer, Wolf Blass, Rosemount and Lindeman's – came into the ring on August 11, a week after KKR and Rhone went into the tent after lifting the buyout proposal to $5.20 a share.

So what has TPG been doing?

Some vigorous sessions with TWE management have been undertaken: one in California's Napa Valley where, thankfully, the earthquake didn't cause too much damage, three full-on days with Treasury management in the Melbourne headquarters, and one in the Barossa Valley in South Australia, at the biggish wineries and vineyards.

TPG's United States boffins were all over the US assets and are right behind the push by Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke to pick up some extra luxury wine assets, but only in a small way.

TPG is very keen on Clarke as the man to take the company forward.

Former Coca-Cola boss Terry Davis is still closely linked to the KKR / Rhone proposal, but there are mumblings in the market that Davis may not have the cosiest relationship with Treasury's biggest customer, Woolworths.

Woolworths, which of course unveils its own full-year results on Friday, runs Dan Murphy's, BWS and a big portfolio of pubs with gaming magnate Bruce Mathieson.

So being in sweet with it seems to be a prerequisite for any big supplier of beer, wine or spirits. TPG has all its ducks in a row when it comes to financing and number-crunching.

On the financing front, TPG has JPMorgan, Deutsche Bank, Morgan Stanley, the Credit Agricole unit Calyon and HSBC in its corner.

JPMorgan and Deutsche Bank are understood to be the investment banks guiding TPG through the barrel halls full of good Penfolds red and top-end Wolf Blass grog.

At the more boring end, PwC is doing the accounting mumbo-jumbo.

But KKR / Rhone hasn't exactly been idle, either. It had a serious meeting on the financing front on Thursday.

TPG will hold its meeting with the banks in the next seven to 10 days.

Clarke is having a few well-earned days off after a ferocious few months where a buyout proposal was lobbed just weeks after he put his feet under the desk on March 31 at Treasury's Melbourne headquarters for the first time.

Flat beer sales shatter bottle maker

Newspaper August 27, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 628 Words
Page: 20
OpenURL Link
A flat beer market and wine companies continuing to ship wine offshore in large bladders before bottling it in the United Kingdom is hurting Australia's largest glass bottle maker, Owens-Illinois, even though it returned to profit after heavy restructuring.

Owens-Illinois Australia, which runs glass container plants in Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide and Brisbane, has also been battling an improved performance from the $2 billion Amcor offshoot Orora which is taking market share from the No. 1 player.

Owens-Illinois shut down some glass furnaces at its Australian plants in early 2012 as the local trend by big wine companies to transport bulk wine in large bladders known as flexitanks to the UK where they are bottled for the local market, began to seriously bite.

The Australian operations of Owens-Illinois generated a 1 per cent rise in revenue to $876 million in calendar 2013, according to the latest financial statements lodged with the corporate regulator last month. Net profit reached $33.7 million for 2013 compared with a loss of $26.6 million in 2012 when the company undertook heavy restructuring.

The tough market conditions have continued in the past few months. Owens-Illinois global chairman and chief executive Al Stroucken said on July 29 in a conference call there was "ongoing weak demand in Australia".

A spokeswoman for Owens-Illinois Australia said on Tuesday the official statistics on beer consumption in Australia showed a flat beer market

"That does play a part," she said.

Owens-Illinois employs 1000 people in its four plants in Australia and administrative and sales operations. It operates a glass-making plant at ­Penrith in western Sydney, one at ­Spotswood in the inner-west of Melbourne, West Croydon in Adelaide and South Brisbane.

The directors of Owens-Illinois Australia said in the financial statements the results for calendar 2013 included "additional costs related to the closure of a furnace as part of the Australian footprint realignment program".

The ASX-listed Orora, which was spun out of Amcor in late 2013, signalled in its results released on Monday that it had been snaring market share in glass. Orora chief executive Nigel Garrard said Orora's Australasian operations generated a 26 per cent rise in earnings before interest and tax to $162.5 million and in the beverage packaging operations "gained market share in the glass division, while volume in the cans division was stable".

Owens-Illinois Australia, which also makes glass containers for products such as baby food and tomato sauce, made a profit before tax of $55.7 million in calendar 2013 compared with a pre-tax loss of $41.6 million in 2012.

The directors of Owens-Illinois Australia said they also liquidated three dormant subsidiaries in 2013 which were a hangover from the former ACI Glass packaging businesses they acquired in 1998 in Australia.

Australia's major wine groups ship large quantities of lower-end commercial wine to bottling plants in the United Kingdom and Europe in large bladder-type containers to save costs in a highly competitive export market which has been squeezed by the high Australian dollar.

A spokesman for Treasury Wine Estates said on Tuesday that it was the more commercial wine that was transported that way, while the higher-priced wines were bottled in Australia and then transported.

Treasury, which owns Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindeman's and Rosemount among its 83-strong portfolio, is the subject of two separate $3.4 billion takeover bids.

Accolade Wines, which is 80 per cent owned by CHAMP Private Equity, ships vast quantities of commercial wine in bladders from Australia to a plant just outside London for bottling for the European market. Pernod Ricard, the owner of the big-selling Jacob's Creek brand, also ships ­commercial wine in bulk from Australia using bladders.

The parent company Owens-Illinois is listed on the New York Stock Exchange, has annual revenues of $US7 billion ($7.53 billion) and operates in 21 countries.

Key points

Owens-Illinois Australia hit by weak beer market and use of large bladders known as flexitanks to ship wine.

The bottle maker has also been losing share to an improved performance by ASX-listed Orora.

TWE eyes US high-end acquisitions

Newspaper August 22, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 660 Words
Page: 16
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke says the company is on the acquisition trail in the United States at the premium end of the market, as he reinforced his desire to keep running the company even if one of the two private equity suitors takes control.

He said momentum had improved in the troubled United States business and at the luxury end of the market Treasury had been caught out with a lack of supply in the US, which it wanted to rectify with acquisitions or alliances.

He also revealed that he thought Treasury's disappointing 2013-14 financial results represented a bottoming out, and improvement was on the horizon as his new plans for overhauling the company began to bite.

"I would hope there's upside from here," he said. But he declined to make a specific profit forecast for 2014-15.

Treasury, which has received two separate $3.4 billion buyout proposals from private equity firms, earlier on Thursday revealed it had plunged to an annual bottom-line loss of $101 million after suffering heavy write-downs of $281 million. Mr Clarke said he wanted to continue as chief executive even if one of the private equity bidders were successful.

Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and junior partner Rhone Capital made an increased bid for Treasury on August 4 of $5.20 per share, while TPG ­Capital made an offer pitched at the same price a week later. They are both doing due diligence.

Mr Clarke and his chief financial officer Tony Reeves are heading the internal deal team for Treasury as the private equity firms clamber through the inner workings of the company.

"We're trying to absorb the brunt of the work," Mr Clarke said, to shield others from potential distractions.

When the write-downs were stripped out, Treasury's underlying earnings for the year declined 14.6 per cent to $184.6 million and the wine group was forced to rely on currency adjustments to reach a previously projected profit guidance band of $190 million to $210 million.

After adjusting for foreign exchange rates, earnings before interest, tax and the SGARA accounting standard was $193 million.

The biggest disappointment for the company was the Australian and New Zealand business, which suffered a 32 per cent slide in earnings to $75.1 million. The Asian operations also slipped 12.3 per cent to post earnings of $47.7 million. The company's troubled US business made a slight improvement when foreign exchange impacts were included, lifting earnings 12.1 per cent to $74.9 million.

On a constant-currency basis, the Americas dropped 7 per cent. The US has been a troubled part of Treasury and its predecessor Foster's Group for much of the past decade after Foster's splashed out $2.9 billion at the top of the market in 2000 for Beringer Wine Estates. To look for expansion in the US now under Mr Clarke may come as a surprise to the market. The company also revealed it didn't end up destroying all of the low-end commercial wine it had earmarked for destruction last year in the US after being caught with too much stock. Mr Clarke revealed it only destroyed 240,000 cases of wine out of an original plan to crush 600,000 cases, and was selling the remainder through other channels. Total sales up

Treasury, which owns Penfolds, Rosemount, Lindemans, Wolf Blass and Wynns among a collection of 83 brands, said total sales revenue for 2013-14 increased 1 per cent to $1.7 billion.

The write-downs totalled $385 million on a pre-tax basis and were $281 million on a post-tax basis.

Those impairment charges related mainly to reductions in the asset value of commercial brands, infrastructure and equipment, and information technology systems.

The write-downs were slightly higher than expected, with Treasury having outlined on June 25 that it would sustain write-downs of as much as $260 million for the year ended June 30. On that day it made no change to previous guidance of earnings of between $190 million to $210 million, as Mr Clarke revealed his own plans on that day to supercharge the Treasury business.

TPG's eye on Treasury stirs Myer ghosts

Newspaper August 14, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Gretchen Friemann Section: Companies and Markets 551 Words
Page: 25
OpenURL Link
Private equity firms are canny investors but there is a crowning irony about TPG's pursuit of Penfolds producer, Treasury Wine Estates.

Part of the winemaker's allure is its $2 billion worth of tax losses, which are attached to its US arm.

Depending on the structure of TPG's bid, the private equity firm could utilise these losses to offset a capital gains tax elsewhere, according to a number of experts in the field. Such a strategy would stand in stark contrast to the firm's controversial exit from the department store chain Myer in 2009.

Back then the Australian Tax Office triggered a long-running debate about the opacity of the private equity sector when it tried and failed to claw back $678 million from TPG in the wake of Myer's disastrous initial public offering.

A belated effort to freeze the buyout firm's bank accounts also proved futile as the money was funnelled off-shore.

The ATO finally conceded defeat in 2011 after the Federal Court overturned the tax watchdog demands that TPG pay what had swollen into $739 million in outstanding liabilities.

Adrian Varrasso, a leading tax specialist at Minter Ellison,who is not acting for any party in the Treasury race, confirmed the private equity suitors may ultimately deploy the winemakers' tax losses to offset capital gains tax in other corners of their empire.

However he stressed that each transaction is "fact specific" and the advantages may be limited given the restrictions likely to come into force.ATO's watching brief

He also predicted the ATO would train a beady eye on the ­Treasury transaction.

While TPG's rivals for the winemaker, KKR and Rhone Capital, may also be able to utilise the tax losses, Mr Varrasso claimed the "history" around the Myer float would mean the ATO is likely to keep a watching brief on any deal.

He added the two situations are not related and whether or not TPG, or a competitor, can obtain benefits from the transfer of the tax losses will depend on the application of the tax law to specific circumstances.

Ever since the Myer controversy, private equity has striven to become more transparent and engage with regulators ahead of a large transaction.

Yasser El-Ansary, head of the Australian Private Equity & Venture Capital Association, previously said the Myer debate had left a positive legacy since the sector "is now a lot more visible".

But many argue Myer's abysmal share price performance post the float, along with the ensuing high-profile tax tussle, tarnished the buyout industry's reputation and closed the IPO market for almost 18 months.

After the 2009 listing, TPG's Australian head, Ben Gray, relocated to Singapore, returning earlier this year to Melbourne. One industry source pointed out the ATO did not have a legal case against TPG, and the ultimate test of the market's reaction was delivered last month with the successful float of Healthscope.

TPG has not confirmed it is looking at Treasury and declined to comment on the tax issues surrounding this potential deal and the Myer float.

Allan Blaikie, at the law firm Clayton Utz, questions how much the tax losses represent an incentive. He said the benefits could roughly translate into $600 million depending on whether the bidders meet a change of control test and, failing that, a same business test.

Key points

ATO tipped to look at potential buyout after controversial department store IPO in 2009.

Winemaker's $2 billion losses a magnet for private equity buyers as a capital gains offset device.

Am I the only person utterly dumbfounded by the fact that private equity sharks Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and Rhone Capital – the two suitors circling Treasury Wine Estates – have engaged former beverage and bottle purveyor Terry Davis as an adviser?

She's got to figure out what to do with a half-baked beer play, her massive (and massively cheesed off) supermarket customers and a ridiculously rampant syrup supplier (and major shareholder), The Coca-Cola Company in Atlanta – who under Davis had a 13-year run of the pen.

But that's just the amuse-bouche! Let us not forget that TWE's existential crisis is rooted in the $1.5 billion decision in August 2000 (by its former self, Foster's, now divided between Treasury and the beer arm owned by SABMiller) to buy American wine business, Beringer. Last year, TWE chief David Dearie copped a bullet for disastrous inventory management in its US (read: Beringer) division.

Well, knock me down with a feather – when Foster's bought into American plonk, Davis was boss of Foster's wine division! Here's what he said to the ASX at the time: "This combination of two great wine companies creates the world's first truly global premium wine company, and [Foster's owned] Mildara Blass and Beringer Wine Estates are pleased to be first movers in this regard."

A year later he was pumping up the tyres on his shiny new asset: "When you're looking at a family-owned company sometimes you don't know what you're buying… that's why we're so keen on keeping Wolf Blass involved with our business, especially with overseas customers. Beringer has been run as a corporate business since 1982 … so they've had several years to get their systems and procedures in place."

Funny then that Blass himself told this newspaper on Wednesday that the Beringer acquisition had been "a nightmare from day one".

Terry Davis, take a bow! Or maybe pass him a stiff drink?

Now Dearie's successor, Michael Clarke, is boganising one of Australia's most valuable brands, Penfolds (and co-conspirator Vintec), in a desperate effort to move stock and fatten his drunken cow for a market day that, God forbid, yet again, may not even come.

Wolf Blass urges US sale as Treasury's fortunes wither on the vine

Newspaper August 13, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Sarah Thompson Section: Companies and Markets 793 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
Former Coca-Cola Amatil chief executive Terry Davis has joined forces with private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts in its $3.4 billion tilt for Treasury Wine Estates, the owner of Penfolds, Rosemount and Wolf Blass.

Mr Davis is understood to be part of the KKR and Rhone Capital buyout bid now, after initially being sounded out for his expertise by rival private equity firm Carlyle Group earlier in the ­process.

Carlyle has yet to declare its hand but is understood to be working on a potential buyout offer of its own, and may soon become the third bidder to enter the field of prospective buyers for Treasury.

The involvement of Mr Davis behind the scenes with the KKR/Rhone Capital buyout bid has been mentioned in high-level financing briefings by Macquarie, which may be involved in the financing of the KKR/Rhone offer, sources said.

KKR and Rhone have their financing package all but signed up subject to satisfactory due diligence, with firms including Credit Suisse, Bank of America Merrill Lynch and Nomura all part of the syndicate.

KKR and Rhone are structuring the deal with a lower-than usual debt component for a private equity deal with debt of around $1.4 billion and equity of $2 billion.

Mr Davis ended a 13-year stint at the helm of soft drinks maker Coca-Cola Amatil earlier this year.

His name was mentioned in market speculation as a potential chief executive of Treasury in late 2013, when Treasury embarked on a global search for a new boss after the board axed former chief executive David Dearie.

Mr Dearie was sacked after an embarrassing write-down of $160 million in mid-2013 mainly related to the troubled United States business.

The job of chief executive of Treasury ultimately went to former British food industry executive Mike Clarke, who started on March 31 and has quickly formulated plans to try to revive Treasury's fortunes.

They centre on stepping up investment in the marketing behind high-end brands like Penfolds and Wynns and stripping out back-office costs.

Also on Tuesday, Wolf Blass, the man behind one of Treasury's most successful international brands, said whoever ends up buying the company needs to quickly sell off the ailing US operations because it has been a "nightmare from day one''.

Mr Blass, who is still part of the Treasury stable as an "ambassador'' for the Wolf Blass brand, said he has been telling the board for years it needed to sell the US business because it was such a drain on return on investment, but it refused to listen.

"America has been a nightmare from day one,'' Mr Blass said.

He also said if either private equity bidder, Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, or TPG Capital, gains control of Treasury, they should do likewise and offload the US business.

But he warns that any move by new private equity owners to make short-term decisions with the broader ­Treasury business would have a ­harmful impact on the entire Australian wine industry.

"That could be harmful if they're in it for a fast buck,'' he said.

TPG Capital emerged as a second bidder for Treasury on Monday with a proposal pitched at $5.20 a share, valuing the company at $3.4 billion.

This was the same as a buyout ­proposal lodged by KKR/Rhone a week earlier.

Mr Blass is a pioneer of the Austra­lian wine industry and listed his eponymous company on the stock exchange in the early 1980s before it merged with another company to form Mildara Blass.

Mildara Blass was then bought out by beer company Foster's Group in 1996 in its first entry into the wine industry.

Mr Blass is 79 and said he is "firing on all cylinders". He travels regularly for Treasury to promote the Wolf Blass brand, which generates 70 per cent of its sales overseas.

He said the strategy by new Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke to trim the brand portfolio from the current 83 brands is smart, because it is too many for all the brands to thrive.

"It's a lunatic situation,'' Mr Blass said.

But he is concerned that the work under way to trim the portfolio might be disrupted by the due diligence process as the US predators scrutinise the inner workings of the company.

"It is interrupting how to split them up and where to go from here,'' he said.

Mr Blass said he cautioned the ­Foster's board against buying Beringer in the United States for $2.9 billion in 2000.

"This is going to be the greatest disaster you've ever made,'' Mr Blass warned them.

He said he doesn't own any Treasury shares, and the biggest mistake that Foster's and Treasury have made in the United States is not having strong ­Australian management running that business.

He has high regard for the abilities of Mr Davis, and the private equity bidders will benefit from his expertise.

Mr Davis was managing director of Foster's wine operations when it bought Beringer in California in 2000.

The purchase has since proven to be a major reason for Treasury's fall from grace.

Former Penfolds boss slates Treasury chiefs - Exclusive

Newspaper August 11, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 699 Words
Page: 13
OpenURL Link
John Spalvins, one of Australia's best-known corporate raiders from the 1980s, whose company once owned the famous Penfolds wine brand for 10 years, has launched a blistering attack against the management of Treasury Wine Estates, and Foster's Group before it. Mr Spalvins blamed poor management of Penfolds and its stablemates over the past decade and boards of directors who, he says, "did not adequately understand the wine industry" for the situation where Penfolds appears likely to fall into foreign hands.

"From Australia's point of view it is regrettable to see ownership of major industries pass to foreign hands, particularly an icon such as Penfolds."

Mr Spalvins said Penfolds had a higher market share in Australia in the 1980s when it was under the control of his once-mighty Adelaide Steamship Co than it does now. United States private equity firms Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and junior partner Rhone Capital made an increased buyout bid for Treasury a week ago, valuing the company at $3.4 billion. Mr Spalvins said Penfolds' market share had diminished in Australia for one reason: "The short answer is management."

Mr Spalvins's strident views about Australian's wine industry, and in particular the management of Penfolds, will no doubt draw criticism themselves because of the way in Mr Spalvins's own company collapsed in 1991 under debt levels of $7 billion. Out of the ashes of Adelaide Steamship, which at the time included Woolworths, David Jones and a string of large food brands, some of Australia's strongest businesses have emerged. Woolworths has grown into a supermarket giant with a market capitalisation of $45 billion.

Mr Spalvins was also scathing about the attempts by Foster's Group to manage such a large stable of wine brands prior to Treasury splitting off as a stand-alone wine company in mid-2011.

Having Penfolds sitting alongside a string of other big wine brands such as Rosemount, Lindemans, Wolf Blass, Seppelt and Saltram made it very difficult, and there was an "inability to manage a large number of brands".Expensive and disastrous

Mr Spalvins said the 2000 acquisition of Beringer Wine Estates in California was expensive and disastrous.

He said both Foster's and Treasury appeared to have boards of directors "who did not adequately understand the wine industry". It was a tough business in which to make profits consistently because of the cyclical factors involved in an agricultural industry, but adequate returns would come from well-managed businesses.

"Without a doubt the industry has a large cyclical factor and investment of capital must be carefully monitored, but adequate returns can be earned if marketing and promotion of brands is correctly handled and capital not wasted," he said.

Mr Spalvins, who does not own any Treasury shares, said there were many examples of premium wine companies in the United States delivering solid returns. He said Treasury, because of its size, had the advantage of large-scale production and marketing, but had failed to earn adequate returns.

KKR and Rhone have offered $5.20 a share for Treasury, up from a $4.70-a-share proposal made by KKR alone in mid-April. "Purely from a financial point of view the shareholders should accept; the track record of current management does not justify a higher price," Mr Spalvins said.

He was speaking from the United States, where he is travelling. He is 78 and lives in the affluent Adelaide suburb of Springfield, and said he still gains pleasure seeing the high-end Penfolds wines on wine lists around the globe.

He said interest rates of 18 per cent and higher in the late 1980s that brought the highly leveraged Adelaide Steamship empire down were ultimately too hard to manage. Australia's big conglomerates now, such as Wesfarmers, were doing a fine job, but it was easier to manage debt levels with current interest rates of 5 per cent or so.

Mr Spalvins said he does look back with frustration at the strong businesses inside Adelaide Steamship that have gone on to be valued very highly by the sharemarket, and the inability of the company to hold them all together.

"Yes, it is frustrating to think of what could have been," he said.

Adelaide Steamship took ownership of Penfolds in 1980 when it bought the broader brewing business of Tooth & Co, but sold it to SA Brewing in 1990 as part of a drawn asset-sale program forced on it by bankers.

Treasury Wine's best hope may rest in private hands

Newspaper August 9, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 821 Words
Page: 37
OpenURL Link
Equity It may be time for the winemaker to free itself from the pressures of the sharemarket.

The Australian executive who once ran the world's biggest wine business has clear views on the best prospects for the resurrection of Penfolds owner ­Treasury Wine Estates: shareholders should take the money and run.

Stephen Millar, who ran the global wine operations of Constellation Brands for three years until 2006, says being a public company isn't the right place for Treasury in its current state.

He says it should hand over the keys to the United States private equity firms led by KKR & Co, who've made an enhanced $3.4 billion ­takeover bid and let them fix it up, away from the ­spotlight of being listed on the Australian Securities Exchange. "There is no doubt that if a business needs strategic changes, resurrecting, and has some challenges, then it is harder because you're needing to ­satisfy the institutional investors all the time," he says.

That is the same argument KKR and its junior partner Rhone Capital are mounting. They are trying to convince the Treasury board to hand over ­ownership of the business, which owns 83 wine brands led by Penfolds, ­Rosemount, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Saltram and Wynns. KKR and Rhone have offered $5.20 a share for Treasury, up from KKR's initial solo proposal of mid-April of $4.70 a share.

Millar emphasises he owns no Treasury shares himself and has no axe to grind, despite having been a fierce competitors to Penfolds and its­ ­stablemates for more than a decade when he ran BRL Hardy until 2003. BRL Hardy's brands including Hardys, Leasingham, Houghton and Brookland Valley are now owned by another private equity firm, CHAMP, and the company is now known as Accolade Wines. Constellation still holds 20 per cent, but itself had a rough time after the buy out in delivering good returns as the Australian dollar rose sharply, making it hard for wine exports from Australia.State of business

Timing is everything. BRL Hardy was the poster child of the Australian wine industry in its halcyon days, with Mr Millar taking it from a $100 million company in 1992 to a $1.9 billion buyout bonanza for shareholders in 2003.

"The timing was right," he says.

He says BRL Hardy needed capital to grow and public funding was the best source at the time. But Treasury is in a far different position, having been through a string of owners with different strategies who've been trying to use its size and clout to make headway.

KKR and Rhone, who has a ­co-founder in Robert Agostinelli, who once tried to buy out the Liverpool ­Football Club in England's Premier League soccer competition in 2010, want to overhaul the Treasury business and argue it will be best done without ­having to deliver half-year and full-year profit expectations without a slip-up.

"A lot depends on the state of the business," Millar says.

There were 20 ASX-listed wine ­companies and wine investment funds in the late 1990s and early 2000s but the public company funding model is now largely out of favour in the industry, with few remaining.

Investments take a long time to bring payback because of the long lead times in an agricultural industry. Under ­private ownership "you will get time for that to pay off", Millar says.

KKR and Rhone will be paying ­special attention to the US operations of Treasury over the next few weeks as they undertake due diligence on the inner workings of the business.

It is the US where Treasury and its previous owner, Foster's Group, have blown $2 billion of shareholders funds over more than a decade.

Foster's bought the Beringer wine business for $2.9 billion in 2000 in a grand ­expansion plan, but analysts now believe the US operations are worth only about $1 billion.Management is key

KKR and Rhone and their advisers will be having long chats with the likes of Southern Wine and Spirits, the ­largest wine and spirits distributor in the US which operates in 35 States. The Miami-based group was one of those left with mountains of cheap bottled wine from Treasury's US wineries in its ­warehouses when Treasury miscalculated its supply and demand. It led to the ­infamous decision in mid-2013 by Treasury to destroy 6 million bottles of wine from warehouses across the country to rid itself of the excess supply.

Treasury was split off from Foster's as a stand-alone wine company in May, 2011 and on its first day of trading on the ASX its share price was $3.45. It had fluctuated, but by May, 2013 it had soared to $6.47. It had always been earmarked as a takeover target but a series of mishaps in the US mainly, caused the share price to plunge and opened the door for offshore raiders such as KKR.

Millar says it is important for any new owner to get the strategy right, and being owned by offshore companies won't be an issue.

"It really doesn't matter whether the owners are American, English or French, it's the quality of the management and getting those strategic decisions right. Management is the key".

Retailers warn Treasury suitors: don't mess with prices

Newspaper August 7, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 672 Words
Page: 23
OpenURL Link
Liquor retailers say the prospect of Treasury Wine Estates' portfolio of 80-plus brands being owned by an ­overseas company is unlikely to produce a customer backlash, unless price rises are pursued by new private equity owners.

Dean Taylor, the chief executive of Cracka Wines, one of Australia's biggest online wine retailers, said on Wednesday the portfolio of brands owned by Treasury including Penfolds, Rosemounts, Wynns, Saltram and Wolf Blass, had been through so many ownership changes in the past two decades that another one won't change customer perceptions.

But what customers would be closely monitoring would be any attempt to lift prices, or change the quality. "That would upset them,'' Mr Taylor said. "There is an element of our customer base who would care about foreign ownership but for most people it would be any change in quality or price that is the major factor.''

Mr Taylor said while the Treasury brands had a large presence in the Australian market, there was little evidence that customers were truly passionate about most of them.

"There's aren't a lot of customers that are brand advocates,'' he said.

United States private equity firms Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and junior partner Rhone Capital made a higher buyout proposal for Treasury on ­Monday pitched at $5.20 a share, which valued the company at $3.4 billion. This was 50¢ higher than a solo ­proposal made by KKR in mid-April at $4.70 a share.

The Treasury board has agreed to let the duo in to undertake due diligence on a "non-exclusive'' basis, which leaves the door open for any rival bidders.

One of the brands which new Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke is trying to super-charge by putting much more marketing spending behind it is the Wynns brand from the Coonawarra district in south-east South Australia.

Mr Taylor said he agreed with the attempt to lift Wynns higher, but pointed to a period in the early 2000s after a merger between former owner Southcorp and the Rosemount company that was then owned by the Oatley family, when Wynns was heavily discounted and the brand damaged.

"Wynns probably deserves to sit a lot higher in the hierarchy,'' he said.Customers indifferent

David Reberger, the general manager of Kemenys, which runs a substantial online business along with a large retail outlet in Bondi, said he didn't think customers would be too concerned about famous wine brands like Penfolds being owned by US-based private equity firms.

"I believe that customers are indifferent,'' he said.

They tend to follow individual brands and aren't generally perturbed about the ultimate owner of the company, he said.

Mr Reberger said Treasury and its previous incarnation, the wine division of Foster's Group, had pushed too hard in driving up the price of the Wynns portfolio over the past five years or so.

"There's only so far you can push it in Australia," he said.

But he said the Wynns brand does have much more potential in offshore markets, and perhaps hadn't been marketed as well as it could have been.

"I think that's a brand that's got a lot more potential,'' he said.

Cameron Kidd, the owner of Rathdowne Cellars in North Carlton in Melbourne, said company ownership was largely irrelevant in the purchasing decision by a buyer, with quality and value at the forefront. He believed a change in ownership wouldn't filter down to the average wine buyer.

"I would say that most of our customers don't care which company owns a brand,'' he said.

But there were exceptions. There had been a major backlash against the Tamar Ridge brand in the late 2000s when it was still owned by timber company Gunns, which was in the spotlight over environmental issues in Tasmania related to a proposed pulp mill.

"There was a huge backlash,'' Mr Kidd said.

Tamar Ridge was sold by Gunns in 2010 to Victorian company Brown Brothers.

United States drinks giant Constellation Brands, which still owns 20 per cent of Accolade Wines, the local firm which owns brands such as Hardys and Leasingham, is considered unlikely to mount a rival bid for Treasury.

Constellation's vice-president of communications, Cheryl Gossin, said from New York the company "does not comment on rumour or speculation''.

Patent puts a plug in Penfolds sales

Newspaper August 6, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Angus Grigg AFR correspondent Section: Companies and Markets 529 Words
Page: 16
OpenURL Link
Shanghai | Treasury Wine Estates' ­top-selling Penfolds brand has been removed from all InterContinental Hotels in China, amid fears those selling the wine could be liable for damages in a long-running trademark dispute.

The removal is a blow to the takeover target's China strategy and raises fears other large hotel chains, supermarkets and online stores may follow in ­suspending sales of Australia's best known wine.

InterContinental, which has 214 hotels across China, removed ­Penfolds from its wine lists last week citing legal advice.

"I would ask you to pull your ­Penfolds branded wines off your menus until further notice," Tim ­Stanhope, the director of food and ­beverage operations, said in an email to managers on July 28. "We don't want to be involved in any further law actions."

The Australian Financial Review reported on July 13 that Penfolds had failed to register its Chinese name and was now locked in a legal battle with a notorious trademark squatter to reclaim it. Penfolds said it had won an initial court case against the man, but the ruling was being appealed and the matter ongoing.

Penfolds is up against the same squatter who won a record 33.73 million yuan ($5.8 million) from French wine giant Castel, after he ­registered its Chinese name.

In a phone conversation, Mr ­Stanhope confirmed InterContinental had stopped selling Penfolds wine and said the company was being cautious.

An industry source estimated the InterContinental contract could be worth around 5000 cases annually to Penfolds or around 5 per cent of its total shipments to China.

"Do you want to take the risk of being sued or do you just find another brand to sell? Lets be frank, there are plenty of other brands," said the wine ­industry source.

The InterContinental decision comes amid a deteriorating business environment for foreign companies in China, who are increasingly being ­targeted by regulators and the state controlled media.

In recent months, United States tech firms Microsoft and Qualcomm have come under investigation for ­anti-competitive behaviour, while Apple and Starbucks have been singled out for heavy criticism in the media.

In this environment, many foreign ­business are being overly cautious.

In his email, Mr Stanhope said under Chinese law outlets which sold goods found to have infringed a trademark were also considered to be have broken the law. "Therefore in order to play it safe, it's better that hotels stop selling the wine until there is a court ­judgement announced," the email said.

This could be months if not years away as Treasury began legal action to reclaim its Chinese name, Ben Fu, in 2011.

Treasury representatives were not available for comment.

A search of China's trademarks office found three variations of ­Penfolds' Chinese name, which loosely translates to "chasing prosperity", have been registered. The last of these was approved in December 2013, more than two years after Treasury began legal action to reclaim the name.

The trademarks are held by Li Shen and Li Daozhi, both from the coastal city of Wenzhou and are thought to be related. Li Daozhi won the record­ ­trademark claim against Castel in August last year after registering the French giants Chinese name, Kasite.

He now markets his own wine under the Kasite brand in China in direct ­competition to the French wine maker.

KKR borrows $1.4b for Treasury bid

Newspaper August 6, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 604 Words
Page: 16
OpenURL Link
United States private equity firms ­Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and Rhone Capital have a debt package worth about $1.4 billion all but finalised for the proposed $3.4 billion buyout of ­Treasury Wine Estates. They will run the company with lower gearing than in normal private equity investments.

The two firms also have teams primed to spend most of their due ­diligence period on the troublesome United States operations of Treasury because they regard this business as the biggest potential swing factor in ­formulating a final, binding proposal.

KKR and Rhone are thought to have signed agreements with several firms for the debt package, including Credit Suisse, Nomura and Bank of America Merrill Lynch. They are all predicated on satisfactory due diligence. KKR and Rhone plan to use a ­conservative ­gearing ratio compared with traditional private equity ­investments because wine is an agricultural ­industry subject to seasonal ­conditions and fluctuating earnings.

The debt component is thought to be about 40 per cent. The introduction of New York-based Rhone Capital into the bidding vehicle with an intended 15 to 20 per cent Treasury stake if the offer is successful, has reduced the level of equity required by KKR, but combined, it will be about $2 billion.

Treasury, which owns brands including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, ­Rosemount, Lindemans, and Saltram, got a higher buyout offer from KKR and Rhone on Monday, pitched at $5.20 a share. It was 50¢ higher than a previous offer from KKR in mid-April of $4.70.

Treasury shares jumped more than 4 per cent on Monday after the higher bid was revealed and gained a further 2¢ on Tuesday to close at $5.17.

Advisers and lawyers for both sides are close to finalising a confidential agreement to allow KKR and Rhone to begin due diligence. They are likely to get access to the inner workings of the company later this week.

The Beringer Vineyards operations in California and the broader United States operations are a major focus for KKR and Rhone, given the major ­blunders at Treasury have been in the US, including the infamous ­mis-reading of the market in 2013 which resulted in overflowing stock ­levels in ­third-party US distributor warehouses of cheaper commercial wine. This resulted in the destruction of 6 million bottles of wine.

Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke was in his Melbourne head office on Tuesday working on his own strategic plans. China's Bright Foods has ruled itself out as a Treasury buyer. US drinks giant Constellation Brands is an unlikely rival bidder after chief ­executive Robert Sands signalled in July when the company released its quarterly results, that the focus was on paying debt and not large acquisitions.

It comes as debate rages about the potential impact of a change in ­ownership of some of Australia's most prestigious wine brands to private equity firms. The private equity model ­generally revolves around owning a business for between five to seven years, before it is sold again in either a public float or a trade sale.

Mitchell Taylor, the chairman of Australia's First Families of Wines, an umbrella body of 12 family-owned wine companies with long histories, said the wine industry needed long-term ­planning and decisions that may not pay off for a decade.

"In the wine game I don't think it's good for the industry if there's that short-term quick-buck, in-and-out mentality. You do need that patient capital," he said.

Mr Taylor is managing director of Taylors Wines, based in Clare Valley, South Australia. It belongs to Australia's First Families of Wine which also include companies such as Brown Brothers, Henschke, Yalumba and ­Tyrrell's Wines. He said the family-owned groups would invest in the business, knowing the benefit may not flow fully until the next generation took over.

"We're looking at generational investments and decisions."

The Australian food businesses owned by Chinese company Bright Food Group are set to form a large part of its initial public offering on the Hong Kong stock exchange, but the company has ruled out making a rival bid for ­Treasury Wine Estates to expand the operations further.

Last month, Bright Food said it was prepared to spend as much as $2 billion on acquisitions in China and around the world.

But chairman Lv Yongjie said the company was not interested in deals that were "too small".

TWE forced to engage as KKR ups bid - Smith

Newspaper August 5, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Michael Smith Section: Companies and Markets 510 Words
Page: 34
OpenURL Link
There is a good chance Australia's most prominent wine brand, Penfolds, will be in foreign hands by year end. Treasury Wine Estates has granted Kohlberg Kravis Roberts (KKR) access to its books after rebuffing the private equity giant's initial offer in April.

TWE's board is still not wild about the prospect of a takeover bid but at $5.20 per share it had little choice but to engage. The tone of TWE's statement to the market on Monday says a lot. The company spends three paragraphs reiterating the current strategy under chief executive Michael Clarke to breathe life back into the company by ramping up marketing, improving costs and building its iconic Penfolds brand.

While Clarke's strategy has been received favourably in some quarters, KKR does not necessarily share their enthusiasm. While moving without waiting to see the company's full-year results later this month gives it first-mover advantage, another theory is that KKR wanted to get in early and head off any potential damage from the change in strategy.

Clarke has made some big changes, including moving the release dates for Penfolds wines. A discounted $650 wine fridge with every six bottles of Penfolds wine has also been the centrepiece of a marketing blitz to boost sales which should enable the company to meet forecasts.

The assumption is the company has avoided another earnings downgrade for now, or else it would have been obligated to announce something on Monday. But KKR has its own ideas about how to fix the company in the longer term and that will involve bringing in its own internal consultants to stress test the business and inject more capital where required.

KKR and partner Rhone Capital have upped their initial offer by 10.6 per cent and it is now a 40.9 per cent premium to where TWE was trading before the first approach was made in April. A non-exclusive clause means TWE has not closed the door on rival bidders coming in at a higher price, although there are no signs so far of a white knight. The $5.20 a share offer is also more in line with higher multiples being paid for wine assets due to their strategic value. The median of the past 24 wine transactions is 13.2 times trailing EBITDA, which is a fraction below the $5.20 per share, according to analysts.

TWE has been a consistent target of takeover speculation since it was spun out of Foster's in 2011 but there is a sense of inevitability it will be taken out this time round. The question then is will KKR break-up the company? Spinning off Penfolds, the jewel in the crown, would make sense as that is where most of the company's value lies. There would be no shortage of TWE's US operations either.

But it is understood the current interest is in TWE's entire portfolio and there are no plans to break up the company. KKR's track record shows it would also seek to drive more value out of the Penfolds brand before looking to hive anything off. KKR will be hoping for a repeat of the returns it made on South Korea's Oriental Brewery, which it acquired in 2009 with partner Affinity Equity.

Author: Sally Rose Section: Market Wrap 564 Words

OpenURL Link
A negative mood in global equity ­markets saw Australian shares post a modest fall, despite the release of some better than expected retail sales data.

The benchmark S&P/ASX 200 Index and the broader All Ordinaries Index each fell 0.3 per cent on Monday to 5540.9 points and 5533.3 points ­respectively. While the market overall was weak, merger and acquisition activity gave some stocks, notably Treasury Wine Estates, a boost.

Local shares fell as much as 0.5 per cent in early trade after the United States' S&P 500 Index and Dow Jones Industrial Average declined, despite the release of a solid jobs report on ­Friday night.

"Australian shares trended lower as investors reacted to further falls in US and European bourses on Friday. ­Volumes were on the light side, affected by Monday's NSW bank holiday," Patersons Securities chief investment strategist Tony Farnham said.

Asian markets provided mixed cues in the afternoon session. When the ASX closed Hong Kong's Hang Seng and China's Shanghai Composite Index were both stronger while Japan's Nikkei had slipped.

China's official non-manufacturing purchasing managers index on Sunday fell to a six-month low of 54.2 points in July, dragged down by a weaker property market.

In local economic news, Australian Bureau of Statistics data showed retail sales rose 0.6 per cent, seasonally adjusted, in June. The result reversed two months of declining sales amid weak consumer sentiment in the wake of the federal budget announcement and an unseasonably warm start to winter.Job advertisements rise

A survey by ANZ Bank showed total job advertisements rose 0.3 per cent in July. That followed June's 4.4 per cent increase and left ads up 4.2 per cent on July last year.

A private inflation gauge indicated price pressures are subsiding. The TD Securities-Melbourne Institute's monthly consumer price index edged up just 0.2 per cent in July against ­seasonal expectations for a much stronger rise.

The better than expected retail sales data helped the biggest food and liquor sellers outperform the market. Woolworths and Wesfarmers, owner of Coles, each edged up 0.1 per cent to $36.49 and $43.35 respectively.

Declines in the big four banks pulled the index lower. Commonwealth Bank of Australia shed 0.3 per cent to $82.58, while Westpac Banking Corporation dropped 0.6 per cent to $33.85. ANZ Banking Group lost 0.7 per cent to $33.34, and National Australia Bank fell 0.5 per cent to $34.77.

Exchange-traded options show promise

Newspaper August 5, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: John Wasiliev Section: Market Wrap 635 Words
Page: 28
OpenURL Link
Traders who consider derivative strategies during the reporting season will be closely monitoring the performance of prominent shares to ensure they deliver what is expected.

Where there is a good chance they will, says Ord Minnett senior derivatives adviser Wai-Yee Chen, this can present selected opportunities to generate extra yield through the medium of exchange-traded options.

One prominent share that could fit this bill, says Chen, is local retail giant Woolworths. With a better earnings expectation and a possible higher dividend when it reports its result on August 29, any investor who agrees with this outlook could consider selling an August $36.50 at the money put option for a 44¢ a share credit trade.

If exercised to buy the shares at any time during August, says Chen, an investor will be in line for an expected 73¢ fully franked dividend in September from shares that were bought 44¢ cheaper through the use of puts. At the same time any downside risk in this strategy can be managed by buying a $36.14 September expiry put option for an 85¢ cost as part of any overall strategy where the net of the two trades is a 41¢ per share debit.

This combined put spread trade, says Chen, has the scope to deliver several benefits to investors in the form of a chance of buying shares for $36.91 with the downside protected to $36.55 – which was around the current price Monday. Then there is the opportunity for an immediate 73¢ dividend, uncapped upside and a guaranteed exit price of $36.14.

While this week will sees BWP Trust and REA Group report, it will be much bigger next week with the Commonwealth Bank, Suncorp and ANZ reporting along with Telstra, Wesfarmers, JB Hi-Fi, Carsales.com and CSL Ltd.

The share action on Monday focused on defensive plays and a number of takeover stocks. CMC Markets chief strategist Michael McCarthy noted that Australian shares steadied Monday after a rocky start. The property sector gained the most as the new Scentre Group appeared to benefit from institutional re-weighting into its shares. Explorer and producer Roc Oil traded to four-year highs on news that it spurned Horizon's attempted "merger of equals" in favour of an all cash bid at 69¢ per share from China's Fosun International and Treasury Wine Estates that saw its shares surge after American private equity firm KKR lifted its takeover offer for the company that owns Penfolds, among a number of other wine brands.

This week, traders are likely to spend a bit of time digesting a deluge of data released on Tuesday, says IG Markets strategist Evan Lucas. This included stronger than expected retail sales, higher job advertisements and benign inflation signals which all looked fairly positive for the $A.

For the trading minded, gold attracted a fair share of comments from trading analysts with Matt Simpson from Thinkforex observing that it continues to whipsaw between key support and resistance levels, in many instances fooling investors into thinking a new trend is forming.

While this was making trading conditions problematic for those prepared to hold a strategy for longer than a day at a time, there may be a more bullish picture unfolding.

Since achieving its $US1345 high price, gold has declined lower, although each swing has become increasingly smaller as the price seemed to be coiling up into a potential bullish wedge pattern.

The problem with gold is timing the bottom of such a pattern.

Simpson reckons it would be safer to wait for a break above a key swing high such as $US1312 before assuming the corrective pattern has ended.

Another gold commentator, OANDA technical specialist Stuart McPhee, noted that while gold eased lower last week and put pressure on the $US1290 support level, it rallied to start the new week above this level. McPhee also observed that the gold position of his firm's clients was 70 per cent long showing a change to a more bullish sentiment despite the price down trend.
