Treasury sets the pace for $3.4b offer - M&A Kohlberg Kravis Roberts accelerates due diligence process

Newspaper August 5, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Sarah Thompson Section: Companies and Markets 703 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
The board of Treasury Wine Estates is demanding private equity suitors Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and its junior ­partner Rhone Capital accelerate their due diligence process to minimise ­disruption as the maker of Penfolds weighs an increased $3.4 billion ­takeover offer.

KKR, which made an initial ­confidential approach to Treasury on April 16 at $4.70 per share which only became public several weeks later, has increased its offer to $5.20 per share which the Treasury board deemed high enough to start engaging in talks with KKR.

The Treasury board, led by chairman Paul Rayner, will let KKR and Rhone Capital inside the company to obtain further detailed information. However, that offer is on a "non-exclusive'' due diligence basis which opens the door for other potential bidders to now make their move.

Treasury is pushing for KKR/Rhone to conduct a quick due diligence period of between four to six weeks to minimise the risk of distraction and ­destabilisation, according to sources.

Treasury shares jumped more than 4 per cent on Monday but didn't ­manage to reach the $5.20 mark, hitting an intra-day high of $5.18, before closing at $5.15, up 20 cents for the day.

This indicates there is some doubt amongst investors that a final offer might be agreed.

It also reflects the market view that a rival bidder may not emerge.

KKR made its first Treasury buyout proposal in April alone and has now brought in a junior partner, fellow United States private equity firm Rhone Capital.

It is likely to have a stake of between 15 to 20 per cent in Treasury if the offer is successful.

The main asset they are after is the Penfolds brand, which makes around 75 per cent of Treasury's profits.

The higher bid was first revealed on afr.com on Sunday night.

The new offer for the maker of ­Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Rosemount and Wynns, continues the ­rollercoaster ride for Treasury, which has suffered a string of heavy writedowns mainly linked to its ­underperforming United States wine operations.

New chief executive Mike Clarke, a former food executive in the United Kingdom, started at the helm of Treasury on March 31.

He has outlined his own plans to revive the company including heavy investment in the top-end brands, a vigorous cost-cutting program and separating out the high-end brands such as Penfolds internally to set them apart from the mass-market brands.

An internal note sent by Mr Clarke to the company's 3500 staff on Monday urged them not to be distracted by the higher bid, and to get on with the job.

"The most important thing you can do over the coming weeks is to focus on the delivery of our FY15 plans and ensure speculation about TWE's ownership does not distract you from making, marketing and selling our fabulous wines,'' Mr Clarke said in his note to staff.

The Treasury board said if a binding offer eventuated, it would assess whether the KKR/Rhone offer would deliver better value to the company's 73,000 shareholders than Mr Clarke's own strategic plan.

Should KKR/Rhone be successful with its $5.20 per share offer, which is at a 40.9 per cent premium to the $3.69 per share price at which Treasury was trading when KKR made its first buyout proposal, it will mean the Penfolds brand will have its seventh corporate owner since 1976.

Jason Beddow, the managing director of Argo Investments, which has a share portfolio worth $5 billion, said on Monday his firm deliberately steers clear of investing in wine companies. "It's been a hard place to make money,'' Mr Beddow said.

Argo was previously an investor in Foster's Group when it had the former Treasury business in its portfolio. Treasury split off from Foster's in mid-2011 but came to grief in mid-2013 when it suffered $160 million in writedowns in the US and needed to destroy 6 million bottles of cheap bottled wine.

This embarrassment caused the Treasury board to axe then chief executive David Dearie.

In the late 1990s and early 2000s, almost 20 different wine companies and wine investment funds were listed on the ASX.

In the six years leading up to 2005 most disappeared through takeover, financial collapse or being wound up because of poor returns.

The wine industry is very capital intensive and subject to both agricultural fluctuations and changing tastes of consumers for different wine varieties.

High-flying US billionaire out to improve his cellar

Robert Agostinelli, one of the principals of Rhone Group, which has linked up with Kohlberg Kravis Roberts to make a higher bid for Penfolds owner Treasury Wine Estates, has always aimed high.

Mr Agostinelli, a US billionaire who set up the Rhone ­private equity firm with Steven ­Langman in the mid-1990s, once tried to buy Liverpool Football Club in Britain when it was in financial distress in 2010.

The flamboyant 61-year-old moves in distinguished circles and is friends with former presidents George Bush and Nicolas Sarkozy.

He also has an extensive wine ­collection and has long been familiar with the global prestige of the Penfolds brand. Rhone also holds a stake in Quiksilver, the Australian ­surfwear brand headquartered in California.

Mr Agostinelli has always had an eye for a deal and even though based in New York, he has strong connections in London, built when he worked in mergers and acquisitions for Goldman Sachs in the 1980s before ­joining Lazard. Goldman Sachs in ­Australia has been bunkered down with Treasury Wine Estates in defending it from offers.

KKR is still very much the driver in the increased bid for Treasury revealed on Monday morning, which values the wine group at $3.4 billion, with Rhone the junior partner. Based on the size of the respective funds, it is likely Rhone will end up with a stake of 15 to 20 per cent, with KKR's investment funds holding the bulk of Treasury. It is believed final negotiations about the make up of the respective stakes in the bidding vehicle are still being finalised.Liverpool bid

Rhone Capital's tilt for a 40 per cent chunk of Liverpool FC in 2010 brought Mr Agostinelli fully into the public ­spotlight in the northern hemisphere.

Liverpool was one of the most successful football clubs in Britain's P­remier League. It's finances were in disarray after debt levels got too high and Mr Agostinelli and his Rhone ­colleagues made a buyout offer for 40 per cent of the club.

After months of intrigue and counter proposals, Liverpool directors put the club up for sale. It was bought by Fenway Sports Group. Liverpool FC is among the world's biggest sporting clubs brands, say global experts such as US business publication Forbes, but it sits below fellow UK Premier League club Manchester United, and Spanish club Real Madrid in the value stakes.

Mr Langman also once worked at Lazard before he and Mr Agostinelli established Rhone.

Mr Agostinelli has also been in the spotlight in Britain's papers over a court battle with his former fiancee, ­Charlotte Lucas, as he used legal avenues to demand the return of an expensive engagement ring he bought her in 2007. The legal battle hit the headlines earlier this year after their six-year ­relationship ended.

A successful takeover of Treasury in conjunction with KKR would mark the first direct investment in Australia by Rhone, although it owns companies with headquarters in overseas ­countries that have local operations in Australia, such as Quiksilver.

It has also just sold out of an investment in the global cosmetics, perfume and beauty products company Coty.

Treasury wine promo pays off

Newspaper July 29, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 492 Words
Page: 21
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke's high-stakes gamble on the biggest promotional drive in the history of the company appears to have paid off, as he pushes to evade a potential takeover by private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts.

An avalanche of buyers have scooped up a discounted $650 wine fridge by buying six bottles of high-end Penfolds wines after Mr Clarke launched an aggressive advertising blitz for the month of July, which will give Penfolds sales a fast start to the new financial year.

Under the offer, if drinkers bought six or more bottles of high-end Penfolds wine from a retailer, they could buy a 30-bottle Vintec temperature-controlled wine cabinet for $200 plus $55 in delivery charges, instead of the usual price of $650.

It is understood that up to 12,000 of the Vintec wine cabinets will be delivered to wine buyers across Australia who have already taken advantage of the promotion and bought at least six bottles of the Penfolds Bin series of luxury wines by July 30.

The high volume of Vintec cabinets taken up by Penfolds wine buyers under the offer in July mean that Vintec will supply to the Penfolds promotion the same number of cabinets and wine cellar units it normally sells in an entire year. The promotion has been heavily subsidised by Penfolds, which has partnered with Vintec, Australia's market leader in temperature-controlled wine cabinets.

Penfolds generates about 75 per cent of total profits at Treasury, which three months ago received a $3.1 billion buyout proposal from KKR.

Mr Clarke, who began as chief executive with Treasury on March 31, a few weeks ago outlined a new strategy for Penfolds and labelled the wine cabinet promotion the biggest consumer-facing promotion ever undertaken by the company.

Liquor retailers say there has been brisk foot traffic and a high take-up of the offer, while Vintec itself says it has been highly successful.Successful strategy

Vintec group marketing manager Thomas Benhamou declined to comment on specific numbers but said demand was very strong.

"I can tell you it's been very, very successful,'' he said.

"We've experienced very good demand.''

Woolworths superstore liquor chain, Dan Murphy's, has been experiencing strong demand from Penfolds buyers taking up the offer, according to Campbell Stott, merchandise manager for Dan Murphy's.

"We've seen a pretty good level of demand,'' he said. "I think it's been a good promotional mechanic for them.''

KKR pitched its buyout proposal at $4.70 per share for Treasury, which also makes the brands Wolf Blass, Rosemount, Lindemans, Wynns and Seppelt.

Treasury shares closed on Friday at $4.99.

Under Mr Clarke's shift in strategy, the company will release its new range of high-end wines, including the flagship Penfolds Grange, in October each year, away from the traditional release dates of March to May which has been the custom for the past 20 years.

Key points Biggest promotional drive in company's history looks to be hugely successful. Shares closed on Friday at $4.99 per share compared with the KKR bid of $4.70.

Treasury targets luxury sales - Wine - Iconic labels to be pitched to world’s travellers

Newspaper July 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Eli Greenblat Section: Companies and Markets 447 Words

OpenURL Link
Takeover target Treasury Wine Estates has kicked off an expansion into the $US39 billion ($41.5 billion) global travel retail market to ensure its iconic wines are not left out of luxury buying decisions at the world's newest and most ritziest airports, duty-free shops and cruise liners.

The renewed pitch to the travelling public comes amid a plethora of new airport expansions and openings, especially in the Middle East and Asia.The massive lounges and upmarket shopping precincts are increasingly focused on luxury goods.

Treasury Wine believes the wine ­category is very underdeveloped at ­airports, lagging behind the spirits ­sector which dominates 70 per cent of all ­alcohol sales, and believes its icon winessuch as Penfolds and Wolf Blass can find their way into the shopping bags of high spending global travellers – especially from China.

After last year's deal with Nuance Group – one of the world's biggest travel retailers – to open a maiden standalone Penfolds store at Sydney Airport, Treasury Wine has appointed Daevid Warren to head up its Middle East and Africa business. He will be based in Dubai where he will also lead the group's global travel retail operations.

Mr Warren will use his 20 years of experience in the retail sector, including stints with World Duty Free and spirits group Bacardi Martini, to ensure the winemaker's leading brands are front and centre at airport lounges, bars and duty-free shops, and on luxury cruise ships. Treasury Wine also appointed two more executives within its global travel retail sales and marketing teams to support the company's lofty earnings ambitions.

The general retail sector continues to flat-line amid tough conditions and anxious consumers, but the $US39 billion global travel industry is in a strong growth trajectory backed by 4 per cent plus international passenger growth rates (6 per cent plus in Asia), the emergence of low-cost carriers and continued strong investment in new airport terminals with larger retail space.

But while perfume and cosmetics has traditionally driven and dominated airport retail sales, wine has been left behind and is hugely underdeveloped.

Wine sales make up 48 per cent of domestic liquor sales but only 21 per cent of the global travel retail ­sector, with spirits taking the lion's share of sales. The current prize is 6.8 million cases a year of wine sold in airport stores, and although Europe is responsible for the bulk of volumes, Asia is forecast to grow at stronger rates.

Treasury Wine has significantly upped its marketing spend for the ­airport and global travel market to latch on to this market, launching displays, pop-up stores and permanent sites for its luxury wines, including airport installations for its recent Penfolds 2014 Bins and icon wines range at Dubai, London, Amsterdam, Singapore and Hong Kong.

Penfolds suffers from China squatters - Trademarks - Choice to buy back the name or relaunch brand

Newspaper July 14, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Angus Grigg AFR correspondent Section: News 775 Words
Page: 8
OpenURL Link
Shanghai | Treasury Wine Estates failed to register the Chinese name for Penfolds and is now locked in a protracted legal battle with a notorious trademark squatter in an effort regain ownership of the iconic name.

An investigation by The Australian Financial Review has found that a rival wine company has registered three variations of Penfolds' Chinese name, Ben Fu. This potentially leaves Treasury open to a hefty fine for trademark infringement and could also allow the rival to sell wine using the Chinese name for Penfolds.

"Treasury has little choice but to buy back the name at a hefty price or relaunch the brand in China," said wine consultant Andy Tan from Mad Wines. "Actually the Chinese company has done nothing wrong. In China the first person to register the name has the right to use it."

Electronics giant Apple, electric car maker Tesla and French winemaker Castel have all suffered similar problems in China, due to its so called "first to file" rule.

New Chinese trademark laws which came into effect on May 1, should make this more difficult, but previous judgements have not been favourable for ­foreign companies. Last August a ­Chinese court ordered Castel to pay Yuan33.73 million ($A5.8 million) for trademark infringement, after a rival wine company registered its Chinese name in 2000. Castel is appealing the judgement, but it has abandoned its well-known Chinese name and relaunched the brand.

Penfolds is up against the same trademark squatter, who won the record judgement against Castel.Chinese names

In China many consumers can't read the Latin alphabet and therefore the Chinese name and associated characters are often far more important than the English or French brand name.

Penfolds is considered to have chosen a very good Chinese name, as Ben Fu roughly translates to "dashing towards wealth".

In a statement to the Financial Review, Treasury said it had been engaged in litigation to "ensure the integrity of the brand" in China for the past three years. Treasury said it won an initial court case, but the defendant has since appealed.

"This appeal is still pending and it will take time for the Chinese legal system to process this matter," it said without giving a timeframe. Treasury has not previously disclosed the lawsuit.

The trademark issue comes as Treasury suffers from falling sales in China and as it attempts to fend off a $3 billion takeover bid from private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts.

According to the London-based, International Wine and Spirit Research, China edged out France as the biggest consumer of red wine in 2013. In all categories – white, red and sparkling wine – China is ranked number five, but is drinking twice as much wine as it did five years ago.Notorious trademark squatter

Treasury's opponent in its Chinese dispute is the notorious trademark squatter Li Daozhi, who also uses the name Daniel Li.

Another man Li Shen, thought to be a relative of Daniel Li, is also involved.

A search of the China Trademarks office shows that Li Shen has the rights to use the Ben Fu name until July 2019 and a more artistic drawing of the characters until March 2024. Both of these are registered in the spirits and wine category.

The Ben Fu name in the hotels, bars and restaurants category is owned by Li Daozhi. He holds this until April 2021. Efforts to contact either of the Li's were unsuccessful.

Despite Castel appealing the adverse ruling against it in China, Li Doazhi is firmly in control of its Chinese brand name – Kasite.

He has even used the brand to sponsor the French Olympic fencing team and was the name sponsor of Wine Futures 2012, a major industry conference held in Hong Kong.

After winning the case against Castel last year, Li Daozhi said money was not his aim. Rather, he wanted to stop "trademark infringement" from those who "want to make money through copying famous brands".

The Kasite brand has since been awarded the title of "well-known imported wine brand" – the highest ranking given out by China's Trademark office.

Only five other foreign beverage brands in China have been given this status – Chivas, Remy Martin, Martell and Hennessy.

The most expensive trademark squatting payout in China was thought to be Apple's efforts to secure use of the iPad name.

In July 2012 it paid a reported $US60 million to secure use of the name in China, after another company claimed ownership and sought to prevent Apple selling its tablet computer in China.

Last week a trademark squatter sought 23.9 million yuan in compensation from electric car maker Tesla, claiming it was illegally using its own name in China. The lawsuit also demanded Tesla shut down its Chinese showrooms and service centres.

Newspaper July 8, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)

Treasury Wine Estates (TWE) has endured a tumultuous year. The world's biggest listed wine group has fended off a $3 billion takeover offer from private equity firm KKR & Co, appointed new chief executive Michael Clarke and downgraded earnings.

Treasury Wine class action fires up

Newspaper July 3, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 577 Words
Page: 27
OpenURL Link
Legal firm Maurice Blackburn lodged documents in the Federal Court on Wednesday to begin a class action lawsuit against Treasury Wine Estates, on behalf of more than 600 shareholders, over what it claims was a late disclosure of heavy write-downs relating to its United States business in 2013.

The lawsuit is being funded by Bentham IMF.

Maurice Blackburn principal Rebecca Gilsenan filed the class action in the Sydney court. The ­documents do not specify the size of the claim but it is expected to run into the "tens of ­millions".

Treasury Wine said in a note to the ASX on Wednesday it "strongly denies any and all allegations against it and will vigorously defend the legal ­proceeding''.

The claim alleges Treasury, whose brands include Penfolds, Rosemount, Lindemans and Wynns, misled the market and breached its continuous disclosure obligations in relation to the financial impact of over-stocked third-party distributors in the US.

It is the second class action lawsuit that has flowed from Treasury's ­surprise $160 million write-down and profit warning in July 2013, which also included the infamous revelations that up to $35 million worth of low-priced bottled wine in the US would need to be disposed of through the systematic crushing of thousands of cartons of ­bottled wine.

Ms Gilsenan said the action was being brought on behalf of Treasury shareholders "who lost millions of dollars when the company revealed the full extent of the problem in July last year".

She said the documents didn't ­specify a figure for the size of the claim but "we expect and anticipate it would be in the tens of millions".

Ms Gilsenan said the case would present evidence that Treasury knew, or should have known by August 17, 2012, that large write-downs were ­inevitable and that the market should have been alerted far earlier.

"It wasn't informed until July 2013, so shareholders unfairly paid an inflated price for the stock in the meantime."

Melbourne lawyer Mark Elliott, via his specialist class action vehicle Melbourne City Investments, has pursued a separate class action against Treasury in the Supreme Court of Victoria.Unwelcome distraction

The Maurice Blackburn proceedings are an unwelcome distraction for Treasury. It received a buyout proposal from US private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts on April 16 which it ­disclosed on May 20.

That proposal, pitched at $4.70 per share, valued Treasury at $3.1 billion but was firmly rejected by the Treasury board as undervaluing the company. But the board hasn't revealed what sort of price might prompt it to reconsider.

Treasury said in its announcement to the ASX on Wednesday that the Federal Court documents lodged by Maurice Blackburn names the applicant as Brian Jones, who bought 1000 shares in Treasury on September 21, 2012 at an average price of $4.76 per share.

Mr Jones brings the claim on his own behalf and that of other persons who have entered into the litigation funding agreement with Bentham IMF. The claim alleges contraventions of continuous disclosure obligations and misleading and deceptive conduct.

Also on Wednesday, Treasury announced former Coca-Cola ­executive Robert Foye had been appointed managing director for the wine group in Asia, Europe, the Middle East and Africa.

Mr Foye, a US citizen, will be based in Shanghai and report directly to Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke, who worked with Mr Foye earlier in his career when they were both at the soft drinks group.

Key points

Shareholders say Treasury misled market with late disclosure of heavy write-downs in its US business.

Investors 'expect and anticipate' to reclaim tens of millions of dollars.

Penfolds aims for top of the liquor cabinet - Luxury

Newspaper June 28, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 792 Words
Page: 37
OpenURL Link
Treasury undergoing shake-up to realign brands.

Louis Vuitton, Christian Dior, Gucci, Prada, Cartier, Moet & Chandon, Penfolds. Is there an odd one out?

The venerable Penfolds, which has a history stretching back to 1844, is preparing to make a concerted push to elevate itself to join the ranks of the world's luxury goods brands. It is vying for the same increased status as Australian boot maker R.M.Williams, which itself has grand plans to become a global heavyweight now that a private equity firm backed by luxury goods giant LVMH Moet Hennessy Louis Vuitton holds a 49.9 per cent stake.

One part of Penfolds is already in this rarefied air: the $785-per-bottle Penfolds Grange, which has earned its spot as a luxury item around the world and is Australia's most famous wine. But those wanting a cheap and cheerful bottle of red to go with their chops and steak on the barbecue only have to pay around $12.80 for a Penfolds Koonunga Hill shiraz.

The gulf between the top and bottom of the Penfolds range is one of the great conundrums for the brand, which makes up 75 per cent of the total profits generated by Treasury Wine Estates.

Treasury's new chief executive Mike Clarke is trying to overhaul the company to act more like the custodian of fine wine brands and less like a grocery business stuffing its product onto shelves in a rush towards the end of each financial year to make sure it meets its profit targets. Mike Clarke, CEO Treasury Wines Photo: Arsineh Houspian

Clarke, a British food executive who joined Treasury on March 31, is implementing the biggest shake-up at Penfolds in 20 years. He's shifting the release date of high-end Penfolds wines each year to mid-October, away from the usual March and May. The obsession with Penfolds Grange among fine wine buffs reaches fever pitch each year on May 1 when the latest drop is released, so it requires a ­substantial shift in consumer behaviour.

A plus for Treasury is the 2010 Penfolds Grange, which will come out of the blocks in October, eight months before its previously slated release date, is from a harvest that produced high-quality wines.Top scores from those in the know

Peter Gago, who has been Penfolds chief winemaker for the past 12 years and has been with Penfolds for 25 years, is absolutely convinced of the merits of the 2010. His 2008 Penfolds Grange received 100 points out of 100 from esteemed US wine publication the Wine Spectator, and a similar perfect score from noted US wine critic Robert Parker.

Mr Gago already struts the international stage conducting wine tastings and master classes with the elite, and only last week was in Monaco holding court at a gathering of hedge funds and investment firms eager to taste the top-tier Penfolds wines. "Grange has cachet. It has crowd appeal. It's real currency," he said.

He has a vested interest, but Mr Gago said the shift to later in the calendar year has been championed by the winemaking staff for years but had been resisted by corporate executives until Mr Clarke came along. "This has been something the winemakers at Penfolds have been pushing for since I've been here," he said.

Luxury goods sales generally are growing at a quicker rate than other retail staples around the world. A report last year by consulting firm Bain & Co estimated that luxury goods would grow 50 per cent faster than world GDP and break through the €250 billion ($361 billion) mark by 2015. This is being driven by higher consumption by increasingly mobile tourists, the rise of the middle class in emerging countries like China and India and growth in a strand of society that Bain & Co termed "HENRYs" – High Earnings, Not Rich Yet – who like to splash their cash around.

So how will Penfolds fare as it pushes to move up the ranks? Mr Clarke is undertaking a structural separation inside Treasury where the luxury brands such as Penfolds will be overseen by a separate unit away from more commercial brands such as Rosemount and Lindemans.

Professor Mark Ritson, a retailing expert from the Melbourne Business School said the definition of what is a luxury item keeps changing and there is no hard and fast rule. "In Australia 40 years ago having a colour television was deemed to be a luxury good," he said.

He believes Mr Clarke is heading in the right direction with his plans, but there are question marks about having the brand selling at relatively low prices for some labels, right up to the top-tier Grange. "The question you should ask is whether the brand architecture can take it?" Professor Ritson said.

He compares that approach to the Volkswagen Group in the automotive group which has different brands for different price points. It owns Audi at the upper end, Volkswagen at the mid-tier and Skoda as a cheaper brand.

Treasury in Woolies, Coles plan - Wine Company looks to exclusive supply deals

Newspaper June 27, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 877 Words
Page: 17
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates is looking to strike exclusive supply deals with major liquor retailers Woolworths and Coles whereby it will only sell some of its lesser-known brands in outlets ­controlled by the two firms.

The deals to quarantine some of those brands to be sold only in Woolworths outlets, or only those operated by Coles, are part of the plan by new chief executive Mike Clarke to try and generate better growth at the less profitable end of Treasury's 83-strong wine brand portfolio.

"We are going to have a good look at Brand A as an exclusive," Mr Clarke said.

He declined to specify which of the more commercial brands might be included in this strategy built around a retailer exclusive.

Other large independent liquor chains may also be involved in separate exclusive deals.

There are also plans to remove between 10 to 15 brands from the lower-end of the portfolio either through a sale, a joint-venture arrangement or by simply discontinuing a brand.

That whittling down of the portfolio would be a gradual staged process to extract maximum value and avoid a fire sale of brands all in one hit.

"They could be sold or they could be a joint venture," he said.

But the top 20 "power brands" which Mr Clarke has outlined for several weeks as being integral to his turnaround strategy for Treasury and which will receive most of a 50 per cent increase in marketing spend will be untouchable in the overhaul. A collection of around 20 other brands which are referred to internally at Treasury as "regional champions" will also be quarantined. Big-selling commercial brands including Rosemount and Lindemans will also be the recipients of major investment in brand-building.

The "power brands" include Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Wynns, Matua from New Zealand and the Californian wine brands Chateau St Jean and Beringer.release date of high-end lines changed

Those in the next set of around 20 brands in the regional champions list include Devil's Lair from Western ­Australia's Margaret River region and Coldstream Hills from Victoria's Yarra Valley.

Treasury shares continued to rise on Thursday, gaining 1.3 per cent by mid-day to be at $5.13 before fading to close 2¢ higher at $5.09. This followed a strong gain of 5 per cent on Wednesday after an announcement that included another hefty write-down of $260 million on commercial wine brands and infrastructure mainly related to the US , and plans to shift the release date of high-end Penfolds wines to October.

While not spelling it out specifically – there was no mention of any profit downgrade – Treasury in practical terms also affirmed on Wednesday that it had met a full-year profit forecast band of between $190 million to $210 million in earnings.

Private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts made a $4.70 per share buyout proposal for Treasury on April 16 which only became public on May 20. It valued Treasury at $3.1 billion but was rejected by the Treasury board.

The shift of the Penfolds releases from March and May to October will undo two decades of convention and mean there will be a second release of the flagship Penfolds Grange this year in October.

The 2010 Grange will be released then, just six months after the 2009 version was released on May 1 this year with a retail price of $785 per bottle.

Mr Clarke said Woolworths and Coles were both receptive to the changes to the high-end Penfolds wine release date changes, and he had been in high-level talks with them for almost two months about it rather than just spring the changes upon them.

"Positive and engaged,'' he said when asked about what the big retailers thought about the changes to the ­Penfolds timetable.

Woolworths owns the Dan Murphy's liquor superstores chain along with the mid-market BWS chain, while Coles runs First Choice, Liquorland and Vintage Cellars.

Stockbroker Citi warned in a note on Treasury after the raft of announcements on Wednesday there was a risk of a disappointing 2014-15 at Treasury. Analysts Gino Rossi and Craig Woolford suggested the possibility of a weak financial result in 2014-15 meant "that KKR or any other suitors can afford to be patient".

They recommended that investors sell their Treasury shares: "With the share price running ahead of KKR's $4.70 bid we think the risks around a weak FY15 result outweigh any potential upside from a higher bid."

One of their concerns is that Treasury appeared to be running behind schedule in its efforts to re-adjust inventory levels in the United States operations after running into serious problems in 2013 with too much stock in the warehouses of third-party distributors.

The analysts suggested that a "stock destruction" part of the plan had happened, but that plans to ship fewer cases of wine into the distributors system to ensure that any oversupply into the market was well and truly eradicated still had further to go and there would need to be a further deliberate slowing of shipments in the 2014-15 year.

The troubles in the US last year led to the axeing of former Treasury chief executive David Dearie in late 2013, who was replaced by Mr Clarke on March 31 this year.

Treasury in mid-2013 announced $160 million of write-downs connected with the US business including an infamous move to destroy $35 million worth of bottled wine in distributors' warehouses.

This involved the crushing of six million bottles of low-priced wine in cartons using steamrollers and special environmental clean-up techniques.

Miners and banks drag the index lower

Newspaper June 26, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Equities Sally Rose Section: Market Wrap 552 Words
Page: 26
OpenURL Link
A sell-off in energy and resources stocks led the losses with shares declining for the second consecutive day as local investors continue to consolidate their portfolio ahead of the end of the tax year next Monday.

The benchmark S&P/ASX 200 Index lost 0.6 per cent on Wednesday, to 5402 points, while the broader All Ordinaries Index fell 0.5 per cent to 5386.8 points, as weakness in the biggest bank stocks added to the selling pressure.

Local shares fell as expected when the market opened after Wall Street declined on Tuesday night. Asian markets provided a negative lead in the afternoon session.

Energy was the worst performing sector on the local bourse, down 0.9 per cent. Australia's biggest oil producer Woodside Petroleum fell 0.5 per cent to $41.38, as Brent crude oil dipped to $US114.22 a barrel, while West Texas Intermediate crude oil lifted to $US106.67 a barrel.

Local energy producers are bucking a global trend seen over recent weeks, whereby oil and gas companies in most markets have risen in response to risks to the global oil supply from Iraq and Ukraine.BHP, Rio fall

"Australian oil and gas producers have under-performed their global peers in recent weeks in reaction to some short-term local issues," Pengana Capital senior fund manager Tim Schroeders said.

"Some investors sold down their interests in smaller energy players to mop up discounted stock from Royal Dutch Shell's sell-down of Woodside, while other people have been switching some of their money into mining stocks after that sector suffered heavy price falls," Mr Schroeders said.

Resources giant BHP Billiton fell 0.7 per cent to $36.21 despite analysts welcoming a plan announced on Tuesday to cut an extra 500 plus jobs. BHP's main rival Rio Tinto shed 0.8 per cent to $59.07, while iron ore miner Fortescue Metals Group was unchanged at $4.36, after the spot price for iron ore, delivered in China, eased 0.1 per cent to $US93.30 a tonne. When the local market closed on Wednesday, Dalian iron ore futures indicated the spot price was set to lift overnight.

But longer term investors are bracing for weaker average iron ore prices for at least the next 12 months. Australia's Bureau of Resources and Energy Economics cut its price forecasts for the nation's biggest export. The official government forecast is now tipping the iron ore spot price will average $US97 a tonne in 2015, down from the $US103 estimated in March.

Investor sentiment took another hit from an official monthly government survey that showed a 1.3 per cent decline in the number of employers advertising to fill skilled positions in May. The data was interpreted by many as a sign the federal budget has had a material effect on confidence.

The big four banks were also lower. Commonwealth Bank of Australia fell 0.7 per cent to $81.02, while Westpac Banking Corporation shed 0.9 per cent to $33.83. ANZ Banking Group lost 0.6 per cent to $33.42, and National Australia Bank declined 0.9 per cent to $32.90.

Among other blue-chip stocks, Telstra Corporation fell 0.4 per cent to $5.17, Woolworths was unchanged at $35.73, and Wesfarmers, owner of Coles, lost 0.5 per cent to $41.19.Scentre down, Westfield up

Treasury Wine Estates, owner of Lindemans and Penfolds, jumped 5 per cent to $5.07, despite flagging an additional $260 million in write-downs for the current financial year as it announced a business restructure.

Jamie Nicol, the chief investment officer of Treasury shareholder Dalton Nicol Reid issued a statement publicly backing the strategy.

Genting still a player in casino war

Newspaper June 26, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Michael Smith Section: Chanticleer 1297 Words
Page: 52
OpenURL Link
Malaysian billionaire KT Lim slipped quietly into Sydney this week to meet with the state's gaming regulator. The timing was significant. It is exactly two years since Lim's Genting Group lodged an application to increase its stake in Echo Entertainment beyond 10 per cent.

This is an extraordinarily long time, even in the tightly regulated gaming world. James Packer had his application to increase his stake in Echo approved in a year, while it took six months for the Nevada Gaming Commission to grant Genting approval for a resort in Las Vegas. Lim is running out of patience and met with the NSW Independent Liquor and Gaming Authority (ILGA) on Tuesday to try to get to the ­bottom of the delay.

Genting has long been the wild card in the feud between Echo and James Packer's Crown over control of the Sydney casino market. The Asian gaming group has taken a low profile in the past year though and Echo executives and investors have been bemused about why the  gaming regulator has taken so long to consider its application.

Lim's presence in Sydney indicates he is still serious about building a presence in Australia. It also suggests it is not Genting dragging out the approval process by not submitting the required information as some have suggested. Unlike his trip to the country in April last year, the Genting chief executive was not accompanied by his usual entourage. His visit was brief and he flew out on Tuesday night. There were no formal meetings scheduled with Echo.

Genting has enormous gaming interests across Asia and plenty of free cash which would make it an attractive partner for Echo which is seeking to increase the number of Asian VIP gamblers coming to Sydney.

Genting is also a potential acquirer of Echo, either by itself or with a partner. While Packer sold his 10 per cent stake in May last year and has since been given the green light to operate a second casino in Sydney, some investors still see Echo as a potential target.

Some analysts believe Genting no longer has ambitions for Echo because it did not seek to team up with it on Brisbane's Queen's Wharf project. Echo announced this week it had partnered with Hong Kong's Chow Tai Fook and Far East Consortium to bid for the multibillion-dollar Brisbane casino and entertainment precinct.

But that does not necessarily stop Genting buying Echo, which would be the Brisbane project's operator if it wins the bidding process. The decision by the NSW government to give Packer the go ahead to operate a second casino in Sydney has enhanced, rather than reduced, Genting's interest in Australia

Crown Resorts competes with Genting in Asia and Packer's ability to capitalise on the flow of VIP gamers between Australia and Asia is something Lim cannot do at the moment. Genting owns casinos in Malaysia, Singapore, the United States and Britain and has interests in mining and agriculture assets. Lim has presented the company as potentially a significant investor in New South Wales' tourism infrastructure at a time when mining investment is slowing.

This looks doubtful if Genting runs out of patience and walks away. The regulatory approval process is normal as NSW gaming rules mean any single shareholder must seek permission from the regulator to increase its stake beyond 10 per cent. But the time it is taking to reach a conclusion is ­raising eyebrows.

The ILGA says it continues to work with Genting about its application and the matter remains under consideration.

Shifting the release date of its top Penfolds wine brands is a brave move for Treasury Wine Estates as new chief Mike Clarke seeks to reboot the business after a shocking ­couple of years.

Penfolds, undoubtedly the jewel in ­Treasury's crown, has shifted the market release date for its top wines from March and May to October. This is a big mental shift for the company and a change that was resisted by former management.

Clarke says the list of positives outweighs the negatives. It allows Treasury to market its most profitable product better in the run-up to Christmas and abolishes the mad rush to sell product into warehouses before the end of the financial year.

It also allows it to move wine in cooler conditions.

Clarke initially looked at changing the company's financial year to get around that problem but was asked by his senior ­management team to change the launch dates instead.

This is an important marketing tweak but it does not solve all of Treasury's problems. The company took a further write-down on Wednesday for assets it paid too much for when it was still owned by Foster's.

But investors were happy the company was not downgrading earnings guidance after Clarke said he was feeling "pretty ­positive" about financial year 2014 forecasts.

This strengthens its defence slightly against private equity suitor KKR which had its $4.70 offer for the company rebuffed by the board in April.

There are no formal talks going on between the two but investors and the board are said to be open-minded to an offer of $5 or more.

Treasury shares closed up 5 per cent at $5.07 on Wednesday, suggesting investors are confident KKR may up its original approach or another bidder will join the fray.

A break-up still looks inevitable if ­private equity moves in – and there appears to be no shortage of buyers for the US operations.

One of the country's leading CEOs says when you start in the top job you have a ­six-week period in which to blame your predecessor for all the company's mistakes. After that, it is time to man up and get things right.

Clarke's grace period is now up and with Wednesday's announcement he is making some progress at least with a restructuring plan, although the jury is still out.

Competition regulation is a hot topic at the moment as Ian Harper prepares the biggest inquiry into the issue in two decades.

Australian Competition and Consumer Commission chairman Rod Sims has made it loud and clear this week he has issues with the upcoming wave of state government ­privatisations. He reiterated his concerns in the ACCC's submission to the Harper review on Wednesday.

It was no coincidence this coincided with the Australian Competition Tribunal's ­decision to back AGL's $1.5 billion acquisition of state-owned Macquarie Generation. The ruling is contentious as it overrules the ACCC's decision in March to block the sale and has huge implications for billions of ­dollars in upcoming asset sale programs.

Working in AGL's favour was a commitment to supply electricity hedge contracts to smaller retailers which alleviated some of the concerns.

Governments have accused Sims of ­getting too involved in public policy rather than being a regulator but he says he is just doing his job.

The ACCC says its position is based on the fact that the deal means three of NSW's ­largest generators will be sold to the three largest retailers, reducing competition in the electricity market.

Sims has high hopes for the Harper review and says the emphasis is getting ­competition back on centre stage, as well as stripping out different regulations between the states which are hurting businesses.

Business and Sims will never see eye to eye for obvious reason. Sims says he accepts that companies must pursue the profit motive as long as they do so in accordance with competition laws. But he says the ­existing regulations are deficient and out of step with comparable markets like Britain, the US, Japan and Korea.

Sims is proposing more clout for the ACCC to address the misuse of market power. He wants limitations on the ACCC's information gathering powers eased as well as the ability to conduct market studies.

The Macquarie Generation decision is a blow for Sims who says he won't be rushed into making quick decisions around ­potential bidders for state-owned assets without the appropriate access regimes.

Aurizon doing more with less

Newspaper June 26, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Matthew Stevens Section: Companies and Markets 1264 Words
Page: 32
OpenURL Link
There are two firm conclusions to draw from the latest rich bank of impairments and provisions confirmed by Aurizon, the nation's biggest rail freighter.

First, Australian coal's long-drafted growth story is as stuffed as coal's current price. Second, the workplace inefficiencies, sustained by rail unions, come at an absurdly high cost to Aurizon shareholders specifically and the nation more broadly.

Within the bounds of standing employment agreements, Aurizon has been reorganising its train operations for the better part of a year now.

As a result it is doing more with less rolling stock. That means it has now twice reviewed the valuation of the locomotives and wagons it owns.

The Casey Jones of this efficiency drive is an import from Canadian Pacific called Mike Franczak. With new eyes set on old problems, Franczak has set about changing Aurizon's operational game with irresistible purpose and energy.

Much of the financial heavy lifting generated by Franczak's efficiency drive was done in the first half as Aurizon booked $147 million of impairments. But on Thursday it added up to $25 million to that number after further work on what is called the "Integrated Operating Plan".

That work identified Aurizon was still running 20 more locos than it needed, and 195 wagons.

As we have reported, Aurizon is knee deep in a negotiations with rail unions over a new enterprise agreement that would bring the terms and conditions of the train drivers' employment, among others, into the 21st century.

At that point, I presume, unless there is some underlying growth in the volume of coal and freight Aurizon might be asked to carry, we might well see further advances in efficiency that require similar sorts of financial concessions.

And what of the coal story? Well, Aurizon will take a second-half impairment of up to $45 million on the preparations for growth projects in Queensland coal that the company believes will not now happen. They include the recently scrapped plan for a new coal terminal at Dudgeon Point, and the slated expansion of the Wiggins Island terminal, the first phase of which is still a work in progress.

For good measure Aurizon has taken a $6 million write-down on work done on expansion of its North Queensland network ahead of the decision in March to align itself with GVK Hancock's Galilee Basin venture.

Interestingly, at least for train track spotters like me, Aurizon also took a $19 million hit on the work it did for potential customers in the Pilbara.

That size of that number says Aurizon's interest in the mooted east Pilbara link, was either serious or a very expensive indulgence. And the decision to write off that work says that Aurizon is now extremely confident of success in its $1.4 billion joint venture bid for Aquila Resources.

Aquila is the owner of a proposal that requires the construction of $6 billion worth of railway and port on the Pilbara's south-western flank. And presently, events suggest Aurizon and senior partner Baosteel are almost in control of that proposition.

To that end, of course, the decision by Aquila co-founder, Charles Bass, to sell into the offer is sweet cream on the cake baked by last week's recommendation of the bid by the junior's board.

That recommendation arrived with slightly conditional acceptance by Aquila's chairman Tony Poli. He owns 28 per cent of the company while Bass owns another 10.7 per cent.

As Baosteel opened this control pitch with a 20 per cent stake, the joint venture now speaks for 58.7 per cent of the business.

Given this situation is not complicated by further, last-minute intervention, that leaves Baosteel and Aurizon with control and with satisfaction of its key 50.1 per cent acceptance condition.

Heavy weight of reform

If Michael Clarke sensed his latest message of reform at Treasury Wine Estates was falling on excessively cautious ears, then he would be right.

Just four months into the Clarke era at TWE and he has taken not one but two reform packages to the market. That both of them are refreshingly sensible, consistent with his introductory pitch and transparently discrete in their impact, does not matter a whole lot.

The problem for Clarke is that reform fatigue weighs heavy among TWE watchers. Pretty much ever since this business was seeded within former owner Foster's Brewing Group through a $482 million acquisition of Mildara Blass, these sort of transformational plans have come and gone as rapidly as executive management.

So far the TWE story has run to the same script since it was gifted independence from beer in May 2011. New management affirmed a plan, things went awry, the board lost patience and, before you know it, we have a new executive broom and a spanking new transformational plan.

Now, the very least we can hope this time around is that TWE's new, but already crisis-scarred, board might exhibit confidence and patience in Clarke, his method and the "step-changes" he now proposes for what should be our national champion.

Clarke arrived at TWE in February with a four-point action template. He would pursue better execution of existing plans, reduce costs, increase marketing by 50 per cent and address the deeper structural challenges.

In May he dealt with the first two step, asserting more direct authority over the business and confirming a $35 million target for cost reduction for fiscal year 2014.

Now he has dealt with some of those more structural problems.

Clarke will consolidate and bring foward the date to release the all-important Penfolds bins and luxury wines. He will also structurally separate across each of his diverse operational geographies, the commercial wine business from the luxury and (dreadfully monickered) "masstige" brands.Both moves are so dreadfully logical it is hard to believe they were not done long ago.

The difference between commercial wine and the stuff that generates real margin is like the difference between cheese slices and a dripping brie. One is manufactured, the other is the product of age-old craft and contemporary genius.Time to flourish

Clarke's new structure aims at allowing both sides of his production equation to flourish without either harming the other.

Shifting the Penfolds releases to a single date in October loomed potentially somewhat more controversial in that it has a compelling logic. The decision brings forward by six months the 2014 releases. It will distort 2015 numbers, making it very difficult for the market to track the underlying business.

It says much about Clarke's savvy that the optics of this move were handled so well. By simply acknowledging the reality that his numbers will be hard to track through the "reset" year of FY15, Clarke defused debate over his numbers.

And by introducing Penfolds' chief winemaker, the irrepressible Pete Gago, to Wednesday's briefing, he settled debate over where the initiative came from. Gago made it abundantly clear the Penfolds family was right behind the change, welcoming it as the expression of an "enlightened executive" with the courage to accept advice offered to others for years.

Gago noted too October was a much more sensible time of the year to be shipping wine, and the opportunity to market his product over a full financial year rather than over just the June quarter, offered new opportunity to extract margin through astute stock management.

The proposition is at the heart of Clarke's strategy. Like any sensible person, he identified Penfolds as the wellspring of his business. What puzzled him was why its bounty was released in two stages through May, leaving the business under pressure to move as much stock as possible in the closing months of the financial year.

Clarke's original idea was to shift his year-end reporting date to September or December. Gago's guys had a better idea. Why not recover history and move to the October release date that set the Penfolds calendar 35 years ago?

New release on Penfolds unveiled

Newspaper June 26, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 706 Words
Page: 17
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke says his bold strategy to break decades of convention and shifting the release date of high-end Penfolds wines is part of a "roadmap'' any owner of the wine group should implement.

Mr Clarke unveiled the new strategy on Wednesday along with a fresh set of writedowns of up to $260 million mainly connected with the company's troubled United States wine business.

A relieved sharemarket largely ignored another hefty writedown by the world's largest stand-alone listed wine company and focused instead on the fact that Treasury had met its own full-year forecasts for 2013-14.

Treasury shares climbed almost 5 per cent, up 24¢ to $5.07, putting pressure on private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts to contemplate a higher offer than the $4.70 per share buyout proposal originally made in mid-April. Meeting profit targets had been one of the conditions in the KKR proposal.

The high-risk strategy for the Penfolds business of moving the release date from the usual March and May timetable to October is designed to smooth out sales of the brand throughout the whole year, rather than ­compress them into the last part of the financial year when there is a rush to meet profit targets.

Penfolds makes up to 75 per cent of the profits at Treasury, which has 83 different wine brands in a sprawling empire that Mr Clarke intends trimming back to a more manageable and efficient stable. Mr Clarke and his chief financial officer Tony Reeves said on Wednesday the majority of the new $260 million in writedowns and impairments were connected with the US business and low-end commercial wine brands selling for below $US10 ($10.6) per bottle, but they declined to reveal the specific brands. This is on top of $160 million in writedowns revealed by Treasury in its US business in mid-2013 which included the now infamous destruction of $35 million of excess low-priced bottled wine. Mr Clarke said the Penfolds shift would swing the company away from a "short-term mindset'' where it was always bustling to ship Penfolds into warehouses in a rush towards the end of the June 30 financial year. "It just became a ridiculous process with retail partners and distributors,'' he said. Penfolds would now be treated as "revered, scarce and special" through the change. Treasury would be able to meet its full-year profit guidance of between $190 million to $210 million on an earnings before interest, tax and the SGARA accounting standard basis, without specifically saying so.

"You can sense we're feeling pretty positive,'' Mr Clarke said. Meeting that guidance was a condition of a $3.1 billion buyout proposal from KKR revealed publicly last month. Mr Clarke said the announcements about the shift in Penfolds releases and the writedowns had no implications for any takeover activity and Treasury was getting on with the job of implementing ''the roadmap'' to fuel future growth. He warned that 2014-15 was a "reset year", but didn't quantify what that meant in terms of future profit forecasts. Treasury, which also owns Rosemount, Lindeman's, Wynns and Wolf Blass, revealed the impairments comprised writedowns of historical prices paid for wine businesses before Treasury was demerged from Foster's in 2011, along with impairments on a string of winery assets at the lower-priced commercial end of the market.Heavy writedowns

Mr Clarke couldn't promise that would be the last of the writedowns but hoped it would be the case. He also announced a restructuring of its Australian operations and is setting up a separate unit to look after more expensive wines .He also signalled that more of those high-end wines including Penfolds Grange, would be sold in the United States, Europe and Asia and less in Australia. Andrew Carter, the chief commercial officer for Australia, Pacific and Europe, will depart the organisation because of that restructure.

Penfolds chief winemaker Peter Gago, who oversees the Penfold's Grange and other high-end reds, said there was only a minor risk in the shift of releases, which brings forward the sale of those wines by up to eight months when they would have previously been still in maturation in a one-off change. "A little minor risk this year but going forward it's all sunshine,'' Mr Gago said. Owner of fine wine retailer East End Cellars, Michael Andrewartha, was puzzled by the change in Penfolds release strategy, saying it would "put people in a spin".and make it harder for Penfolds in a crowded pre-Christmas market.

Luxury giant LVMH among suitors for Treasury Wine

Newspaper June 25, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Edited by Sarah Thompson, Anthony Macdonald, and Gretchen Friemann Section: Market Wrap 388 Words
Page: 14
OpenURL Link
Global suitors are emerging out of the woodwork for Treasury Wine Estates, setting the stage for a potential bidding war for the ailing owner of Australia's best known wine brands.

According to sources, LVMH, the luxury goods brand controlled by French tycoon, Bernard Arnault, is mulling whether to submit a full offer for the business or prise off the coveted Penfolds brand as part of a takeover consortium.

Bright Food, the Chinese state-owned food group, is also circling, although some question its determination to pursue Treasury given its performance in previous merger and acquisition scenarios in Australia.

Constellation Brands, the world's largest wine seller has also been crunching the numbers with sources pointing to a potential consortium involving one of the US buyout firms.

Treasury's US arm, which includes Beringer, is widely regarded as the least attractive part of the business.

Sources claimed a revival in its fortunes could take three or four years and argue the challenge is best suited to a private equity player.

KKR submitted an offer of $4.70 per share or $3.1 billion for Treasury last month but was given short shrift by the board, which deemed the bid too cheap and vowed to carry on instead with a turnaround plan that involves a 50 per cent increase in the marketing outlay as well as a swingeing cost-cutting regime.

Yet the improvements are unlikely to affect this financial year's performance. As JPMorgan noted recently, Treasury will struggle to hit its 2014 earning guidance, arguing luxury wine sales in May and June will be pivotal to the outcome.

Treasury is said to be preparing an announcement, which could come as early as Wednesday, confirming the weak conditions. But it is understood management will also seek to allay investor concerns that the entire business model is flawed, as was suggested in a trade publication this week.

The denouncement comes as many in the market question Treasury's abrupt rejection of KKR's approach.

According to sources the company's advisers, Goldman Sachs have been working hard to drum up competing interest in the company, which ranks as one of the world's largest pure play winemakers. But these players may not submit a proposal and even if a rival bid does emerge few believe it will be pitched at a significantly higher level than KKR's. Those familiar with the matter argue the buyout firm would not countenance an increase of more than 5 per cent on its existing offer.

KKR moves again on Treasury stock

OpenURL Link
United States private equity firm ­Kohlberg Kravis Roberts is understood to have made fresh soundings to major institutional shareholders in Treasury Wine Estates as the wine company prepares to cut about 100 staff by June 30.

New chief executive Mike Clarke is aggressively pushing to cut costs. The company is preparing for two large redundancies tranches in a restructure which may axe slightly more than the 5 per cent of the workforce target ­outlined by the company last month.

About 100 Treasury staff in middle management, administration, logistics and distribution, and some contractors, will be made redundant by the end of June – between 50 and 60 per cent of the broader restructuring.

In the next and final wave of the ­cost-cutting, up to 60 people will lose their jobs by the end of September. A small number of staff have already left after Mr Clarke outlined a $35 million cost-cutting program on May 20 when the wine group, whose brands include ­Penfolds and Rosemount, rejected an indicative buyout proposal from KKR pitched at $4.70 a share.

Most of the restructuring and ­thinning out of middle management layers is occurring in the United States and Australian operations. A ­substantial portion of the cuts is from the ­company's head office in Queensbridge Street in the Melbourne suburb of Southbank .

It comes as some large institutional shareholders in Treasury have again been approached by KKR and its ­advisers who seek to outline the rationale for its proposal at $4.70 a share.Direct approach

Mr Clarke and his board were ­worried the "confidentiality" of the KKR proposal had therefore been lost.

The public announcement of the ­previously secret KKR proposal came five weeks after it was received, and also after Treasury had publicly rejected speculation during that five-week period about other potential buyers such as Pernod Ricard and Constellation Brands being involved in possible deals.

Advisers to KKR are thought to have again approached some of Treasury's main institutional shareholders in the past few days.

Treasury's share price has ­continued to soften in the past few days and slipped a further 7¢ on Wednesday to close at $4.82.

Takeover plays by sector - M&A

Newspaper June 7, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Jared Lynch Section: Smart Money 1283 Words
Page: 24
OpenURL Link
Food and beverage companies are among those ripe for the picking, writes Jared Lynch .

Globally, $US504 billion worth of merger and acquisition deals have been struck; it was $US142 billion for the same period last year. With action picking up in Australia as well, we take a look at which sectors and companies here are potential takeovers.

After two years of disciplined spending, the sector is waking from its M&A slumber. Graeme Browning, an Ernst Young partner who leads transaction advisory services, says valuations have fallen to a point where deals are becoming attractive to mining and mining services companies.

M&A  activity among resource companies fell for a third year in 2013. The number of deals dropped 25 per cent year on year amid intense volatility in metals prices, particularly iron ore, Australia's most lucrative export. Browning says many mining services companies face financial stress after spending cuts, so he expects consolidation.

Holly Stiles, Grant Thornton partner focusing on energy and resources, expects deals to start flowing among metal producers, particularly those enjoying prices rises.

Nickel tops the list. It has been the best-performing commodity this year – soaring 56 per cent until a temporary correction in May – after Indonesia, the world's biggest producer, banned exports of raw material in January. Stiles says Asian investors looking to secure supply are particularly interested in acquiring Australian companies. ''Generally nickel is a good sector, copper, even bauxite,'' she says.

Credit Suisse analyst Hasan Tevfik says Panoramic Resources, Sirius Resources and Western Areas are the most likely takeover targets given the renewed interest in nickel. He also tips Alumina to go on the block, with its partner US aluminium giant Alcoa its most likely acquirer.

A domino effect is expected to start rolling through the retail sector after Woolworths South Africa launched a $2.2 billion take­over of Australia's second biggest department store, David Jones.

Tevfik expects the upmarket retailer's rival, Myer, to generate interest among international players, as it trades at a 40-to-50 per cent discount to the global sector average.

Browning expects more mergers and acquisitions among retailers, and not just the bigger companies. ''As consumer spending strengthens, that will encourage transaction activity among the speciality retailers.

''There will be more opportunity for IPOs, for example. I saw speculation this morning that Smiggle could be a spinout from Just Group. I don't know whether that will occur but that's the type of transaction that's more likely in this stage of the cycle.''

He said renewed interest in the sector was typical after a significant takeover bid, such as Woolworths SA's offer for David Jones.

Food and beverage

As the world's population explodes, so does the need for food security. Global players are jostling to position themselves for a baby boom like no other.

The Earth's population is expected to rise by 1 billion people in the next 11 years, according to the United Nations. But that's not the only thing that is set to increase.

A rapidly expanding middle class in China – which is demanding a higher-­quality, more Western-style diet – has thrust Australian food processors into the spotlight, says Tony Pittito, Grant Thornton's national head of food and beverage. A flurry of merger-and-acquisition activity has erupted in the sector. Treasury Wine Estate, makers of Penfolds, has rejected a $3 billion private equity bid from US private equity giant Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, and Australia's biggest breadmaker,Goodman Fielder, is the target of a $1.37 billion offer from a Singapore-Hong Kong consortium.

Meanwhile, Australian private equity outfit Pacific Equity Partners has offloaded Peters Ice Cream for what is believed to be more than $400 million to Europe's second-biggest ice-cream maker R&R.

''We think it will continue in food and ­beverage,'' Pittito says. ''Why? First, access into Asia is very important for these global players, and second, Australian food and beverage companies are clearly seen as being high quality and have a very good reputation." Also vital is food security, in being able to shore up supply. ''The other reason is companies are looking for export growth. Acquisitions into Australian companies help because it gives a foot print into Asia.''

Pittito says Australian companies are generally cheaper on a price-to-earnings basis compared with their international peers, adding to their takeover appeal.

''The Australian dollar is very strong and if it were to slip, then this activity would increase further.''

Ideal targets are companies that have strong brands and the potential to leverage those brands into Asia. ''If you look at the Goodman Fielders, the Warrnambool Cheese and Butters, the Peters, they are all products that have good growth opportunities into Asian markets, Treasury Wine Estates is another one.

''The key takeaway for Australian companies is this activity is going to continue and they need to be getting their house in order, planning for it, and make sure they are maximising their value so if they're subject to a takeover, they can maximise that value.''

Agribusiness

Like food and beverage, agribusinesses are vital to feeding the world's surging population. But PAC partners agribusiness analyst Paul Jensz expects M&A activity to be different from other sectors. He predicts more strategic partnerships among agricultural businesses than outright takeovers.

This is despite a three-way global bidding war for Warrnambool Cheese and Butter, which made it the world's most expensive dairy company on a price-earnings multiple.

But that transaction also saw Bega and Fonterra strengthen their alliance, with Fonterra taking a 10 per cent stake in the company, and that followed Japanese-based Sumitomo Chemical Company buying 20 per cent of Australian agriculture chemicals supplier Nufarm in 2011 in a deal designed to share distribution, development, product formulation and logistics.

''I think all the easy M&As have been done,'' Jensz says. ''There's a case for spreading the management expertise from some other companies, and this, I suppose, is the start of M&A activity.

''It's a matter for long-term investors and corporates forming a relationship with the Begas, the Select Harvests of the world. It's similar to what Nufarm and Sumitomo have done. M&A would be more of finding the right dancing partner, investing together, maybe trading assets between partners and over time getting to know each other and working out if you can get together and make a lot of money.

Jensz said these sort of partnerships would limit the losses that some internationals have had investing in Australia.

''Buying something is expensive and investing money in logistics and processing is pretty big bucks as well. You want to be sure that you're on the right team.

''It's pretty aggressive going in and buying something. It's mergers and acquisitions. The first one is merger, trying to do something together.''

Still, Tevfik expects some deals within the sector. He wouldn't be surprised if Sumitomo completely acquires Nufarm, and supermarket-to-coal conglomerate Wesfarmers takes over fertiliser company Incitec Pivot, saying it's a ''good strategic fit''.

The Abbott government has given its strongest indication yet that it will relax restrictions on media ownership, paving the way for more consolidation of the industry.

In March, Communications Minister Malcolm Turnbull questioned why the country needs specific ownership rules for print, television and radio, given the surge in competition from the internet.

Media reforms would allow a potential takeover of Ten by News Corporation, which has strong links with the network. Lachlan Murdoch, Ten's former chairman, is now co-chairman of News Corp with his father Rupert.

The changes could also allow Fairfax Media, owner of The Australian Financial Review, The Sydney Morning Herald and The Age, to join forces with a television partner such as Channel Nine or Seven.

Also under scrutiny is the 75 per cent reach rule. This restricts television networks from broadcasting to less than 75 per cent of the Australian population. Analysts at JPMorgan say the scrapping of the reach rules could make takeover targets of regional TV networks Prime Media, which broadcasts Seven content in regional areas, and Southern Cross Media, which broadcasts Ten content. The privately held WIN Television broadcasts Nine content.
Copyright © 2014 Nine Entertainment Company. www.afr.com. Not available for re-distribution.

Coles fields Vintage Cellars bids

Newspaper June 6, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 674 Words
Page: 13
OpenURL Link
The Wesfarmers-owned Coles supermarket and liquor group has received approaches from private equity buyers wanting to buy the Vintage Cellars ­liquor chain for up to $300 million.

It is understood the approaches have been made in the past few days and involve splitting off the Vintage Cellars chain in a clean sale out of the larger Coles liquor division.

Vintage Cellars operates 77 stores around Australia and has been battling to make headway with its smaller footprint stores in a highly competitive market, increasingly dominated by superstores such as rival Dan Murphy's, owned by Woolworths.

Coles does not separate the financial performance of its individual liquor retailing brands. Industry sources suggested annual sales at Vintage Cellars are currently around $250 million and that it makes profits of between $15 million and $20 million.

But it has been weighed down by onerous head office costs that have crimped its performance.

The approaches have been made by at least two different parties as part of deals involving Coles' underperforming liquor division and are centred on a spinoff of Vintage Cellars.

A Coles spokesman said on Thursday: "We don't comment on media or market speculation around mergers, acquisitions or divestments."

New South Wales provides a large chunk of the sales revenue of Vintage Cellars; 35 of the stores are in that state.

There are 15 stores in Victoria and 12 in Western Australia, nine in Queensland and four in South Australia, with one each in the Northern Territory and the Australian Capital Territory.

Vintage Cellars has struggled to lift its performance in the past few years, and operates at the upper end of the market.Major overhaul

Speculation has intensified about major changes at the Coles liquor ­division after John Durkan, who will officially take over as managing ­director of Coles supermarkets and ­liquor on July 1, signalled last week there would be a major overhaul of the liquor business.

It is understood internal structural changes were made inside the broader Coles liquor operations around six to eight weeks ago, which in practical terms separated Vintage Cellars from its stablemates Liquorland and the 1st Choice Liquor Superstore brand, and made it more autonomous.

Speculation has been bubbling in the liquor trade in the past 48 hours about the possibility of a sale of Vintage Cellars, and the prospect of a future public float of the business.

Mr Durkan, currently chief operating officer of Coles, signalled last week at a Wesfarmers strategy briefing there would be a comprehensive review of the entire Coles liquor business and its performance was ­unacceptable.

He was blunt in his assessment of the performance of the Coles liquor retailing portfolio, at the briefing on May 28 .

"The returns, quite frankly, have been unacceptable, and we've got a lot of work to do,'' Mr Durkan told investors. He also said on that day in a specific reference to Vintage Cellars that "we actually see a really good business in Vintage Cellars''.

He foreshadowed a major overhaul of the liquor operations and a focus on liquor outlets located adjacent to Coles supermarkets.

Liquor sales make up about 10 per cent of Coles' total profits. Wesfarmers has been concentrating on the turnaround of its supermarkets business since paying $19 billion for the Coles Group in late 2007 in a takeover. The supermarkets business has delivered an impressive turnaround under Ian McLeod.

But Coles has been unable to produce the same performance in liquor, where the first-mover advantage of Woolworths' Dan Murphy's has made the gap hard to bridge.Fresh look

Coles hired former prime ­minister Kevin Rudd's economic adviser Andrew Charlton to become general manager of Coles liquor in 2012.

Mr Durkan's review of the ­liquor business will include a fresh look at all the executives in charge of the various Coles ­operations.

Woolworths' general manager of the Dan Murphy's business, Martin Smith, signalled earlier this week that he intended to open between 10 and 12 stores annually for at least the next six years, which would take the chain up to about 250 stores.

The private equity interest in the Vintage Cellars business comes just weeks after US-based giant Kohlberg Kravis Roberts launched a $3.05 billion proposal to buy out the struggling Treasury Wine Estates. The deal has been firmly rejected by the owner of Penfolds and Rosemount.
