KKR's bid condition tests TWE

Newspaper June 4, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 614 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
Private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts is understood to have made one of the conditions of its $3.05 billion takeover proposal for Treasury Wine Estates that the wine company hit a full-year profit forecast range, putting extra pressure on the owner of Penfolds and Rosemount.

One of the previously undisclosed conditions of the buyout proposal from the United States private equity firm is that the $4.70 cash offer is prefaced on Treasury reaching the full-year earnings range of between $190 million and $210 million for 2013-14.

The earnings hurdle puts extra ­pressure on the Treasury board and new chief executive Mike Clarke to ­generate solid profits in the vital last few weeks of June.

A Treasury spokesman declined to comment on Tuesday, except to say that "the proposal was submitted in con­fidence and we intend to preserve that confidentiality".

Treasury shares continue to trade well above the $5 mark and closed on Tuesday at $5.14, down 6¢ for the day.

Mr Clarke has been urging Treasury staff internally to stay focused on ­delivering a strong sales performance in the final weeks of June and not to be ­distracted by the KKR proposal.

But the prospect of KKR with­drawing its $4.70 cash offer if Treasury misses its earnings guidance is hanging over the company in a difficult ­marketplace.

Treasury and KKR continue to be locked in a stalemate, with Treasury refusing to grant access to KKR to undertake a due diligence process inside the company.

The Treasury board's stance has not changed since May 20 when it first publicly disclosed the KKR ­proposal and rejected it on the basis that it did not reflect the "fundamental value" of the company.

The Treasury board did not divulge what it regarded as a high enough price for it to let KKR in to conduct due diligence.

The original proposal by KKR went to Treasury on April 16 but was only publicly disclosed to the ­market on May 20.

On May 20 Mr Clarke, who started as chief executive in late March, also outlined that he would aggressively pursue $35 million in savings, axe 175 jobs from the 3500-strong workforce and bump up investment in the company's wine brands by 50 per cent.

Mr Clarke also vowed not to chase short-term profits as he outlined there were difficult conditions in a highly competitive market, re-affirming at that point that Treasury's expectation was that it would still reach its ­previously stated forecast.

That forecast, of an earnings before interest, tax and the SGARA accounting standard, was for a guidance range of $190 million to $210 million, and had been made months earlier after a profit downgrade caused by poor trading ­performance in December as the group refused to be part of aggressive pro­motional discounts with major ­liquor chains.

Mr Clarke said on May 20 that "we will not engage in short-term ­practices to the detriment of our long-term objectives".

Interestingly, in a different sector, the standards and compliances firm SAI Global has begun an auction process, opening up a data room to all cred­ible bidders a week after it disclosed that it had received a $1.1 billion buyout proposal from Pacific Equity Partners.

Treasury has more than 73,000 shareholders, and chairman Paul Rayner sent a letter to shareholders more than a week ago outlining the ­reasons why the company had rejected the proposal.

But Mr Rayner also said Treasury would carefully consider proposals that did provide proper compensation for the value of the company.

Most fund managers believe that for the Treasury board to engage it would require an offer well above the $5 mark.

The large volumes of high-quality red wine under the Penfolds brand, that are currently sitting in inventory and due to hit the market over the next two to three years, are considered the main prize for any buyer of the company.

Those red wine stocks are projected to substantially accelerate earnings at Treasury in 2015-16.

Private equity eyes up 'cheap' local firms - Financial Services

Newspaper May 28, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Tom Cowie, and Vanessa Desloires, With Jared Lynch Section: Companies and Markets 623 Words
Page: 20
OpenURL Link
Record low interest rates have prompted cashed-up private equity firms to open their wallets again and circle cheap Australian companies.

Monday's $1.1 billion bid for SAI ­Global from private equity firm Pacific Equity Partners has continued a rush of merger and acquisition activity. According to Dealogic, M&A entry deals are up from $3.01 billion in 2013 to $6.16 billion so far this year, while M&A exit deals total $1.13 billion. Equity capital markets activity, such as floats, are just short of $2 billion in 2014.

So why has the private equity industry thundered back into action, having stuck mainly to the sidelines since 2012?

David Buckland, chief executive of fund manager Montgomery Investment Management, attributed the offloading to a buoyant market. He said private equity firms aimed to time parting with an asset when they could make more money from an initial public offering as opposed to selling to a trade buyer. "They obviously want to do it when the market can take these floats comfortably and that's the case at the moment," Mr Buckland said.

On the buy side, record low interest rates had spurred takeover interest, considering private equity firms normally "aggressively geared" acquisitions, Mr Buckland said. He said there were also perceived bargains in the market, describing United States private equity giant KKR & Co's $3.1 billion bid for Treasury Wine Estates , which owns Penfolds, as "opportunistic".

Treasury thought so too. Although the bid of $4.70 a share was a premium of 27 per cent to where the company traded on April 15, Treasury said in a statement to the ASX that "the proposal does not reflect the fundamental value of the company".

Mr Buckland said private equity firms would circle companies with ­reliable cash flows, given their high gearing, as well as businesses with substantial market share. He tipped the industrial sector would be a target.

Yasser El-Ansary, CEO of Australian Private Equity and Venture Capital Association, said the health and aged-care sectors were also being watched.

On Monday, private equity firm Ironbridge launched the IPO of fertility business Monash IVF Group, with a capital raising of between $267 million and $298 million. "As our population ages and the need for services increases, I think the level of interest in that part of the economy has moved in tandem," Mr El-Ansary said.

"The more private-sector capital that can be channelled into healthcare the better, because the government will not be able to sustain the entire system on its own. It will be very dependent on the role of the private sector in bringing capital and to carry some of the load," he added.

Blue Sky Private Equity's managing director, Tim Wilson, agreed that the health and aged-care sectors were the go-to for private equity firms.

"We've made an investment into a retirement village and we're looking at others in the care space, in early care and aged care. Those segments are performing well," he said.

Mr Wilson said a selling window had opened which relieved pressure from the past five years and that explained the abundance of exit stories for private equity firms. "It's fair to say that right through the last five years there's been a growing pressure from investors to get capital returns," he said.

"Once money starts returning to investors, when there are good returns confidence builds in the private equity sector. Capital starts getting invested. We might have been sitting there longer than we wanted to, but the returns are now coming."

Wolseley Private Equity's managing director, James Todd, who focuses on companies valued between $30 million and $120 million, is noticing more opportunities, partly due to less-captive private equity teams being operated by large banks and insurance companies.

"As equity markets have increased over the past 12 months that has led a range of business owners thinking about the future ownership positions of their companies and has provided the interest," he said.

Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke will start swinging the axe within the nation's biggest winemaker on Monday as 5 per cent of the workforce is culled in the first phase of an earnings turnaround plan.

Treasury's big squeeze - Wine industry

Newspaper May 24, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Perspective 1693 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
CEO Mike Clarke has rejected KKR's takeover bid. But how does he convince short-term investors it's worth hanging on, asks Simon Evans.

The grapes from Adrian Hoffmann's gnarled 100-year-old shiraz vines in the northern Barossa Valley were once a regular part of the precious annual supply for Australia's most famous wine, Penfolds Grange.

But in 2007, Hoffman, a sixth-generation vigneron with 120 hectares of some of the oldest vineyards in the Barossa, decided he didn't want to be enmeshed in a large corporate machine. There was more appreciation and warm inner glow from selling the grapes to prestigious but much smaller, family-owned Barossa wine producers such as Rockford Wines, Two Hands Wines and Glaetzer Wines, which he believes are more patient about the in­evitable ups and downs of the wine industry.

Hoffman is bemused but not surprised by the latest corporate machinations surrounding the Penfolds brand and its stablemates, including Wolf Blass, Wynns and Rosemount. The parent company of those famous wine brands, Treasury Wine Estates, received a $3.05 billion buyout proposal on Tuesday from New York-based private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts. If it is successful, KKR will become the seventh corporate owner of Penfolds since 1976.

Penfolds, with its flagship Grange retailing this year for $785 a bottle, remains robust and a strong performer despite the head-spinning changes in the entities controlling the purse strings, as it passed from one set of owners to the next. One aspect that has suitors salivating this time is that, while Treasury's overall financial performance has been disappointing owing to some poor management decisions and operational controls, there's up to $280 million of higher-quality red wines sitting in barrel halls due to come on the market over the next two years.

KKR, a global pioneer of leveraged buyouts, was the focus of the lively book Barbarians at the Gate, which documented the $25 billion takeover of biscuit maker Nabisco in the United States in the late 1980s. This time it's Penfolds they're after. Analysts estimate the famous label makes about $180 million in profits annually, which equates to about 75 per cent of Treasury's total profits. The Penfolds profits alone could rise to at least $350 million annually in 2015-16, according to Bank of America ­Merrill Lynch, because of the higher levels of luxury wines waiting to be wheeled out.

Hoffman is glad to be away from the ­bigger conglomerates, and regards the latest deal-making as a cautionary tale in an industry notoriously difficult to consistently make money from.Volatile corporate sector

"I saw the corporate sector as a little bit volatile,'' Hoffman says. He bears no malice towards Treasury or Penfolds' previous owners Foster's Group and Southcorp, for whom he also supplied for years.

The volatility of the industry is underlined when Hoffman recalls that in the mid-1980s the precious family holdings now known as Dimchurch vineyards were put up for sale in lean times.

This was at a time of a government-­sponsored vine-pull scheme to eradicate a surplus of grapes across the industry, but the family didn't qualify for the incentives on offer. There were no buyers. That was a ­godsend. Hoffman still marvels at the quality of the grapes from the old vines when weather conditions are right. "Nothing can touch them."

This year was a tough one, with frosts in October last year cutting his output from the 2014 grape ­harvest by 60 per cent. That is part of the cyclical nature of the industry, and Hoffman accepts it is sometimes a hard slog.

Mike Clarke, the fledgling chief executive of Treasury, also faces an uphill battle. He was headhunted by the beleaguered board after running British food company Premier Foods, which makes products including Bisto gravy and Mr Kipling cakes. His first official day on the job was March 31. He outlined a broad five-year plan just over a week later, warning that Treasury had way too many brands, with a bulging portfolio of 83. But then on April 16 a sick feeling welled up in his stomach. KKR, which is being advised by Nomura investment banker John Hanson, made a confidential buyout proposal to the company of $4.70 cash a share. It wasn't revealed publicly until May 20.

KKR made it clear it wanted full access to the internal books so it could do proper due diligence before proceeding. Treasury won't let it in and argues the company is worth more, but won't quantify how much. Well-connected Goldman Sachs investment banker Christian Johnston, a long-time adviser to Treasury and its former parent Foster's Group, is in the trenches with the Treasury board.

There are also question marks about whether Treasury should have let the ­market know immediately it received the proposal.

Treasury announced it to the ASX on May 20, only after KKR, frustrated by Treasury's intransigence, had started talking directly to its bigger institutional shareholders about the proposal.

KKR is no stranger to Clarke. After he left Premier Foods in mid-2013 he remained in the northern hemisphere and helped KKR as an adviser when it wanted some fast-moving consumer goods expertise. At the time, the raider was mulling over making a potential offer for the nutritional drinks brands Lucozade and Ribena. Sinking morale

Clarke has his hands full keeping up morale inside Treasury. As part of a $35 million cost-cutting plan revealed on Tuesday (at the same time as he revealed the KKR bid), he will slice out 175 jobs from the corporate head office in Melbourne and other areas. Straight out of the corporate play book, Clarke shot an email to Treasury's 3500 staff, acknowledging the proposal "may be unsettling" but urging them to concentrate on delivering solid performance over the five weeks leading up to June 30 "in the strongest possible way".

Morale has been sinking since ­Treasury endured derision in mid-2013 when it revealed it would need to destroy six million bottles of under-$10 wine in the US, after being caught with too much stock in the warehouses of third-party distributors.

Why is it that Penfolds has had so many corporate owners? A broader look at the wine industry shows the model of wine companies being listed on the stock exchange in Australia, which held so much promise in the late 1990s as a means of accessing capital, has largely not worked.

In the late 1990s and early 2000s almost 20 different wine companies and wine investment funds were listed on the ASX. In the six years leading up to 2005 most of them disappeared through takeover, frustration from investors over poor returns, or obliteration through financial collapse.

Fund managers demand strong short-term performance to be delivered without fail every six months when results are released. But it is a difficult task in the wine industry where there is significant capital tied up in wine maturing in barrels in the case of higher quality red wine, and processing infrastructure at wineries is only used for three months of each year during the vintage overlaid with the volatility of being an ­agricultural industry.

Robert Hill Smith, the managing director of family-owned Yalumba Wines, has argued passionately for years that "patient capital" is needed over decades to ride the agricultural cycles, and that is in short supply in publicly listed companies.

The string of listed wine companies that emerged onto the ASX 10 to 15 years ago came during a strong growth phase for the industry, super-charged by a low Australian dollar which fuelled strong export sales. There was heavy expansion in vineyard plantings to generate more supply to fill the bottles, but his went too far.Consistent return needed

Industry dynamics were brought undone by a raft of tax-driven managed investment schemes that triggered a rapid surge in vineyard areas in Australia, from 78,000 hectares in 1998 to 143,000 in 2003. More broadly, a global grape oversupply late in the decade caused havoc.

KKR thinks Clarke has the right plans to fix the Treasury business, but argues it needs to be taken away from the spotlight of the public markets and spend at least five years in private hands. In this way, reinvestment in brands will have time to work and cost-­cutting will flow through to deliver a more consistent and sustainable return on capital.

Clarke is a huge fan of the Penfolds brand and refers to it as one of the brands "that we haven't mucked up in the past", but is frustrated at the lack of investment in brand marketing for most of the other sleepers such as Wynns.

So, from 1976 and NSW brewer Tooth & Co through to the 1980s corporate raider John Spalvins and his Adelaide Steamship Company, through to SA Brewing, then Southcorp, then Foster's Group and then as the standalone Treasury, Penfolds has been on a rollercoaster ride.

Whether KKR will get the opportunity to perform corporate surgery on Treasury and become the new owner depends on the mood of big investment funds, which may be growing tired of fluctuating fortunes and the long lead times of good wine and instead take the money and run.

TWE 'tardy' disclosure cops flak - M&A Investors hit out at one-month delay in disclosing KK Roffer

Newspaper May 24, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague Section: Companies and Markets 423 Words
Page: 36
OpenURL Link
Treasury Wine Estates says it has the support of the majority of its largest shareholders over its handling of a $3.05 billion takeover approach by private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts.

It comes as some investors hit out at a month-long delay in revealing the bid and called on Treasury to engage in ­discussions with KKR and extract a higher offer. "If someone has put a price on the table, a limited due ­diligence is not out of the question," one ­shareholder said.

Treasury waited 33 days before telling the market on Tuesday that it had received an indicative, non-binding offer from KKR. It said it informed the market after the private equity giant began secretly approaching shareholders about its offer, a move Treasury believed created a risk that confidentiality had been lost.

KKR became frustrated at a lack of cooperation from Treasury on an initial $4.70 cash per share proposal and while it approached some Treasury shareholders it maintains "appropriate confidentiality protocols" were in place.

A Treasury shareholder and senior institutional fund manager lashed out at Treasury's disclosure. "If you get a genuine approach from a credible place with a price attached to it, my reading of the listing rules is you should disclose that," the fund manager said.

"Juxtapose it to their position, and their actual disclosure, saying they were not talking to Pernod Ricard or they were not talking to Constellation. It's ludicrous. The whole time they knew about the KKR approach."

KRR's offer has put Treasury in play but China's Bright Food Group ­distanced itself from speculation it was eyeing the Penfolds and Wolf Blass wine­maker. A spokesman told Bloomberg Bright Foods had not had internal discussions about whether to bid for the winemaker.

Treasury maintains it has not breached disclosure rules because KKR's offer was incomplete and ­confidential. Treasury chairman Paul Rayner declined to comment on Friday but a Treasury spokesman said the company had been in discussions with the majority of its largest shareholders and had their support. "They fully ­support our position on how and why the market was informed," the ­spokesman said.

The board would seriously consider any offer put forward to the company, and if those proposals "represent fair value", Treasury "would be prepared to open the books to due diligence", he said. A number of shareholders and analysts believe KKR needs to pay more than $5 a share to win the company.

Another fund manager earlier this week said it was "pretty poor" Treasury failed to disclose the bid when it was first made. "If you get approached with the stock at $3.70, I would say that was a material bid," the fund manager said.

Shares advanced for the third week in a row as a flurry of corporate deal activity and initial public offerings helped the market rally from a 1.3 per cent fall at the start of the week following a slump in the iron ore price.

The S&P/ASX 200 Index added 0.3 per cent over the week to close at 5492 points, while the broader All Ordinaries Index gained 0.2 per cent to 5470.3, as investors shrugged off worries about slower demand growth from China and questions lingered about whether the sustained bull run remains justified.

"Growth prospects for Australian corporate earnings are improving and are supportive of current market val­uations," Alphinity Investment Management portfolio manager Johan Carlberg said.

On Friday, the benchmark index added 0.2 per cent, with a solid debut from the biggest initial public offering to hit the boards in 2014. Maintenance and catering services company Spotless Group Holdings lifted 7.2 per cent to $1.72. Intueri Education Group rose 8.7 per cent on its IPO to $2.37.

Telecommunications was the best-performing sector over the week, as Telstra Corporation advanced 5.38 per cent to a nine-year high of $5.38.

Resources giant BHP Billiton lost 1.1 per cent to $37.65, while main rival Rio Tinto shed 1.2 per cent to $60.54.

Junior goldminer Regis Resources was the worst-performing stock, dumping 25 per cent on Friday after an operations update and revision to 2015 guidance disappointed analysts.

Energy stocks were mostly lower as China and Russia announced a major gas deal. Woodside Petroleum fell 0.3 per cent to $41.61 after abandoning an Israeli joint venture, leaving investors uncertain about how it plans to use the allocated capital.

Treasury Wine Estates, owner of Penfolds, was the best-performing stock, climbing 26.72 per cent to $5.17 after the company revealed it rejected a takeover bid from private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts last month.

Retailers Woolworths and Wesfarmers were both modestly higher.

KKR's $3b funding needs inside access

Newspaper May 22, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague and Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 795 Words
Page: 21
OpenURL Link
United States private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts has full financing in place for its $3.05 billion buyout offer for Treasury Wine Estates but is pushing for immediate access to the wine group's books because the funding is predicated on it doing due diligence.

But Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke and his fellow directors, who spent Wednesday locked in a board meeting, are refusing to budge on access as they finalise plans to begin cutting 175 staff from Monday in a desperate push to slim down the nation's biggest winemaker, after rejecting a takeover proposal from KKR.

Mr Clarke, a former Kraft and Premier Foods executive, is under pressure to secure support quickly from long-suffering shareholders after it emerged KKR began secretly approaching investors about the merits of its conditional takeover proposal.

The private equity firm had become frustrated at a lack of cooperation from Treasury on an initial proposal put to the company on April 16.

It comes as analysts encouraged Treasury to engage with KKR, noting the conditional $4.70 price was much higher than their valuations.

Credit Suisse says without the takeover proposal, Treasury is worth just $3.15 per share.

However, a number of analysts and shareholders believe KKR needs to lift the offer price to beyond $5 to secure the group. Merrill Lynch has estimated Treasury could be worth up to $6.50 if its assets were broken up and sold.

Shares in Treasury, which owns brands including Penfolds, Lindemans and Rosemount, continued to rise on Wednesday, gaining as much as 6 per cent before closing up 5.6 per cent, or 27¢, to $5.07, a seven-month high.

KKR's corporate advisers Nomura made fresh requests on Wednesday for access to the company's books for a formal due diligence process, but continued to be rebuffed on the basis that the Treasury board believes the company is worth more than $4.70.

KKR is understood to have its financing in place, subject to due diligence.

Treasury has never been short of interested buyers. Treasury's former owner, Foster's Group, knocked back an approach worth as much as $2.7 billion from Cerebus Capital for the wine business in 2010. It's understood Constellation Brands has also eyed the group's US assets over the years.

It also emerged KKR's interest in Treasury gathered pace in May 2011 when Treasury split from former parent Foster's Group in a demerger.

It has been linked to a potential tilt alongside TPG in 2010, when Treasury was part of Foster's.

Treasury, which was virtually debt-free at that time in a deliberate strategy by the Foster's board to give it as much chance of success as possible, traded at $3.45 on its first day as a listed entity. The stock has been as high as $6.47 in May 2013 before a succession of operational stumbles in the US and Australia.

Mr Clarke, who left London to take up the Treasury job on March 31, quickly laid the ground work for hefty cuts, telling the market on April 8 "there is a lot that needs to be fixed" and warned jobs would go. On Tuesday he was forced to bring forward firm details of his plan, including $35 million in savings and boosting marketing spending by up to 50 per cent in a bid to improve consumer demand for its brands.

The KKR approach could make it more difficult for Mr Clarke to convince shareholders of the merits of sticking around for his five-year journey,especially after enduring a roller coaster ride for the past 15 years as numerous chief executives extolled the virtue of a long-term strategy. Citigroup analyst Gino Rossi, who lifted his target price from $3.80 to $4.70, said Mr Clarke's turnaround strategy looked "sensible and well thought out" but executing it could prove difficult. This meant a higher offer from KKR would be a good alternative for shareholders.

"Treasury Wine is a complex business and the wine industry remains challenging," Mr Rossi said.

"Mr Clarke now has the added pressure of the KKR bid, which will mean he is not as free to trade off short-term profits for long-term benefit. When factoring in risk, a KKR bid at a slightly higher price seems a good alternative for shareholders."

One fund manager scotched suggestions KKR was being opportunistic in its approach by trying to secure Treasury as it desperately tries to achieve bullish earnings guidance.

"I think they waited until they had a found a buyer in the US for Treasury's assets there," said the fund manager. who did not want to be named. "They could then work for a few years on Penfolds and the Australian business and relist Penfolds or a trade sale."

It is understood KKR has no immediate plans to carve up Treasury's assets.

Credit Suisse analyst Larry Gandler lifted his target price to $4.70 a share. In a research note he said the $4.70 proposal "is so far above our $3.15 valuation that we think the board should engage with KKR''.

Poor tasting notes have not dissuaded private equity firm KKR & Co from launching a $3.1 billion offer for Australia's Treasury Wine Estates, one of the world's largest pure-play winemakers. And new chief executive Michael Clarke plans to improve things further with a 50 per cent increase in consumer marketing spend, to be financed by $35 million of cost cuts.

The attractions for a buyer lie in two areas. First, restore TWE's margins; second, push its brands, including Lindeman's, Wolf Blass and Penfolds. Half of the company's sales are in Australia and New Zealand, but Euromonitor says this is one of the weakest regions in terms of forecast wine market growth (less than 1 per cent a year over the next four years).

North America, Latin America and Asia, growing at 2 per cent, 3 per cent and 6 per cent respectively, are where the action is. There are opportunities for anyone with the finance and patience to push the brands hard.

KKR's $4.7 per share offer gives TWE an enterprise value of 11 times next year's forecast earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation. Large drinks groups, such as Constellation and Pernod Ricard, are all on 13 to 15 times. Even if KKR fails, other tasters will soon start sniffing around TWE's cellars.

Treasury takes defensive steps - Wine - TWE to cut jobs, costs to fend off $3.05b KKR bid

Newspaper May 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans, and Julie-anne Sprague Section: Companies and Markets 809 Words
Page: 13
OpenURL Link
New Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke will strip out $35 million in costs and axe 175 jobs from the global wine giant that owns brands, including Penfolds and Rosemount, in a desperate push to kick-start growth and save it from falling to private equity predator Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, which has made a $3.05 billion take­over bid for the troubled business.

Mr Clarke, who became Treasury's sixth chief executive in four years after arriving from the United Kingdom just seven weeks ago, is in a race against time as United States-based KKR seeks to capitalise on shareholder discontent with the Treasury board and a string of operational disasters to swoop on Australia's most famous wine brands.

"We are moving from first gear into fifth gear in this company,'' Mr Clarke said. KKR denied it breached any confidentiality agreements by going direct to some institutional shareholders in Treasury with its $3.05 billion takeover approach, after becoming frustrated at the progress of secret talks.

It originally delivered the takeover proposal to Treasury on April 16.

The company said on Tuesday that in the last week its advisers from Nomura held discussions with certain shareholders on "a wall-crossed, confidential basis and subject to appropriate confidentiality protocols''.

It is believed that KKR is concentrating its efforts on TWE's ffshore institutional investors.

Earlier on Tuesday, Treasury revealed it received an approach from KKR more than a month ago and had been in secret talks about the offer since then. There had been "engagement'' with KKR but that stopped when Treasury found out that KKR had been talking to institutions about the bid, Mr Clarke said.

One of the factors that had prevented deep engagement with KKR was Treasury's own heavy restructuring plans.

"We're doing a shake-up in how this company will be run,'' he said.

But Treasury only disclosed the approach on Tuesday morning, when it urged shareholders to take no action and also revealed it was taking a knife to costs across the company in an ­aggressive $35 million cost-cutting blitz, while at the same time bumping up spending on its wine brand marketing by 50 per cent.

Mr Clarke said the cost-cutting, which would bring $35 million in savings in 2014-15, would involve scrapping 5 per cent of positions in Treasury's global workforce of 3500.

The savings would be reinvested in consumer marketing behind the 20 top brands of the 83 inside Treasury's portfolio. Penfolds was the prized asset that needed to be worked harder still, while Wolf Blass and Wynns would also receive more consumer marketing investment.

Mr Clarke said Penfolds was "one of the brands in our portfolio that we haven't mucked up in the past''.

KKR's bid increases the pressure on Mr Clarke to win support from shareholders for his turnaround plan. He wants shareholders to look past the next six weeks and focus on the next five years, suggesting he is far from confident on delivering fiscal 2014 earnings numbers set for him by previous management and the current board.

"The longer-term value of our company is not going to be dependent on the next six weeks trading results. That I feel very clear on."

On April 8, Mr Clarke recommitted the company to achieving earnings of between $190 million and $210 million, which requires more than $145 million to be achieved in the second half, outperforming the previous year.

The KKR bid is a cash offer priced at $4.70 a share and values the Treasury business at $3.05 billion. Treasury shares jumped almost 20 per cent to beyond the offer price in early trade on Tuesday and by the close had finished 18 per cent higher, up 73¢ to $4.80.

"I think KKR will get this done," one analyst said.

"It will need to be above $5 but I think the job is very hard for Mike Clarke now he has to keep one eye on KKR."

One fund manager said it was "pretty poor" Treasury failed to disclose the bid when it was first made.

"If you get approached with the stock at $3.70 I would say that was a material bid," the fund manager said.

It continues a rollercoaster ride for shareholders in Treasury, which was split off from former parent Foster's Group in May 2011 to be a stand-alone listed wine company on the ASX.

It also marks a tumultuous introduction to Treasury for Mr Clarke who took the helm on March 31 after being recruited from the United Kingdom, where he was chief executive of Premier Foods, which makes Hovis bread and Bisto gravy.

KKR said on Tuesday its offer represented a 27¢ premium to the Treasury's $3.69 closing price on the day before the proposal was put to the company.

It also said "has not executed a non-disclosure agreement with TWE, and access to company records per our requests has not been provided''. In practical terms, it has been rebuffed by Treasury, which won't let KKR come in and do serious due diligence.

Strength in Asian markets, such as Japan, Hong Kong and India, helped buoy the local bourse in afternoon trade. At the Australian close, the ­Bangkok stock exchange was trading 0.9 per cent lower after the army imposed martial law in Thailand.

Local shares got a boost following the release of minutes from the Reserve Bank of Australia's May policy meeting.

The central bank board said it intends to keep the official cash rate at a record-low 2.5 per cent for "some time yet" to support an economy likely to slow amid federal budget cuts and softer Chinese demand for iron ore.

Treasury Wine Estates, the owner of Penfolds and Lindemans wines, also helped lift the index as it posted the ­biggest percentage gain among the ASX 200, up 17.9 per cent to $4.80 after rejecting a $4.70-a-share takeover offer from private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts, fuelling speculation of a higher bid.

The market remained buoyant despite a private survey showing ­consumer confidence fell to its worst level since the global financial crisis in 2008 after the release of the federal budget last week.

The monthly ANZ/Roy Morgan measure of consumer ­sentiment dropped 14 per cent over the past four weeks on budget worries.

Barclays economist Kieran Davies said he was hopeful the drop in ­confidence will prove temporary as it is offset by an ongoing improvement in employment but presented a downside risk to consumer spending.

The big four banks were mixed. Commonwealth Bank of Australia added 0.3 per cent to $79.83, ANZ Banking Group gained 0.3 per cent to $32.75, and National Australia Bank lifted 0.5 per cent to $33.10.

Westpac Banking Corporation fell 0.3 per cent to $33.68.

ASX debutante Genworth Mortgage Insurance Australia soared 13.2 per cent on listing to $3.

Stocks in the mining sector were more robust than expected after the spot price for iron ore, landed in China, dropped 2.2 per cent to $US98.50 a tonne, below the psychological ­threshold of $US100 per tonne to its lowest price since September 2012.

Resources giant BHP Billiton edged up 0.05 per cent to $37.45, while main rival Rio Tinto slipped 0.08 per cent to $60.05.

Iron ore miner Fortescue Metals Group jumped 3.9 per cent to $4.54 as exploratory drilling showed an additional 1.16 billion tonnes of ore in its Greater Solomon project. "Iron ore producers rallied on Tuesday despite the fall in the commodity price overnight because they had already sold off over the past week in anticipation of the news," Equity Trustees chief investment officer George Boubouras said.

Mr Boubouras forecasts the iron ore price to end 2014 at $US115 per tonne, but expects significant volatility in the meantime as investors react to mixed signals from the supply, demand, and financing factors that influence pricing.

"Our only exposure to iron ore is through the three biggest players, BHP, Rio and Brazil's Vale, because they are the least volatile," Mr Boubouras said.

Smaller iron ore miners also rallied, including Mount Gibson Iron, up 2.8 per cent at 74.5¢.

Hormone therapy maker Acrux Ltd was the worst-performing stock in the ASX 200, down 6.4 per cent to a five-year low at 88.5¢. The stock has suffered a sharp selloff in recent months after the US Food and Drug Administration ordered a report into testosterone treatments. On Tuesday, Macquarie analyst Craig Collie reiterated a "neutral" on the stock with a 12-month target price of $1.

With global shares riding high against macro-economic backdrop of low growth and low interest rates investors have been grappling over whether better value is to found in stocks or bonds.

Treasury under the pump on disclosure

Newspaper May 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 618 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
The vexed issue of when boards should disclose takeover approaches has flared yet again as revelations emerge that Treasury Wine Estates had been in secret talks with private equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts for weeks while publicly hosing down speculation about other potential deals.

Earlier in the year there was controversy over department store chain David Jones failing to disclose for almost three months that Myer had made a full proposal for a merger of equals on October 28 last year.

The Australian Financial Review revealed the approach in late January. The issue was complicated further by admissions that David Jones directors had been allowed to buy shares in Myer after it lodged its proposal and before a decision was made to reject it.

KKR, with corporate advisers from Nomura in its corner, first approached Treasury on Wednesday April 16 about a cash offer. But Treasury didn't disclose it publicly to "preserve the confidentiality of the proposal'' and because premature disclosure would hurt Treasury shareholders. Treasury chief executive Mike Clarke said late on Tuesday the proposal "was subject to a number of different deliverables" and didn't need to be disclosed at that time.

That policy didn't stop Treasury from going on the front foot when speculating on potential corporate transactions involving its troubled US wine business. On May 2, it told the ASX French drinks giant Pernod Ricard, which owns Jacob's Creek, had not approached it about buying Treasury's US business. On May 15, it lodged a similar response to the ASX in relation to speculation about interest from New York-based alcoholic beverages giant Constellation Brands, saying it "had not been approached by and is not in discussions with Constellation Brands regarding the company's US business".

But behind the scenes KKR and Treasury were discussing a transaction involving the company and its brands including Penfolds, Rosemount, Lindeman's, Wolf Blass, Wynns and Seppelt. KKR vowed on Tuesday it hadn't breached any confidentiality agreements by going to institutional shareholders in Treasury on a "wall-crossed, confidential basis". Treasury claimed KKR had forced its hand by going to shareholders. Mr Clarke described it as a "leak".

The ASX points to its Listing Rule 3.1 about continuous disclosure as a guide, which states that once an entity "becomes aware of any information concerning it that a reasonable person would expect, to have a material effect on the price or value of the entity's securities, the entity must immediately tell ASX that information".

Interestingly, KKR set the ball rolling in late 2006 for the $18 billion takeover of Coles Group by Wesfarmers a year later. KKR came knocking with a proposal, which was quickly disclosed to the ASX and which set in train a torturous auction process and more than a year of instability at Coles.

Expect a serious bun-fight over Treasury's non-disclosure and the fact that its shares were on the rise in early May as speculation about a separate deal in the US bubbled up. This has implications for benchmark takeover premiums as takeover battles are fought over weeks and months. There is also the question about how skilled private equity operators are when it comes to the wine industry. Wine is notoriously capital-hungry and private equity firm CHAMP have their hands full with Accolade Wines, which sells Hardys, Houghton and Banrock Station.

CHAMP bought 80 per cent of Accolade for $290 million in 2011 from New York-based Constellation Brands, which retained 20 per cent. Constellation became the owner in 2003 when it made a $1.9 billion takeover for what was then known as BRL Hardy in 2003.

Accolade two months ago bolstered its balance sheet with a $US300 million new financing package extended by GE Capital International.

Treasury and Accolade have close links. Treasury embarked on a deal in late 2013 to make and package wine for Accolade its facilities in California. In mid-2012 the companies ­consolidated their international bottling operations.

Bad luck in Australia haunts KKR - Investment - Funds manager swears off Australia after string ofmisses

Newspaper May 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Brian Robins Section: Companies and Markets 460 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
"Barbarians at the gate" may be the sobriquet associated with the leveraged buyout fund manager KKR in the wake of its controversial $US25 billion purchase of RJR Nabisco 25 years ago – a record value which was not challenged for many years.

But in Australia, a more accurate description could be "always a ­bridesmaid". The private equity giant's $3 billion tilt at Treasury Wine Estates – owner of Penfolds – has thrust KKR back into the spotlight, but in reality the firm has a long history in Australia, and a mixed track record in getting deals over the line.

KKR burst onto the local scene in 2006 when it paid a mouthwatering $1.8 billion for the Cleanaway unit of Brambles with the waste assets onsold for $1.25 billion a year later to Trans­pacific Industries. It retained the BIS unit, so KKR probably came out ahead on the play.

But successful bids since have been scant.

In late 2006 KKR was unveiled as a potential bidder for Coles Myer, but board eventually agreed to a weaker offer from Wesfarmers.

Ditto KKR's $1.75 billion pitch in 2010 for fund manager Perpetual, which also failed. Perpetual is now valued in the market at $2.1 billion.

Then there was the bid – 50 minutes late – for a block of 7 per cent of Transpacific Industries which was auctioned in 2012. KKR bid 66¢ for the shares, which were sold for 62¢.

"The KKR bid was received some 50 minutes after the book had closed and I'd compare the situation to ­someone trying to bid at a house auction after close," a Transpacific official said at the time.

At around the same time it also looked at Pacific Brands, but decided to keep its hands in its pocket. Likewise with Pact Group, which floated onto the ASX six months ago.

There has been recurring talk of a float by BIS but the downturn in the mining industry has put paid to that.

So, it has kicked plenty of tyres, but to little effect. In between, there was a joint venture with Kerry Stokes, with KKR putting $735 million into a joint venture with the businessman which took ­control of Channel 7, Pacific Magazines and its local online joint venture with Yahoo!.

That venture wound up a year ago, but figuring out whether KKR made or lost money is all but impossible, with the upswing in the Australian dollar against the greenback over the seven years of this venture likely to have seen the US firm come out ahead – just.

Yet at least – unlike some other ­leveraged buy-out investors – it would appear not to have lost money on its local forays, unlike CVC Capital, which lost a thumping $US1.8 billion on an ill-judged buyout of the Nine Network.

Just this week, it raised $US3.5 billion ($3.8 billion) for a new Asian fund, but its disastrous experience Down Under has prompted the firm to vow never to return to Australia.

Grange Hermitage became final drop for a premier

Newspaper May 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Sophie Boyd Section: Companies and Markets 412 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
Penfolds wine has long been the jewel of Treasury Wine Estates' crown, and a rejection of Kohlberg Kravis Roberts's $3.1 billion takeover bid shows TWE is unwilling to part with its prized drop.

While TWE owns 80 brands in total, Penfolds is central to their operation as a premium brand that can deliver strong margins and provide a halo for the entire portfolio of wines.

Penfolds began life as a family business which produced wine as a medicinal tonic to treat anaemia.

Englishman Dr Christopher Penfold and his wife Mary bought the Magill estate in 1844 and Mary Penfold took up the task of managing and curating the wine.

By 1870, the 60 acres of several grape varieties was producing over one-third of South Australia's wine. Mary Penfold remained at the helm of Penfold&Co, working in partnership with her son-in-law, Thomas Hyland, and cellar manager Joseph Gillard.

In 1980, a subsidiary of the Adelaide Steamship Group purchased the winery and a decade later, it was sold to SA Brewery Holdings, which amalgamated all its wine holdings under the Penfolds banner.

SA Brewery was broken into three separate groups before its wine arm, Southcorp Wines, was sold to Foster's in 2005. TWE was spun off from Foster's in 2011, taking Penfold with it.

Penfolds and TWE are no strangers to defying the market and following their own direction. Since its origins, self-determinism was integral to the success of its flagship Grange.

From its experimental beginnings in 1951, the Grange Hermitage was divisive, with a testing of the 1951-1956 Granges resulting in criticism by wine experts and management alike.

In 1957, head office directed chief winemaker Max Schubert to end production of the wine but in defiance, Schubert continued making it in secret until the 1959 Grange achieved general acceptance and the board quietly reversed their ban. The 1955 Grange, initially rejected by all critics, went on to win 50 gold medals before Penfolds retired it from shows.

But its controversial beginnings are nothing compared to the recent notoriety achieved by Penfolds' 1959 Grange Hermitage: the wine that destroyed a premier.

Australia's most infamous wine was thrust into the political and media light after becoming the centrepiece of an ICAC investigation. Former NSW premier Barry O'Farrell stood down after evidence showed he did not declare the $3000 gift given to him by AWH's Nick Di Girolamo.

TWE said it would carefully consider any future proposals that maximise shareholder value, but for now, Penfolds remains securely TWE's from "1844 to evermore", as its label says.

Treasury is ripe for the picking

Newspaper May 21, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Michael Smith Section: Companies and Markets 1234 Words
Page: 32
OpenURL Link
The performance of Treasury Wine Estates (TWE) over the past year has had more than a few shareholders reaching for the bottle so it should not be a huge surprise that an opportunistic private equity firm has as come knocking.

The winemaker, which produces Penfolds and Lindeman's, dropped a bombshell on Tuesday with news that it had knocked back a $3.1 billion takeover offer from Kohlberg Kravis Roberts (KKR).

The sudden decision to reveal an approach made more than a month ago is even more intriguing given the company has spent the past week denying reports it had received approaches from Constellation Capital and others for its struggling US operations.

The trigger was KKR's decision to talk a number of TWE's shareholders about the approach, which chief executive Michael Clarke describes as a "leak" which obligated the company to inform the market.

TWE has been a constant subject of takeover speculation since the day it was spun out of Foster's in 2011 but there is a sense of inevitability it will be taken out this time round.

KKR is the latest in a string of private equity and potential trade buyers linked to a company which has lost the confidence of investors following a string of profit downgrades, a pricing stuff-up over Christmas and a messy management transition under chairman Paul Raynor.

Another private equity firm, Cerberus, offered Foster's $2.3 billion to $2.7 billion for the wine business when it was still owned by Foster's in 2010. TPG, which made a packet selling Beringer to Foster's in 2000, and TPG have also reportedly looked at the company.

More recently, the talk has focused TWE's troubled US operations. TWE last week played down reports Constellation Brands and premium beverage group Pernod Ricard were looking at its North American business. At the time, TWE did not see the need to mention the fact that KKR had made an offer for the entire business.

That approach was rejected, but the company looks riper than ever for a takeover bid. This is reflected by an 18 per cent jump in the company's share price which closed at $4.80 on Tuesday following news that KKR had come knocking.

Comparisons will be made to bread maker Goodman Fielder which last week bowed to a modest takeover offer from two Asian investors who took advantage of the company's weak share price. A string of earnings downgrades and lack of confidence in the future meant key Goodman Fielder investors were willing to sell their stakes even though the premium was not as high as they would have liked.

The difference with a suitor such as KKR is that the private equity firm does not have a track record of engaging in hostile takeover bids. It prefers to avoid bad blood and negotiate on good terms with a target board, although talking to shareholders has had the opposite affect.

KKR made a non-binding and indicative $4.70 cash bid for the company, one that would imply a value of $3.05 billion. That was rejected by TWE on April 16 when the stock was trading a $3.70, which meant the offer represented a 27 per cent premium at the time.

While both sides agreed to keep the engagement confidential, TWE spat the dummy after KKR spoke to a number of its investors about the offer. Both sides have different opinions about whether the cone of silence has been breached or not.

TWE chief executive Clarke says by doing so KKR broke the terms of that confidentiality and it decided to announce the approach to ensure the market was informed. In a conference call late on Tuesday he said TWE did engage with KKR and suggested there would have been continued dialogue if there had not been a "leak".

KKR disputes this, saying there was never a formal non-disclosure agreement with TWE in the first place. Second, talks between its advisers and the shareholders were on a confidential basis and would not have been made public anyway.

Whatever the motive was for going public, the process highlights the minefield companies face when it comes to disclosure obligations. While it was not technically obligated to disclose the approach, the company selectively left out the fact it had been sitting on a $3 billion offer while denying talk Constellation was looking at the US operations.

A hastily issued press release, which initially said the proposal was received in 2016, highlights how quickly things moved from being an amicable discussion to a dispute over confidentiality.

The key now is TWE's shareholders who can pressure Raynor to engage with the private equity firm, hopefully to agitate for a higher offer. Clarke appeared to be leaving the door open for further talks.

There are several schools of thought. The company could take the money and run, sell of the US operations or pursue Clarke's promise to turn things around.

The decision to disclose the KKR approach also forced Clarke to fast-forward an announcement around cost cutting and plans to ramp up its consumer market spending. It is targeting $35 million in cost savings in financial 2015, which will involve cutting 5 per cent of its 3500-plus workforce. The company has not exactly issued an earnings downgrade but it may as well have given the downbeat tone of Clarke's commentary, which avoided committing to the numbers either way.

The next six weeks of trading are crucial for the company's outlook, but at the same time Clarke wants investors to take a longer-term approach when judging the company's potential value. UBS is forecasting 2014 earning of $182 million compared with TWE's guidance of $190 million to $210 million.

Clarke's strategy rests on investing in the company's luxury brands and has identified spending priorities for 20 of its 83 brands. He still views the US business as a growth platform.

It is still early days for Clarke, who has only been in the top job for seven weeks and Tuesday's conference call with investors will do little to resurrect confidence in the company. He says there are still some other structural changes he is working on but not ready to announce yet, while trading in Australia is challenging.

The strategic rationale behind KKR's interest is TWE's strong luxury brands with Penfolds the absolute jewel in the crown. The private equity firm does not view the company as an Australian-specific acquisition but likes TWE for the ability to sell its brands more effectively into Asia and China in particular. A break-up is inevitable under private equity ownership and there appears to be no shortage of buyers for the US operations.

There is clearly scope for KKR to go higher. Morgan Stanley analysts note that wine assets tend to trade on high multiples given their strategic value. The median of the past 24 wine transactions is 13.2x trailing EBITDA, which imply $5.18 a share for TWE. The bid will put the spotlight back on to Raynor-installed director Warwick Every-Burns as an interim chief executive after sacking former chief David Dearie in mid-2013. This followed an embarrassing $160 million write-down on the US business where six million bottles of low-priced wine were destroyed.

Goldman Sachs is advising Treasury Wine, while Nomura is advising KKR.

Unable to afford to take out Duet Group in a takeover bid, Spark Infrastructure is positioning itself for next round of consolidation in the energy space by buying a strategic stake in its rival.

Spark boss Rick Francis has been keen to do a big deal since he came on board in 2012. After losing out on the NSW desalination plant sale and passing on the Moomba-Adelaide gas line deal, Francis has been scouting the market for deals.

While some analysts question Spark's motives, the deal looks like it gives them a seat at the table if there is another round of consolidation.

Out of the box - WINE - ANNIVERSARY

Newspaper May 2, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague Section: Life & Leisure 1025 Words
Page: 7
OpenURL Link
Wine label Penfolds is celebrating 170 years by releasing a shiraz with a difference, writes Julie-anne Sprague.

Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Michael Clarke is taking a cold approach to the winemaker's brands as part of a fresh strategy to revive earnings for the world's largest stand-alone listed wine company.

Clarke has no emotional attachment to brands, instead focusing on wine labels, cutting costs, and improving consumer marketing.

However, he does makes an exception.

"If I'm totally honest, I am emotional about Penfolds," Clarke told investors last month in his first public comments since being recruited to improve Treasury's fortunes. "Thank God we've got that in our portfolio."

This month is crucial for Penfolds and Treasury. Penfolds is the engine room of Treasury's profit and critical for the group to achieve full-year earnings guidance.

The annual May release of Penfolds' icon wines, including Grange, is always met with great fanfare but this year comes with a huge weight of expectation.

Penfolds is without a 2008 Grange, the wine that received a perfect score from Robert Parker's Wine Advocate, triggering – to the disgust of some wine retailers – price rises before it even hit the retail shelves this time last year. The 2009 – yes, priced the same as the 2008 at $785 a bottle – will no doubt be good, but it's no 2008.

At the same time, China's demand for luxury wines is faltering.

Recent figures released by Wine Australia show exports of premium red wines – or wines priced about $7.50 a litre – have slipped 11 per cent in the year to March, compared with a 6 per cent drop for all bottled wine exports to China.Rabbit out of a hat

So how does Penfolds pull a rabbit out of its hat, and generate fresh excitement and all-important sales?

With a parcel of fruit from its old vines at block 3C in the Barossa Valley's Kalimna vineyard and help from the Queen's nephew, David Albert Charles Armstrong-Jones, Viscount Linley.

David Linley, the son of Princess Margaret, chairs his bespoke furniture and cabinetry company Linley. He is also chairman at auction house Christie's UK.

Linley been commissioned to design special wooden boxes for a special release wine – the 2010 Bin 170 Kalimna shiraz, a single vineyard wine not made since 1973.

Launched by Penfolds winemaker Peter Gago in London, on May 1, at $1800 a standard bottle it is the most expensive bottled wine released by Penfolds in its 170-year history ( its most expensive release is the $168,000 ampoule, released in 2012).

Launching the shiraz in London, the birthplace of Penfolds founder Christopher Rawson Penfold, lends old world charm to the new world producer celebrating its 170th year.

The original Bin 170, released only once before in 1973, was meant to be blended with Bin 169 Coonawarra cabernet sauvignon to create a Bin 60A. Instead it was released as a stand-alone wine.

This wine has been made many times since and, while it has never been commercially released, it has occasionally been entered in wine shows, winning a swag of gold medals for Penfolds.

Usually, the wine from Block 3C is blended for Grange.Limited supply

Penfolds doesn't usually reveal how limited its supplies of elite wines are. It has made a rare exception for the 2010 Bin 170 Kalimna shiraz, which will be sold in imperials, magnums and standard bottles. All up, there are just 5544 bottles.

Of those, there are 11 imperials (six litres) – with only seven released commercially for $65,500. There are about 5000 magnums, priced at $4800 a bottle.

The bottles are housed in bespoke wooden cases designed by Linley. The imperial box features a compass and can only be opened with the combination of the longitude and latitude co-ordinates of Penfolds' Magill Estate.

Linley began the design in June 2013. The boxes took about 80 hours to craft and a further 40 hours to polish, plate and engrave; the time involved was largely due to the complexity of the engineered locks. The lid is customised with the buyer's name accompanying the bottle number.

The cachet of special packaging is usually reserved for a wine other than shiraz, says Phil Hude, who runs Armadale Cellars in Melbourne.

"Champagne has always relied on packaging to offer something different," Hude says. "It creates interest and some hype. When Penfolds did that ampoule they may have only made a dozen but look how much coverage they got for it. It was worth millions."

Vince Salpietro designs cellars for wealthy executives, including BRW Rich lister Nigel Satterley. He expects the wine to flow to the cellars of Chinese multimillionaires and says Penfolds are taking a leaf out of the marketing playbook mastered by Bordeaux first growth producers.Captivating story

"Before you have even tasted it, you are so into the story of where the wine is from, how it has been crafted and everything else that it can taste like vinegar and you'll love it," Salpietro says.

Such is the power of luxury.

But Gago is staking his reputation of what's in the bottle. And it's not vinegar. Unblended and matured for 16 months in French oak, 55 per cent new, the 2010 Bin 170 Kalimna Shiraz can be cellared until 2055, long after Penfolds turns 200 in 2044.

"Why does this luxury stuff belong to the French? Why doesn't little old Australia play with the big boys?" Gago argues. "With our reds, we are right up there."

Critics will jump on this release as being a gimmick. A Bin 170 released as Penfolds celebrates its 170th anniversary?

Gago is quick to point out that the wine, which has been in bottle since 2011, was supposed to be released last year – proof that the wine is the real deal.

"It didn't have the name 170 but it was wine we had kept to one side," Gago explains. "It was meant to be released last year but someone around the table said guess what? We are turning 170 in 2014 . It wasn't purpose built to be a commemorative but it couldn't have worked better."

He says deciding whether the wine could make a standalone product, worthy of special status, is challenging. "If you were to listen to the bean counters there'd be a special bin release every three years," Gago says.

Wine buyers in Australia can pick up bottles at Penfolds' cellar door, Langton's or at Duty Free stores.

New Grange will sell for $785 a bottle

Newspaper April 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: News 407 Words
Page: 3
OpenURL Link
Who would pay $785 for a bottle of wine? Many, according to ­Campbell Stott, merchandise manager for Woolworths' liquor chain, Dan Murphy's, which is limiting customers to two bottles of the new Penfolds Grange which goes on sale on Thursday.

A hot spell which affected the 2009 vintage has cut the amount of Grange on sale this year.

Wine experts agree the 2009 vintage isn't the highest quality compared with other vintages, which are sold five years after the grapes are picked.

Mr Stott said the number of bottles allocated to Dan Murphy's, which runs 185 stores around Australia, was about 50 per cent lower than normal.

While it hadn't received the glowing reviews that the 2008 Penfolds Grange garnered last year, it is still likely to be sought after by collectors and higher-end drinkers.

Mr Stott said many customers were collectors who religiously purchase a bottle each year, while it was also popular to buy a bottle of Grange in a child's birth year and cellar it, to give to them when they turn 21.

Sydney Morning Herald wine writer Huon Hooke in March described the 2009 Grange as "a very good wine, a worthy Grange, but certainly not a top-level Grange. It lacks the fruit intensity of a great Grange and has some 'cooked fruit' hot-year characteristics."

Jules Gutierrez, the fine wine manager at North Sydney Cellars, said some older vintages were better value.

He said a few weeks ago a businessman from South Korea bought 11 bottles of Grange from 1990, 1991 and 1998. The most highly sought after by customers among the back vintages was 1990, which North Sydney Cellars has for a shelf price of $750.

Mr Gutierrez said Grange had an international reputation. "Most of the Grange purchased by our customers are intended as gifts for other people," he said. "It's very disconnected from the actual taste of the wine, it's more the association."

Penfolds, owned by Treasury Wine Estates, has kept the recommended retail price of the 2009 Penfolds Grange at $785 per bottle, the same as last year. The wine was made even more famous through its role in the downfall this month of NSW premier Barry O'Farrell, who resigned over a gift of a 1959 bottle of Penfolds Grange.

The cellar door at the Penfolds Magill winery in the Adelaide foothills, which is regarded as the spiritual home of Grange even though the wine is bottled in the Barossa Valley, will open at 8am on Thursday instead of the usual 10am opening time.

Clarke's grand Treasury goals - Management

Newspaper April 12, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Julie-anne Sprague Section: Companies and Markets 634 Words
Page: 37
OpenURL Link
New chief has a global vision for battling wine company.

It was on an English golf course that an out-of-work Mike Clarke first heard of the world's biggest stand-alone listed wine company, Treasury Wine Estates.

As the seasoned, fast-moving ­consumer goods executive took to the tee in July 2013, his golfing partner mentioned the spectacular destruction of shareholder value occurring on the other side of the globe.

"He was talking about this company that had done this massive inventory write-down and we were both commenting on what a shame that was; to write down inventory and good quality product," Clarke recalls.

Less than a year later, the 50-year-old South African has taken the helm of the winemaker to resuscitate earnings for the maker of Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Wynns and Rosemount.

The plan is to cut costs, including staff, trim the number of wine brands and boost consumer branding.

Unfortunately for Clarke, a new boss talking up a fresh strategy to realise Treasury's true potential has been a recurring theme for the Melbourne-based company.Executive churn

For a business operating with long term fundamentals – decisions in the vineyard can take at least three years to materialise – it has acquired a nasty habit of churning chief executives.

Its most recent, David Dearie, was in the top job for 2½ years, before problems selling wine in the US triggered a $155 million write-down, claiming his scalp. It's little wonder some analysts and investors are jaded.

"The strategy announced by the new chief executive echoed the strategy announced by Keith Lambert in 2001, Jamie Odell in 2004 and 2008 and David Dearie in 2010," JPMorgan analyst ­Stuart Jackson told investors this week.

But Clarke impressed the market with his tough talking this week.

"Rest assured, I'm looking at every single option that all the analysts have articulated in the reports," Clarke told investors on Tuesday. "Everything is on the table."

Several analysts and investors have been calling for some time for Treasury to sell its troublesome US operations.

Clarke has since clarified that while he is looking at all options, it's options to fix the business, not sell it. The US, he says, remains important to Treasury's growth outlook.The problem for Clarke is that many before him have held the same view and failed to deliver.Earnings dwindle

It remains to be seen how long they give Clarke and the board to get it right, considering Treasury has failed to deliver acceptable returns since Foster's paid $2.9 billion for Beringer in 2000. Earnings are now about a 10th of what they were 14 years ago.

"We believe that Treasury's commitment to its US business is impeding the ability of the company to reach its full potential," Merrill Lynch analyst David Errington says.

Errington has estimated a sale of Treasury's US business could yield up to $1 billion.

Clarke is confident he has the backing of chairman Paul Rayner to execute his "road map" for Treasury, a "journey" he anticipates will take about five years.

Before agreeing to the job, Clarke met each director and sent the board a one-page document outlining his vision for the company.

Clarke, a keen surfer, was born in South Africa, where he trained as an accountant before leaving at 21 to take a job with Deloitte in Hong Kong, where he met and married his wife.

"Any team that's playing against England I support, just to wind her up," Clarke says.

And the other big sporting question?

"Whoever in our organisation who had the guts to come up to me in say this is the [AFL] team you should support, this is why and this is what you should wear . . . I would choose that team," he says, although noting he wanted to choose a team close to the company's vineyards.

"One of our guys at the Wynns [winery] facility said the team you need to join is the Adelaide Crows."

Treasury Wine Estates chief executive Mike Clarke will slash jobs, cut wine brands and consider selling the winemaker's US operations as part of a turnaround strategy to restore faith with battered investors.

It will form the first part of a five -year journey to improve shareholder returns following more than a decade of persistent write-downs that have plagued the nation's biggest winemaker by revenue.

Its brands include Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Rosemount and Wynns.

"There has been a lot of talk in this company and not necessarily a lot of delivery," Mr Clarke told investors in his first public comments since relocating from London to Melbourne last month.

Within weeks, Mr Clarke, who becomes the company's sixth chief executive in four years, will have a handle on the quantum of the job cuts as he tries to pinch funds to throw at consumer marketing and brand building that he says will improve wine sales.

"It will mean there will be some jobs that come out of our business."

He also tipped cuts to spending on consultants and within its supply chain while it was also to quit "tail" wine brands.

"I think we do have too many brands in our portfolio," Mr Clarke said, noting that at last count the company had 83 brands.

"There's some 'tail' that we need to address."

He said the company's top brands would get the "lion-sized" chunk of its consumer marketing spend as it engaged with consumers "and drive them in to stores".

The market responded positively to his comments, pushing Treasury shares up 7.2 per cent, or 26¢, to $3.87.

The market has become used to turnaround plans and ambitions to deliver sustainable profits.

Mr Clarke replaces David Dearie, who was shown the door by the board after an embarrassing $155 million write down at the hands of its problematic US business last June.

"I was playing golf with a friend of mine in the UK and he was talking about this company that had done this massive inventory write down and we were both commenting on what a shame that was, to write down inventory and good quality product," Mr Clarke saidMr Clarke, 50, has a long career in fast moving consumer goods, leading a turnaround for Kraft's European operations and more recently salvaging distressed Premier Foods, the company behind Hovis bread, Mr Kipling cakes and Bisto gravy.

Analysts were scathing of Treasury chairman Paul Rayner's decision to pick an executive with no wine experience. Mr Clarke said he suffered similar criticism when he left Coca-Cola to join Kraft in 2009.

He said investors were concerned an executive that knew nothing about food was responsible for improving the fortunes of a $13 billion business.

"Very quickly I became the top performing business unit in Kraft globally," he said

Mr Clarke is committed to the delivery the company's earnings guidance but warned he would not make decisions that impacted the long term health of the business. He highlighted how significant its Penfolds brand is to the company.

While it has 83 brands it is Penfolds that delivers the lion's share of its profits, which means its current quarter, when it releases the Penfolds luxury range include Grange, is its largest for sales. "I'm not emotional about any part of our business," he said.

"But I am emotional about Penfolds. That's a gem that I think we have a good job with and I think we can do an even better with Penfolds. If we can do ­something good with Penfolds we can probably do something good with the rest of our portfolio." One shareholder, who declined to be named, said: "words are cheap, actions are more important".

The shareholder was concerned that while Mr Clarke was considering all options, including calls by the market to sell its US operations, he may find it difficult to do because the board has indicated they want to retain the division.

Mr Clarke has unofficially been working in the business for about three weeks and spent a week in the US understanding its operations.

He officially took on the job on March 31. He said "there is a lot that needs to be fixed".
