Clarke charms Treasury Wines

Newspaper April 9, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Matthew Stevens Section: Companies and Markets 1276 Words
Page: 34
OpenURL Link
It says much of the mislaid potential of Treasury Wine Estates that the first glimpse of executive clear-thinking, commercial smarts and oratorical clarity served to add more than 7 per cent to its crisis-crushed winemaker's share price.

Michael Clarke's first contact on Tuesday with investors and analysts went exceeding well, with the new boss indicating that everything was on the table, that "cold" and unemotional decisions would be taken and that the market would not have to wait long to see rhetoric translate into action.

Given the frustration and scepticism released by his confirmation in February, things could have gone very poorly indeed in this debut outing for Clarke.

But he disarmed the potentially hostile with the judicious use of agreement and then hinted, without any firm commitment, at the shape of the reforms already formulating a week into his tenure.

So we heard how past TWE management had failed to deliver on two years of promises to the market and failed to unlock the value of either TWE's assets or people and deal with obvious structural problems.

"There is a lot that needs to be fixed," Clarke started, adding that he agreed with much that had been said and written about the challenges that cloud his new business.

With that, Clarke, who demonstrated his clear preference to distill problems and their solutions into neat and digestible trinities, defined TWE's strengths as its quality wines, its strong balance sheet and its talented people.

Clarke's pitch was sustained by the idea that past turnarounds at Kraft's European food colony and Premier Foods in the UK left him well prepared to extract full shareholder value from each of those core positives.

His repeated reference to the structural challenges he is identifying as he tours the TWE business suggested that he plans to deal with the biggest problem facing this business. The simple fact is that since Foster's spent $US2.9 billion to acquire a footing in the US wine business, this business has been burning shareholder wealth. And it is TWE's US outposts that the market identifies as the problem Clarke must deal with promptly and definitively.

Given the commercial qualities hinted at yesterday and demonstrated by his past successes, the market might well be prepared to give Clarke a bit of time to set the US ship right.

But it is hard to believe that this crippling misadventure, now nearly 15 years old, can suddenly be righted for the longer term. Any improvement simply has to be aimed at sustaining the conscious uncoupling that the market so desires.

It has to be said that Clarke offered some real hope that some hard decisions will be taken. In response to allegations that past management had proven too emotionally attached to assets generally and the US business specifically, Clarke said he arrived with no emotional attachment to anything in the portfolio.

Happily, he later offered one exception to that, thanking God that Penfolds was part of his business and expressing firm confidence in its business model and its current management.

Clarke introduced his pitch with a forgivably tepid recommitment to profit forecasts offered in interim management in February.

He said the targets remained live but in the next breath he offered the quite acceptable caveats that there was not a lot he could do to influence the result given the fourth quarter was already upon him and that he would not "chase a full-year number to the detriment of a long-term plan".

In February, acting CEO Warwick Every-Burns promised TWE's FY14 EBIT would range between $190 million and $210 million.

This seemed a big call back in February, not least because the December half EBIT came in at a lowly $45.8 million.

To hit the bottom of the target range TWE needs to throw off more than $145 million in the second half. To do that it needs to outperform the FY13 second half, which was $138.1 million. And there was not a lot that Every-Burns offered which generated confidence such a momentum shift was within his reach.

Bill Doyle's retirement from Potash of Saskatchewan will doubtless be watched with some considerable interest by the potash wannabes at BHP Billiton. Big Bill is the bloke from Chicago that Canadians like to imagine is the face of the $US20 billion global potash market. They also like to recall he was the guy who stared down BHP Billiton when Marius Kloppers came knocking in 2010 with a $US40 billion hostile takeover.

The truth is rather more complicated. POS was not saved from BHP's acquisitive claws by Doyle's commercial cunning nor the brilliant promise of his business. No. POS was saved for Canadian prosperity by a government slightly irritated by the global Australian's self-possession and by a conservative prime minister keen to sustain his party's grip on key seats in Saskatchewan.

And, as the recent turmoil has demonstrated all too clearly, the face of potash is Russian, rich and driven by a quite different set of priorities to those that have guided Doyle's 15 years at the top of the fertilizer heap.

The recent tiff between Russia's Uralkali and its erstwhile partner in potash marketing from Belarus that triggered a worrying collapse in potash prices served only to demonstrate the potential for a meaningful shift in the balance of power in the potash export marketplace. It is hard to believe that risks exposed by the dispute on potash's eastern front that is as yet unresolved did not contribute to the board's decision that the time for fresh eyes might have come.

Mind you, Doyle has also endured criticism for spending $C8 billion ($7.9 billion) and more against new capacity at a time when the market is over-supplied and the price is already in retreat. To that end, the narrative of his departure has a ring very familiar.

So, why does any of this matter to BHP and its new management?

Well, Andrew Mackenzie was the bloke who ultimately ran the nitty gritty of BHP's pitch for potash and was the man most personally aggrieved by the government's decision. And Mackenzie has made it known often enough since he took over at BHP last year that potash is his potential fifth pillar.

Mackenzie reckons BHP is owner of the best undeveloped potash in Saskatchewan. By 2017, BHP will have spent $US3.8 billion ($4.1 billion) on collecting tenements and completing two preparatory shafts at its Jansen project. It's an option that ticks a lot of Mackenzie's boxes. It is a commodity that requires complex mining, that is in an OECD constituency and that would be extracted from a long-life basin-play equivalent to sat BHP's iron ore in the Pilbara.

But Jansen would be an expensive business to develop, costing maybe $US20 billion or more and it is evident BHP does not want to carry the financial load and risk on its own, so it is seeking partners for the project.

And then there is the issue of how to market the stuff.

Currently the potash export market is under polarised influence of two marketing cartels, one that represents the three current Canadian producers and the other the currently dysfunctional partnership between Uralkali and Belaruskali.

If BHP enters the market it will be the outlier, selling its product direct to customers and oblivious of the constraints of Canpotex, the Canadian cartel.

To make a complicated story too simple, BHP's view is that markets will respond rationally to this shift. But not everyone accepts that certainty, if only because there is not a whole lot rational that happens in Russia.

The big question for BHP right now is whether or not Doyle's replacement, current INMET boss Jochen Tilk, will entertain more open-minded conversation than his predecessor. Would a Tilk-managed POS be interested in aligning itself with BHP's progress on Jansen, for example. And, more importantly, is an operator like Tilk – who has spent a working life selling into open-call metals markets – going to be content selling product through an export cartel?

Uphill battle for Treasury Wines after latest misstep in strategy

Newspaper April 7, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)

Mike Clarke, the new chief executive of Treasury Wine Estates, will brief analysts this week for the first time as he tries to rebuild market confidence in the company using skills he's gathered while selling Reebok sporting goods, Kraft food brands, Coca-Cola, Hovis bread and Bisto gravy over two decades.

After a busy first week beginning on March 31 which included a large number of meetings with a diverse range of Treasury's workforce, Mr Clarke turns his attention on Tuesday this week to a briefing with analysts in a first step in the rebuilding process.

It will be an uphill battle, with Treasury's share price languishing at $3.60 after the latest profit stumble caused by a misstep in strategy in the crucial Christmas trading period in Australia. There is also a sharp focus on the ailing United States business and whether it belongs in the Treasury stable, or whether an $800 million-plus sale of the US operations might be the best option. Speculation about takeover bids for Treasury is a constant in the market.Winning back confidence

Winning back the confidence of the liquor trade will be crucial in Mr Clarke's tenure. The company has a prized collection of brands including Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Rosemount and Wynns but delivering consistent returns has proved elusive since Treasury demerged from the former Foster's Group in late 2011.

Tony D'Anna, owner of Boccaccio Cellars in the leafy Melbourne suburb of Balwyn, said the higher-end Penfolds brands such as Grange, Bin 389, Bin 707 and St Henri still had substantial cache with customers. "They're still holding their own," he said.

A initial director's interest notice filed with the Australian Securities Exchange on Friday showed that Mr Clarke didn't yet own any shares in Treasury. A handful of Treasury directors last month topped up their own shareholdings in the company. Lyndsey Cattermole bought an extra 1000 Treasury shares at $3.75 each on March 14, fellow director Ed Chan bought an extra 3000 at $3.75 each the same day and Michael Cheek added 2666 extra Treasury shares to his holding as they used the buying window allowed under the director's share acquisition plan.Softened blow

Treasury shareholders will be paid their 6 cents per share interim dividend on April 10 which will partially soften some of the blow of the ailing share price and memories of last year's horrific $160 million in writedowns stemming from the troublesome US business. That also included the now infamous announcement that $35 million of wine would be destroyed in the US after an inventory blowout in third-party distributor warehouses in low-end commercial wine, which later claimed the scalp of then chief executive David Dearie.

The board was effectively in control of Treasury for six months until Mr Clarke started, with chairman Paul Rayner having installed fellow director Warwick Every-Burns as stand-in chief executive.

There is solid upside if Mr Clarke can win the confidence of the market, although any further strife will increase the clamour for a break-up of the business. Bank of America Merrill Lynch analyst David Errington says Treasury is in a strong position with its luxury wine inventories at $474 million representing a "sizeable level of value" to be released into the market over the next three to four years. He estimates that Treasury will have double the amount of high-end luxury wines available to sell in 2015-16 compared with 2013-14. But there are issues around investor confidence in the company's ability to capture this value with solid execution, given the events of the past 12 months.

Winemakers brace for a bad year as China dries up

Newspaper March 31, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Eli Greenblat Section: Companies and Markets 488 Words
Page: 25
OpenURL Link
Australian winemakers might have to wait until 2015 to see a return to growth in China after Pernod Ricard, the world's second-biggest drinks company and owner of Jacob's Creek, warned sales hadn't risen since the Lunar New Year as an austerity drive and crackdown on luxury gift-giving kept glasses empty.

The dour forecast from Pernod Ricard, whose Jacob's Creek label is the second-most imported wine into China, will likely send shockwaves through the local wine industry already ­suffering from the sting of a strong Australian dollar and downturns in traditional markets through Europe and North America.

Up until 2012 Chinese drinkers had been devouring Australian wine, along with iron ore and coal, but Beijing's anti-corruption drive along with a broader austerity program has smashed demand for luxury goods, from wine to Ferraris, and pinched local wine exports to the region.

In a much-awaited update from Pernod Ricard, the world's fourth-largest winemaker and owner of top-selling spirits brands Chivas Regal and Martell cognac, the French drinks group said it failed to see any meaningful uptick in sales since the Chinese New Year, also warning that demand in China could remain limp until 2015.

Its a sentiment shared by many Australian wine companies.

''We think the Chinese market won't improve for the next 12 months,'' said Mitchell Taylor, the managing director of Clare Valley winemaker Taylors.

''It certainly hasn't picked up since the Chinese New Year and I'm hearing reports of a lot of stock still in the market,'' he told Fairfax Media. ''And I'm also hearing in some of the restaurants and the on-premise venues are not as full as they previously had been.''

China has eclipsed traditional ­markets such as the UK and America to become the fastest-growing export market for Australian wine for ­several years, however exports dropped by 7 per cent to 33 million litres in 2013 when austerity measures curbed spending by government bodies on luxury goods such as premium imported wines.

In October, Treasury Wine Estates, the world's largest pure-play winemaker and owners of Penfolds and Wolf Blass, warned shareholders that consumer demand for wine in China had softened in line with souring demand for luxury products ranging from beverages to fashion.

Treasury Wine Estates subsequently reported volume and earnings falls in China, once its biggest growth market, with first-half volumes to the Asian region down 17.6 per cent and pre-tax earnings falling 63.7 per cent to $4.9 million.

The China downturn also contributed a damaging profit downgrade from Treasury Wine Estates as austerity measures imposed on government officials by President Xi Jinping nudged shoppers away from ostentatious displays of wealth.

Mr Taylor, who is also chairman of Australia's First Families of Wine, a club of 12 multi-generational family-owned wineries, said reports from his winemaker's representative in the region confirmed confidence had not returned to the sector. ''We have noticed it's still a bit patchy in China, some regions are going OK but generally there is still a bit of caution within the Chinese market,'' Mr Taylor said.

Make or break

Newspaper March 28, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Australian Financial Review Magazine 2405 Words
Page: 46
OpenURL Link
The decision by Treasury Wine Estates to destroy six million bottles of cheap plonk in the US could not have come at a worse time for the Australian wine sector, which wants to remind the world that we make a lot of top quality wine, too.  By Simon Evans.

Smashing millions of bottles of wine to smithereens is a messy, ear-piercing job. A steady flow of liquid snakes its way across the floor into specialised vessels and drains, staining the crushed cardboard boxes that remain on the floor. The broken glass and soggy cardboard are collected separately for recycling, each destined for new industrial uses.

This is the scene that has been playing out in the US since December, when the world's largest stand-alone wine company, the Melbourne-based Treasury Wine Estates, began destroying six million bottles of cheap wine worth an estimated $34 million. The unprecedented task is so mammoth that it is not expected to be finished until June. Flagged to the market last July, the demolition was triggered by a serious miscalculation of demand in the US for a raft of wines made by Treasury's Californian business which sell in the less-than-$10 price bracket. Millions of bottles with a quick use-by date had been languishing in the warehouses of hundreds of third-party liquor distributors across the US.

The destruction and $160 million worth of write-downs that accompanied it claimed the scalp of Treasury chief executive David Dearie, who took the fall last September for the company having so misjudged the US market. It is now squarely in the lap of a new boss at Treasury, former Coca-Cola and Kraft Foods executive Mike Clarke, who begins on March 31.

The situation was a potential nightmare for more than just Dearie and the Australian Stock Exchange-listed company. There were concerns it could be a branding disaster for the entire $5 billion Australian wine sector, which had been working assiduously to remind the world that, while we make a lot of good cheap wine, we also have many top-shelf labels. The most renowned of these, Penfolds Grange – a 2008 bottle of which will set you back close to $800 – is also produced by Treasury.

Nor would the destruction help reverse the stark drop in overall wine exports over the previous five years, which have fallen from $3 billion in 2007 to $1.85 billion in 2012. A confluence of factors has forced many Australian labels to compete on price and convenience rather than brand, discounting just to stay on shelves. These include a global grape oversupply that has only just abated, and a crowded marketplace in which an uncompetitive currency made it hard to compete with rivals from Argentina, Chile and Europe.Such wholesale destruction

Respected British wine writer Jancis Robinson was blunt about the Treasury decision last July. "I have been writing about wine since 1975 and I have never heard of such wholesale destruction of wine, and such a public admission that the sort of wine being produced today has such a short shelf life," she wrote. "So much for the old adage that wine improves with age. Even before this announcement, and before presumably Treasury's export volumes dwindle considerably this year, the Australian wine exporting juggernaut has been running out of steam. The strength of the Australian dollar has, of course, not helped. Chile looks set to overtake Australia in terms of total volume of wine exported – after Italy, Spain and then France – even if at a lower average price than Australia."

Only a few months after Treasury's shock announcement, the Winemakers' Federation of Australia, on behalf of the nation's 2500 wine companies, released a 43-point plan designed to lift the profitability and prestige of our brands offshore and at home. Premium wines enjoy better margins and thus mean greater profitability, but just as importantly, can be used to reset the tone for how the whole sector is perceived.

Federation president Tony D'Aloisio, owner of the premium Yarra Valley producer Oakridge Wines and a former chairman of the Australian Securities and Investments Commission, says it's important to separate the premium market from the commercial one. "The fine wine market is obviously the way to set the position of Australia," he says. "The fine wine market, as it does in any other market, is the lead. It stamps the brand and the recognition." The federation believes there needs to be a broad range of tailored activities to rebuild support for Australian wine among consumers, distributors, commentators and other influential players they term "gatekeepers'' in both the domestic and international markets.

A report prepared for the federation by Centaurus Partners suggests that the top end of the export market held up better during the bad years from 2007 to 2012 than the hard-hit middle ground. Exports of Australian wine that retails at $30 or more fell by only 1.1 per cent to $360 million in that time. By contrast, exports of wine in the $10-$15 bracket fell by a whopping 67 per cent, from $854 million to $286 million; wine in the $15-$30 category tumbled 43 per cent from $271 million to $155 million.Pie biggest at commercial end

The commercial end – defined as wine priced at less than $10 – also held up better than the middle. Exports of wine retailing for less than $7 actually grew by 4 per cent to $198 million. Wines priced between $7 and $10 fell 36 per cent to $854 million. As those last two figures show, the pie is biggest at the commercial end, which helps explain why it is so difficult to shift perceptions.

Commercial wine production actually accounts for two-thirds of the $5 billion Australian wine industry, with our export success in this field led by Yellow Tail, produced by Griffith-based Casella Wines, and Jacob's Creek, owned by global drinks giant Pernod Ricard. They have been at the forefront of the "critter brands'' which have found success in export markets over the past decade. Other critter labels include Little Penguin, Barramundi, Black Swan, and Bear Crossing, which has a koala bear on its label.

Casella produces about 12 million cases of Yellow Tail annually, 95 per cent of which is exported to the US. It is not only Australia's biggest export wine; more of it is imported into the US than any brand worldwide. Yellow Tail didn't exist when the Sydney Olympics were held in 2000. But by October 2013 one billion bottles had been produced. US consumers responded to the stylised yellow-footed rock wallaby, which most Americans think is a kangaroo, on its labels.

The brands being destroyed by Treasury don't retail in Australia. Going by names such as Beringer, Meridian and Stone Cellars, they are made exclusively for the US market. Gaining approval for their destruction has not been without bureaucratic frustrations. The political impasse in the US last October, when President Barack Obama battled to gain clearance for his budget, temporarily froze the entire workings of the US government. The consequences flowed through to every level of the government, and caught Treasury in a web of red tape. Vital applications and paperwork sent to the US Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau sat motionless in in-trays. Without them, nothing could proceed. Complex laws around the taxation of wine and the multiple jurisdictions involved added an extra layer of difficulty, such that the destruction that was announced to the market in July took until December to begin.Penfolds Grange standard-bearer

Penfolds, with its flagship label Grange lauded worldwide, is a standard-bearer for Australian wine offshore. Peter Gago has been its chief winemaker for the past 12 years. He was stunned when he first heard about the stumbles of his stablemates in the US, and the fact that so much wine was being destroyed. "Shock-horror was the first reaction,'' he says. That most of it was blush-style wine with a deliberately short shelf life, similar to rosé, gave those with the spreadsheets in sales and supply planning a "stark and abrupt'' reminder of the risks associated with not staying close enough to the marketplace. But Gago doesn't believe the stain from the US destruction will spill over into the other brands owned by Treasury, nor the broader Australian industry. While there was some initial curiosity from the more educated wine professionals and those in the liquor industry when it was first announced in July, that has abated. "It's only the informed people who make the association,'' he says. "The Penfolds culture is very independent of the others.''

Gago believes the trail blazed by Yellow Tail, Jacob's Creek and Nottage Hill has been a plus for the industry. There are some premium producers who regard these brands as "a bit of a distraction'', but he is not one of them. "I think rather than undermine, they augment. The marketplace is very complex. The critter brands have a certain niche.'' What Australia needs to do, he argues, is a better job at explaining the sheer breadth and diversity of the wine it can produce. "The magical catchword is choice. We're preaching diversity; we're preaching choice.''

The clout of high-profile wine writers and the points they hand out for particular wines are crucial to raising the reputation of Australia's top wines offshore, and to this end Penfolds Grange is doing its bit. The 2008 release has been applauded around the world, receiving a perfect score of 100 points from one of the world's leading wine magazines, Wine Spectator, and similarly perfect scores from Robert Parker's highly influential Wine Advocate and the Swiss wine publication Weinzeitung.

These scoring systems provide a universal benchmark that everyone understands – and Gago says the 2010 Grange, out in 2015, is better still. He is relishing the prospect of releasing this vintage, which he is sure will help bolster Australia's reputation at the very upper echelons of the world of wine.

Robert Hill Smith is the managing director of Yalumba Wines based in South Australia's Barossa Valley. The winery reeks of history, with glorious bluestone walls, lush green lawns with a very English sensibility, set against the backdrop of rolling hills and green vineyards just outside of the historic town of Angaston. Hill Smith says patience is required in the wine sector and that those focusing on short-term investment returns are bound to run into strife. "I think that patient capital is certainly something that is a requirement for giving Australia a more credible foundation and driving its reputation around the planet.''

Yalumba is family-owned and doesn't have the full glare of the public spotlight on its every decision, as an ASX-listed company like Treasury does. Hill Smith says being publicly listed is perhaps not the best model for a wine company, because of the need for long-term decisions that won't necessarily deliver consistent shareholder returns.

He believes there needs to be a better-funded, co-ordinated approach from industry bodies to help elevate perceptions of quality overseas. A Winemakers' Federation report showed that levies paid to government agency Wine Australia to support the sector's activities dropped from $17 million to $11 million between 2007 and 2012.

"Those organisations are underresourced given the complexities and the compliance that is needed,'' Hill Smith says. "We have to have an income stream with sufficient substance in it that allows the industry as a whole to have a bank to promote itself.''

Explaining the broad geographic spread of Australia's different wine regions and what they can do is paramount to shifting perceptions offshore, argues Hill Smith, as is offering different cues to educated wine drinkers. He was impressed with the Wine Australia-backed Savour Australia event held in Adelaide in September 2013, in which food and wine were promoted together. He warns that any such marketing needs to be committed to for the long term. "The days of a one-hit wonder are long gone.''

Like Gago, Hill Smith believes the success of the lower-priced commercial wines such as Yellow Tail and Jacob's Creek hasn't been damaging to the broader Australian industry. Every country has inexpensive offerings that take away some of the uncertainty for an anxious consumer not sure what to pick from a shelf. "We've got to grow up and accept that every wine-making country has an introductory level.''New Zealand proves the point

New Zealand proves that point. Its wine industry has grown enormously on the back of its sauvignon blanc offerings, which run the gamut from entry level to more sophisticated offerings. It has very successfully parlayed the popularity of its more mainstream offerings into enticing drinkers to move up to the higher-priced luxury wines. "New Zealand has an ocean of sauvignon blanc,'' says Hill Smith. The Australian federation is trying to even the playing field, calling for changes to how a rebate is applied to this country's complicated wine equalisation tax. In essence, D'Aloisio says that 205 Kiwi wine companies selling their products in Australia shared $25 million in tax rebates in 2011-12. There's a view that they shouldn't be given a leg up to take business away from local producers.

Sophisticated drinkers in any market tend not to be too worried about the price tag. The trick, then, is to be among the group of labels that these drinkers are choosing from. "The more serious and considered purchases, it doesn't depend on price,'' Hill Smith says. "It's a bit more cerebral and can take a long time.''

Reaching the educated drinker is a tough task. The large retailers selling wine in markets such as Britain have enormous power. They want to stock brands that sell quickly, which doesn't necessarily lend itself to a high-end wine. The internet is useful in overcoming the difficulty of securing offshore shelf space, but that too has its challenges, not least of which is merely getting clicked on.

In the end, there is no quick fix. Vanya Cullen, managing director and chief winemaker at Cullen Wines, believes the passage of time will help advance Australia up the global wine spectrum. Ours is a young wine-making nation, particularly compared with European countries, and a proven track record is important for an individual company as well as a wine-producing nation. "It takes a long time to understand what the land can produce,'' she says.

A high-end producer from Western Australia's spectacular Margaret River region, Cullen's winery is nestled on fertile land close to the wild surf beaches that compete with the wine circuit for the attention of tourists. She notes that backing off on yields in favour of optimising quality into the future is an important ingredient to getting that track record. "There is that commitment to looking after the land," she says. "I don't see a lot of commitment to sustainability at the moment.''

Getting the message right is especially important in younger markets like China. "You go to China and it is more brand-based," Cullen says. The answer for Australia, she believes, is to build greater awareness of the different regions and what they each produce. "I do believe it is about understanding the great wine-growing sites of Australia.''

Investors greedy for growth - Chanticleer

Newspaper March 1, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Tony Boyd Section: Chanticleer 1146 Words
Page: 56
OpenURL Link
Take a look at the top-performing stocks as measured by share price rises over the four-week reporting season and it is obvious investors are willing to pay a lot for growth. Growth is scarce in Australia because consumers remain nervous about the future and the corporate sector is holding back on capital investment.

So when multinational pizza-maker Domino's Pizza and electrical appliances distributor Breville delivered growth in their interim results, the market responded warmly. Domino's shares have risen 26 per cent in the past month. Its interim earnings per share jumped 20 per cent on a 38 per cent rise in net profit.

Breville, which sells a co-branded range of Nespresso machines, saw its shares rise 19 per cent over the past month, despite the fact that earnings per share were flat at 24¢ a share in the interim period.

Fund managers told Chanticleer the share price could be attributed to the 23 per cent increase in the firm's core business revenue.

Growth was rewarded elsewhere, particularly in relation to several stocks that were listed last year through initial public offerings. Veda Group, a data analytics company, said it was on track to deliver results in line with its prospectus forecasts.

Fund managers said the search for growth was harder these days because of the structural forces impacting upon sectors that were traditional proxies for growth, such as the media sector.

A cyclical upturn in the economy would usually be reflected in higher advertising spending, which made media stocks the natural home for those seeking organic growth. Nine Entertainment Co has enjoyed a rally in its shares after delivering a solid result, while Fairfax Media appeared to rally strongly simply because it delivered what was promised.

One fund manager said Fairfax was in the same basket as a number of companies which enjoyed relief rallies on the back of interim profit results that did not immediately disappoint.

Another stock in this basket was Leighton Holdings. Its shares have risen about 15 per cent in the past three weeks despite a warning from at least one analyst that the composition of its cash flow "lacked quality".

A Macquarie Securities analyst said Leighton's receivables had risen by $660 million to $1.5 billion in the six months to December. This pointed to an increase in "underclaims", which relates to contract variations, scope changes and difficult terms and conditions. Leighton is the last mining services company not to have had a profit downgrade.

A study of the company announcements over the past month relative to analysts' forecasts found that only 22 per cent of companies missed on dividend per share, while 35 per cent missed on earnings per share and half missed on free cash flow.

The author of the study, Hasan Tevfik of Credit Suisse, says this shows that yield has been favoured ahead of capital investment.

Nevertheless, fast-growing companies that are investing for growth have been rewarded with strong share price rises, including SEEK, REA Group and G8 Education. All of these were among the top 20 performing stocks over the past month.

A feature of the reporting season has been the focus on cost-cutting in resources, media, aviation and mining services.

However, an increasing number of leading business figures are saying the cost-cutting cannot continue and the focus will have to be on growing revenue.

Gary Wingrove, the relatively new chief executive at professional services firm KPMG, says his clients have just about exhausted the amount of costs that can be removed from their businesses.

However, Grant O'Brien, chief executive of Woolworths, made it clear on Friday when he released the company's best interim results for several years that there were more efficiency gains to be extracted from its supply chain.

O'Brien told analysts the company would soon be able to give details of its next-generation supply chain capability. This project will be as big as the one that transformed the company more than a decade ago under former chief executive Paul Simons.

Investors who have not thought about putting a proportion of their superannuation savings into alternative investments ought to look at the relative performance of Australia's two largest wine companies.

The two companies are Accolade Wines, which owns leading brands Hardys, Houghton, Leasingham and Robert Mondavi, and Treasury Wine Estates (TWE), which owns Penfolds, Wynns, Wolf Blass and Rosemount.

Accolade is 80 per cent owned by CHAMP private equity on behalf of a range of investors, including sovereign wealth funds and institutional fund managers.

TWE is a publicly listed company that was spun out of Foster's Group as a separate listed company in May 2011.

The performance of TWE has been poor since it became an independent company. It has had to deal with excess inventory in its supply chain in the United States. Also, chief executive David Dearie was dumped in September last year.

TWE's earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation have been flat over the past two years at around $280 million. Analysts at Credit Suisse say the company will have EBITDA in the year to June 2014 of $284 million and $304 million in 2015. Revenue is expected to be about $1.7 billion this year.

Contrast that with Accolade, which was spun out of the American premium wine and spirits company Constellation Brands in 2011. Under the guidance of CHAMP's John Haddock, Accolade will this year double its EBITDA to $100 million on revenue of about $1 billion. Accolade has been able to take advantage of its strengths, which include the largest wine volume in Australia and the largest bottling plant in Europe.

Haddock says the secret to the turnaround at Accolade has been the appointment of quality managers from private equity and global wine and beverage industries. Accolade's new chief executive, John Ratcliffe, who started in February, was the commercial director at UK wine merchant Oddbins.

TWE's new chief executive, Warwick Every-Burns, has a long experience in the fast-moving consumer goods sector.

Tough corporate restructuring can be more easily achieved under the ownership of private equity. There is not only a willingness to take hard decisions, but the managers have significant skin in the game and work towards goals that have no relation to the half-yearly reporting schedules of public companies.

A private equity investment usually lasts for about five years.

Sovereign wealth funds and pension plans from Australia, Canada and the US, which have very long time frames for investment, are increasingly seeking to lift their returns by accessing private equity.

For example, the Future Fund, which manages about $100 billion in assets, has more invested in alternative assets ($14 billion) than in Australian equities ($9.7 billion).

The move by Westpac Banking Corp to invest $50 million in a venture capital fund for early-stage investing is likely to be copied by its rivals.

The Westpac move recognises the fact that the bureaucratic culture of a major bank is not suited to finding and nurturing entrepreneurs.

The bank's investment in venture capital is tiny compared to its $45.6 billion in share capital and retained earnings. But it is large enough to build a portfolio of companies and provide an opportunity to own the next great disruptive technology in financial services.

Has Treasury been hit by the Penfolds' grapes of wrath?

Newspaper February 20, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 927 Words
Page: 19
OpenURL Link
Wine woes It is as if the founders have put a hex on the famous brand.

Is there a Penfolds curse at work? A succession of six corporate owners of Australia's most famous wine brand over the past three decades have either been taken over or run into financial strife, and the prospects of it happening again are high.

It's as if the ghosts of the founders Christopher and Mary Penfold, who started the wine label in 1844, are wreaking their revenge because the wine brand has strayed so far from its ­original  purpose: a source of medicinal benefit for patients of the doctor's ­medical practice.

The latest custodians, Treasury Wine Estates, have earned the wrath of investment markets and the credibility of the board and management is in tatters as they battle to try and restore some confidence in the company's direction after a run of disasters. Much depends on the reception received by Treasury chairman Paul Rayner and his stand-in chief executive Warwick Every-Burns, who stepped out of the Treasury boardroom to run the company last September. They will attempt to defend their strategy on Thursday when they outline the full details of the $40 million full-year profit downgrade announced three weeks ago, as Treasury officially unveils its first-half profit results. But how long will Treasury be around in its current form?

The irony is that Penfolds itself is a highly profitable business and the jewel in the crown of Treasury. Penfolds is estimated to make about $180 million in profits annually. That's about three-quarters of the total profits of Treasury's sprawling wine business, which also includes the Beringer brands in the US and Wolf Blass, Rosemount, Seppelt, Lindemans and Wynns in Australia.

Penfolds's earnings potential in the next two years is vast as a treasure trove of up to $280 million of high-quality red wines currently maturing in barrel halls hit the market.

As a result, potential suitors are closely eyeing the corporate mess that Treasury has found itself in and the potential upside for a new owner. Chinese firms and private equity buyers are speculated to be those most interested.Grange halo effect is as strong as ever

The Penfolds brand, with a 170-year-old history, has proven very resilient over three decades of corporate ructions, having passed through six different sets of corporate owners since the early 1980s.

The prestige of the flagship Penfolds Grange, the latest release of which the company was selling for $785 a bottle, is a huge strength.

Bruce Kemp was chief executive of Southcorp Wines, a forerunner of Treasury, for most of the 1990s and is today chairman of Tasmanian wine company Pipers Brook. He says ­Penfolds has enormous cachet in the ­marketplace.

"It still stands out,'' Kemp says.

Penfolds Grange and the higher-quality Penfolds range that sits underneath it were big profit drivers in the 1990s, and two decades on, the "halo" effect is still intact. "There's a pretty big halo, and a big shadow down the line,'' Kemp says.

Bank of America Merrill Lynch analyst David Errington has all but given up on Treasury's current board and management, saying, "if the board's focus turns to maximising shareholder returns, it will need to consider breaking the company up".

A break-up would result in a scramble for the best assets, with Penfolds at the top of the tree. The one possible saviour in the ongoing Treasury saga is the prospect of a new, highly respected chief executive being appointed soon. There are persistent rumours about the long-time boss of Coca-Cola Amatil's Australian operations, Warwick White, having been approached to take the job. Outgoing CCA chief executive Terry Davis, who had been the subject of speculation that he has being courted, says he has no interest in taking another chief executive role at a public company.Six owners in just three decades

But Penfolds has been through a lot. It's had six owners in just over three decades. In the mid-1970s Penfolds was acquired by then NSW brewer, Tooth & Co, and then in the 1980s became part of corporate raider John Spalvins's Adelaide Steamship Company, which also owned Woolworths, David Jones and a host of big-name food brands including Peters ice-cream and Four'N'Twenty pies.

Spalvins's empire fell apart after the 1987 sharemarket crash under the weight of $7 billion in debts, and Penfolds was sold off to another beer company, SA Brewing, in 1990. SA Brewing, an ASX-listed company, changed its name to Southcorp in 1993, sold off packaging and water heater businesses, and Southcorp Wines became a stand-alone wine business.

It then merged with privately-owned Rosemount Wines in 2001 in what was effectively a reverse takeover, to become the world's largest wine company. But things went awry under new management and in 2005 it was taken over by Foster's Group.

Plans by Foster's to extract efficiencies from selling beer and wine into the same liquor retailers didn't work properly, and after further management changes, Foster's was split into two companies in 2011. The wine company was re-badged Treasury Wine Estates and in May, 2011 set sail on its new voyage as an ASX-listed debt-free wine group, with Penfolds as the biggest brand.

Chief executive David Dearie lasted a little over two years but was then punted by the board in September, 2013, taking the blame for $160 million in write-downs connected with the US wine operations.

A potential predator of Treasury will be closely eyeing the red wine inventory sitting in barrel halls at Penfolds. Bank of America Merrill Lynch believes the profit increase from the Penfolds business over the next two to three years is substantial, with Penfolds having increased inventory levels of its higher-priced wines from $50 million in 2011 to $280 million in 2013.

Treasury Wine Estates, the maker of globally recognised brands like Penfolds, Lindemans and Rosemount Estate seems to be suffering from the mother of all hangovers.

Treasury Wine Estates is under severe pressure to reverse a new strategy of spurning aggressive promotions and discounts with big liquor chains run by Coles and Woolworths because of the risk of further damage to profits in the next few months.

Mr Georgas says December is crucial in the liquor industry because of seasonal celebrations and gift-giving.

Smaller producers can top up their glasses - How it works

Newspaper February 1, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans and Edmund Tadros Section: News 460 Words
Page: 4
OpenURL Link
If you are preparing to screw the cap off a robust bottle of shiraz at a barbecue this weekend or a grassy sauvignon blanc at a Saturday-night dinner party, you might like to know more about the complex wine equalisation tax (WET) that reaps more than $700 million in net tax annually in Australia.

About 90 per cent is paid by Australia's top 20 wine companies, because the way the WET works, the more sales of wine a company makes to retailers who sell it, the more tax it pays.

Treasury Wine Estates, for example, which has the labels Penfolds, Lindeman's, Wolf Blass, Rosemount and Wynns, and which has had a rough week with a $40 million profit downgrade, pays the most WET.

But it is also able to claim the maximum $500,000 WET rebate, as do more than 3000 other local claimants.

For a shopper strolling into a liquor store to buy a $20 bottle of wine, the WET of 29 per cent is already included in the price.

WET was brought in at the same time as the goods and services tax in 2000 with the "equalisation'' referring to the way the WET was supposed to maintain wine prices at similar levels to the pre-GST regime after the previous wine sales tax was scrapped.

A WET rebate was introduced in 2004 and the rebate was boosted to a ­maximum of $500,000 per wine ­producer in 2006.

For small operators with minimal sales it is a godsend because it effectively means they pay no wine tax because the $500,000 rebate cancels out what they are paying in their 29 per cent of WET on the small amount of wine they sell.

In practical terms, Treasury Wine Estates will soak up its $500,000 rebate in the first two or three days of each financial year, while a tiny wine producer enjoys a much greater relative benefit.

Former federal Treasury secretary Ken Henry was not a fan of the WET or the rebate. In his 2010 Henry Tax Review he recommended that the 13 different tax rates on different types of alcohol, including beer and spirits, be scrapped and replaced by a single flat rate based on the volume of alcohol in an alcoholic beverage. This is the classic volumetric tax that has been a political hot potato for ­decades. A volumetric tax would sharply increase the price of cheaper wine, and in particular cask wine.

Dr Henry warned about the risks of tax avoidance in the WET regime and particularly the risk of "double dipping'' or claiming multiple times.

The Rudd government subsequently decided against making any changes because of the impact on employment in rural regions where wine companies are based, and the fact that the $5 billion wine industry was going through a tough time because of a high Australian dollar and a grape oversupply. The wine industry employs about 30,000 people directly, most in country areas.

Hangover, part II - Retail

Newspaper January 31, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Features 1144 Words
Page: 44
OpenURL Link
Paul Rayner has gambled on Treasury Wine Estate to survive in a crowded Australian market – and lost, writes Simon Evans.

Paul Rayner's worst nightmare is coming true. The former tobacco executive who stepped up to become chairman of Treasury Wine Estates in September 2012, exerted clear authority a year later when he axed chief executive David Dearie in September.

Dearie was blamed for the sloppy inventory controls in the United States at the heart of an embarrassing profit downgrade and $160 million in writedowns that were revealed by Treasury in July, 2013. When he received his marching orders, Rayner relied on one of his own boardroom colleagues, Warwick Every-Burns, to step into the driver's seat and run the company while a long search continued to find a successor.

As so often happens when a board of directors takes control of a company, some of the commercial nous, gut feel for the industry and relationships with the trade may slip and suffer.

Rayner, a former chief operating officer of British American Tobacco Australasia and a former finance director of British American Tobacco for six years until 2008, and his right-hand man Every-Burns, a former Clorox cleaning products executive, are in the spotlight for all the wrong reasons.

A 20 per cent share price plunge on Thursday, after a $40 million profit downgrade in December as the Australian business hit the skids and China sales slumped as government-imposed austerity measures hurt sales of higher-priced wines, has dragged investor confidence in Treasury to rock bottom.

Every-Burns and Rayner quickly instit­uted a management shake-up soon after Every-Burns took over the top job on an interim basis. A simplified structure was put in place after being announced on November 27. But the troubles this time have emerged in the Australian business, which had previously been a strong performer and was a shining light in the Treasury stable. Much of the management focus has been on the serious problems in the United States, but in a perverse situation, Australia is now a trouble spot.Gambled and lost

Treasury has a glittering collection of wine brands in its portfolio which are the envy of rivals. They include Penfolds, Wolf Blass, Lindemans, Wynns and Rosemount. Every-Burns and Rayner have backed the power of those brands to continue to thrive in a crowded market where hundreds of brands compete for the drinkers eye on retailer's shelves.

They have gambled and lost. In December, sales and profits slumped badly in Australia in the crucial month of December. Treasury lifted prices on a number of brands in the commercial category of $5 to $10 a ­bottle and thumbed its nose at being part of the aggressive promotional drives and discounts with the big liquor retailers of ­Woolworths and Coles in the lead-up to Christmas.

It has turned into a disaster. The heat is also on Treasury in the form of a class action planned by lawyers Maurice Blackburn over last year's surprise profit downgrade in July. The latest downgrade raises more questions about management systems and disclosure. Maurice Blackburn's Ben Slade said the ­latest downgrade raised further questions about transparency in Treasury's ­operations.

Bank of America Merrill Lynch analyst David Errington warned in late October of the dangers of focusing too much on the United States, in a note that seems to have eerily come true. He said at the time that Treasury should sell its US wine business because it would be too much of a management distraction. He added it was a "material drag'' that needed to be sold off, with the risk being that a poor-performing business might negatively influence the better performing parts.

Rayner has been strident in his view that the US operations are an integral part of the Treasury business and won't be offloaded.

However, the new element that has since been injected is the different strategy implemented by those managing the Australian business when it comes to discounting and enforcing price rises at such a difficult time in pre-Christmas trading.

It has simply caused mayhem. The latest share price slump wiped $590 million from the market capitalisation. It also makes Treasury much more vulnerable to an offshore predator, particularly a cashed-up buyer from China or a private equity operator eyeing a quick turnaround, just as industry conditions appear to be improving and the Australian dollar falls, making exporting wine a more profitable proposition.A bad time for the industry

Industry conditions have been very difficult for Australian wine companies generally over the past few years.

Ferrier Hodgson partner John Hart, a wine industry expert, said it should be remembered that at its heart, wine is an agricultural industry with a long lead time. For each of Australia's 2500 wine companies, whether they be large or small, it is difficult to consistently produce solid profit increases.

"There are long cycles,'' Mr Hart said.

The three to four years' lead time for vineyard plantings, the capital investment required in plant and equipment that is only being utilised for part of the year and the prospect of being tripped up by changing consumer demand for different wine varieties and styles means it is a difficult industry.

He cited the example of a slow wane in demand for sauvignon blanc in white wines, which means producers heavily weighted towards that variety would come under more pressure.

"Sauvignon blanc now seems to be going out of fashion,'' he said.

Mr Hart said the biggest factor in the overall success of the wine industry was the Australian dollar. When the currency was trading around US50¢ more than a decade ago, Australian exports surged.

But when it reached parity with the US currency then rose to US$1.10 it was a substantial squeeze on margins. Combined with a grape oversupply (which has now largely diminished) there was an extended period of difficult times.

The Australian wine retailing market can be a fickle beast.

Long-time observers who throw their minds back to around 2002 will recall the flipside of being too aggressive with discounts and promotions and the problems that can bring, so perhaps Treasury management need a touch of sympathy.

Strangely enough, that involved some of the very brands in the spotlight now. In 2002, former Southcorp boss Keith Lambert went on a discounting rampage to try to expand market share for brands including Rosemount after Southcorp and the former privately-held Rosemount merged.

That too turned out to be a disaster, ­costing Lambert his job and ultimately paving the way for Southcorp to be taken over by Foster's in 2005 to form an even bigger wine business alongside the Foster's beer business.

For long-time Treasury shareholders who are glass half-full types, there is one interesting point to cling on to for a tiny sliver of happiness.

When Treasury split from former parent Foster's Group in May 2011 to become a stand-alone wine company that was largely debt-free, its share price on the first day of trading – May 9, 2011 –was $3.45.

Treasury shares finished on Thursday at $3.64 so those who were there at the start are still in front on paper. But this will be small consolation, because Treasury has been as high as $6.47 in mid-May 2013, before its troubles began.

Treasury hit by supermarket snub - Wine $40 million profit downgrade after Christmas sales slump

Newspaper January 31, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 657 Words
Page: 15
OpenURL Link
Treasury chairman Paul Rayner and his board are under immense pressure after a disastrous decision not to take part in the usual aggressive pre-Christmas promotional discounts with Coles and Woolworths' liquor stores triggered a sales slump and a $40 million profit downgrade.

The sharp about-turn in strategy for the crucial month of December was one of the main reasons behind a the profit downgrade revealed on Thursday by Treasury, which has lost the confidence of the market.

Industry players suggested the hardest hit in the sales and profit slump in Australia were in the commercial wine segment under $10 per bottle and included brands such as Rosemount, Lindemans, Wolf Blass, Jamiesons Run and Penfolds Koonunga Hill.

Treasury increased prices on under $10 bottled win and decided against taking part in traditional deep discounting and promotional activities with Woolworths and its liquor chains such as Dan Murphys and BWS, and Coles with its First Choice and Vintage Cellars brands, and paid the price by losing prime spots in in-store displays and in advertising catalogues.perplexed liquor retailing veterans

The decision to lift prices and wind back promotional discounts for such an important trading month as December has perplexed liquor retailing veterans.

Michael Andrewartha, who owns East End Cellars, says November and December are the strongest months of the year, and when brands need to have strong momentum. Treasury had taken a big punt that its brands would still be sought after without good promotional deals, but seriously miscalculated their clout.

"They certainly bet and lost,'' he said.

"There's a lot of strong brands out there that people shift to,''

A continued downturn in sales to China, where the government has implemented austerity measures to curb spending on higher-priced wines, also contributed to the Treasury downgrade. Treasury shares slumped 20 per cent after the company came out of a trading halt and revealed the extent of the profit slide, which continues a run of disasters since July 2013. The stock closed at $3.64, having opened on Thursday at $3.91. Treasury announced the $40 million profit downgrade for 2013-2014 because of a substantial drop in wine sales volumes over the Christmas period and now expects ­full-year profit to be between $190 million to $210 million, compared with the previous range of $230 million to $250 million.

It is also still experiencing tough times in the United States, as it continues to reduce its previously overflowing stock levels in that market by cutting back on total shipments. Treasury said it expected its first half earnings before interest, tax and the SGARA accounting standard would be between $42 million and $46 million, compared with $73.4 million a year earlier.

In September 2013 former Treasury chief executive David Dearie was dumped by Mr Rayner from his position in the wake of $160 million in embarrassing writedowns in the US announced in July, and a decision to destroy $34 million of bottled wine sitting in the warehouses of third-party distributors to ensure an oversupply of wine in the market didn't worsen.

Mr Rayner installed one of his fellow directors, Warwick Every-Burns, as interim chief executive while a global search for a new boss got under way.

Nielsen figures showed that Treasury's Australian business was performing strongly in the September quarter, with the Treasury brands increasing by almost 9 per cent by volume and 12 per cent by value. But this rapidly reversed. Industry players suggested Treasury's volumes may have dropped by as much as 14 per cent in December, although Treasury wasn't commenting on Thursday beyond its statement to the ASX.The steep profit downgrade added to Treasury's woes on another front where it is the subject of a proposed class action led by Maurice Blackburn Lawyers over last July's initial shock profit downgrade.

Maurice Blackburn managing principal Ben Slade said on Thursday that the fresh downgrade raised further questions of transparency about the company's operations.

It was looking closely at the implications of the new downgrade.

"If our investigations reveal that TWE has been less than frank with the market, our class action will be expanded to claim for the losses suffered today,'' he said.

Treasury Wine's China woes reveal misplaced optimism - Comment

Newspaper January 31, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Angus Grigg Section: Companies and Markets 514 Words
Page: 26
OpenURL Link
Shanghai The wreckage of Treasury Wine Estate's China strategy can be seen on websites across the country.

Discounting is everywhere.

The company's best-known brand, Penfolds, has been heavily marked down across China since November last year, as retailers attempt to clear surplus stock.

In its release to the Australian Securities Exchange on Thursday morning, Treasury blamed "austerity measures" imposed on government officials by President Xi Jinping.

Yet this is only half the story.

Communist Party cadres have certainly scaled back their banqueting, but Treasury also got greedy.

Those on the ground in China say the company, in its desperation to hit quarterly sales numbers, pushed a huge amount of product into China at the beginning of last year.

Even without the austerity campaign insiders say it would have been difficult to move all this stock.

Treasury's mistake was believing its stellar 2013 financial year, where volumes rose 39 per cent in Hong Kong and China, would continue this year. Such optimism came despite plenty of evidence that the good times were over.

President Xi outlined his austerity drive in December 2012 and issued the guidelines for the policy in January 2013.

By March, when he had officially taken over as President, the policy was in full swing and sales of high-end goods had began to suffer.

Yet Treasury ignored the signs and believed those saying the campaign would not last long.

The company has a very different view now, saying the problems in the first half will continue into the second.

One issue for investors is why it took Treasury so long to come clean about the problems in China.

In September last year those selling its products were talking openly about a large surplus of stock. By November the discounting could be seen on every Chinese website.

But the bigger issue for Treasury is the trashing of the Penfolds brand across China.

Prior to this wave of discounting, China offered some the highest per-bottle margins anywhere in the world.

Those margins will be very hard to claw back now that consumers are accustomed to big discounts.

Take Rawson's Retreat. It previously sold for 228 yuan ($A43), but has been marked down 74 per cent to 59 yuan on yhd.com, a Chinese website that sells everything from groceries to electronics.

The lower Australian dollar will help somewhat, but is not going to make up for the massive discounts being offered.

At the higher-end Penfolds' Bin 138 is marked down from 3510 yuan to 825 yuan for those who buy three bottles.

In the mid-range, Bin 128 is being discounted by 70 per cent.

These prices are only marginally above those seen in Australia, despite the extra freight costs and China imposing a 48 per cent tax on wine.

This suggests the days of fat margins in China are over.

Last year Treasury said its earnings before interest and tax (EBIT) margin were 40.3 per cent in Asia – it does not break out figures for China.

This compares with 9.5 per cent in the Americas and just 6.4 per cent in Europe, Africa and the Middle East.READ NEXT: Treasury Wine reveals $40m downgrade as sales slump Chanticleer | Succession plan needed as Treasury Wine hits new low

TWE stumble opens door for offshore buyer

Newspaper January 30, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Analysis Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 557 Words
Page: 18
OpenURL Link
The prospect of Australia's iconic wine brands Penfolds, Rosemount, Wolf Blass and Lindemans being owned by an offshore company increases with each misstep by Treasury Wine Estates.

The steady fall in the Australian dollar is also making the prospect of an offshore buyout of the company and its prized collection of brands more appetising. A lower dollar makes Australian assets cheaper, while exports into offshore markets from the Australian brands become much more competitive.

There will be considerable hand-wringing should that situation eventuate – particularly when it comes to Penfolds, with its 170-year heritage and flagship status in the $5 billion Australian wine industry.

Investors are bracing for a hefty profit downgrade on Thursday, when Treasury outlines the extent of the damage after chairman Paul Rayner and his stand-in chief executive Warwick Every-Burns finalise their review of preliminary financial results for the December half.

Treasury Wine Estates' previous full-year profit forecast of between $230 million and $250 million for 2013-14 is likely to be scaled back.

While lower numbers will hurt, the bigger problem is that investor confidence in the company will slide even further towards rock bottom.

Treasury has essentially been run by the board since late September 2013, when former chief executive David Dearie was dumped over the $160 million in write-downs announced in July and the need to destroy $34 million of excess bottled wine in the United States.

Treasury's director and former Clorox cleaning company executive Mr Every-Burns stepped out of the boardroom to assume CEO duties in the short term, and has been working closely with Mr Rayner since then.Exchange rate a 'double whammy'

There were high hopes for Treasury in May 2011, when it began life as a largely debt-free stand-alone entity after Foster's Group split its beer and wine assets into separate vehicles. Six months later the Foster's beer business was snapped up for $12 billion by global giant SAB Miller.

With confidence ebbing away in Treasury's board and management, offshore predators have two elements moving in their favour: a share price under pressure and a falling Australian dollar.

The sliding Aussie means they will have more financial clout in paying for an Australian asset, while wine exports from an Australian business will be more keenly priced on retailers' shelves overseas.

Stephen Harvey, chairman of Deloitte's Australian Wine Industry Group, said currency is a big factor. "It's a double whammy,'' he said.

"It is certainly more attractive for foreign investors to invest in the Australian industry."

Ironically, it was a desire by wine and tourism billionaire Bob Oatley to see Penfolds and Rosemount remaining in Australian hands that prompted the 2005 sale of his crucial 18 per cent stake in those brands' former corporate owner, Southcorp, to Foster's. This paved the way for a full takeover of Southcorp by Foster's at a time when global drinks groups were circling.

Penfolds is the most profitable brand in the stable and the main prize for any predator of Treasury.

The company went into a trading halt on Tuesday at $4.45, and was above $6 prior to July 2013 when the first round of troubles emerged.

It has solid wine and consumer goods experience on its board: Garry Hounsell, who is also a director of Qantas, owns wine group Toolangi Vineyards in the Yarra Valley; Peter Hearl is a former executive with the firm behind fast food brands KFC, Taco Bell and Pizza Hut; and Michael Cheek is a former executive with US spirits giant Brown-Forman.

Wine woes: bad December at Treasury

Newspaper January 29, 2014 | Australian Financial Review, The [3 Day Embargo] (Australia)
Author: Simon Evans Section: Companies and Markets 350 Words
Page: 13
OpenURL Link
Investors in Treasury Wine Estates are bracing for another profit downgrade from the ailing wine group because of soft profit figures for December.

Treasury was scheduled to announce its first-half results on Feb­ruary 20 but after top executives received the latest set of preliminary ­figures for December, the extent of the fall-off in the United States was enough to prompt an urgent request to the ASX for a trading halt, which has been granted until early Thursday.

The expected downgrade stems from a poor profit performance in December in the US market, and tougher times in Asia, where austerity measures imposed by the Chinese ­government have hit sales of higher-priced wines in China.

The downgrade will further damage investor confidence in Treasury, which has experienced a horrific run over the past seven months. In July 2013 it announced $160 million in write-downs related to excess wine that had built up in third-party distributors' warehouses in the US, and a shock ­decision to destroy $34 million worth of lower-end bottled wine to prevent it from heading onto retailers' shelves.

Treasury executives and the board are currently reviewing the latest set of internal figures for the company, which sells brands including Penfolds, Wynns, Rosemount and Lindeman's.

December is a crucial month for wine companies because of the large percentage of sales for ­Christmas ­parties, gift-giving and ­seasonal ­celebrations.

Part of Treasury's strategy for dealing with its excess supply in the US was to move more shipments by discounting, but this appears to have worsened the situation for all wine companies, and this was alluded to by rival ­Constellation Brands three weeks ago.

Citi food and beverages analyst Gino Rossi said on Tuesday that market conditions were poor in the US.

"It's not a good environment to be trying to clear excess stock," he said.

Treasury's interim chief executive Warwick Every-Burns warned at the annual meeting on October 23 that first-half profits were expected to be "lower than prior years" because of lower ­shipments to the US and increased brand investment in Asia, but said he remained committed to a previous forecast of full-year earnings for 2013-14 of between $230 million and $250 million.
